Musta'man's Property as Fay': The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Deposits and Mortgages
Expands ruling on musta'man's property as fay' and records intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the fay' status of a musta'man's property and distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding deposits and mortgages.

Core Idea
When a musta'man becomes Muslim and his former territory is conquered, his property becomes fay' — but deposits and mortgages in Muslim hands are disputed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on Kanz al-Daqa'iq's ruling: if a musta'man (an enemy with safe conduct) embraces Islam in Dar al-Islam, and the Muslims later conquer his original land, his family and property there become fay' (booty without battle, no khums). The passage addresses objections from a hadith on protection, and then reveals an intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Yusuf holds that deposits (wadi'ah) left by the musta'man become fay' for all Muslims, while Muhammad holds they belong to the capturing detachment. A similar dispute arises for mortgages. Understanding this ikhtilaf refines our grasp of the principles of ghalaba and property rights in siyar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Determine the basic ruling: when a musta'man embraces Islam in Dar al-Islam and the Muslims later conquer his original territory, his family and property left there become fay' (booty without battle, no khums).
Fay' differs from ghanimah; no khums, distributed like kharaj and jizyah.
2
Understand the exception for deposits and mortgages
The text extends the same ruling to deposits (wadi'ah) and mortgages (rahn) left with Muslims or dhimmis, but then introduces a disagreement among the Hanafi imams.
3
Locate the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Abu Yusuf holds that the deposit becomes fay' for all Muslims collectively. Muhammad holds it is fay' for the specific military detachment that captured the musta'man. For the mortgage, Abu Yusuf says it remains with the mortgagee for the debt; Muhammad says it is sold, the debt paid, and the excess becomes fay'.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; both imams are students of Abu Hanifa.
4
Examine the tarjih (preference)
The commentator in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq prefers Muhammad's view on the mortgage, arguing that the excess over the debt is like a deposit in legal custody. Al-Nahr leans toward Abu Yusuf, but the commentator refutes this.
The tarjih is based on the principle that property in legal custody (hukman) does not become fay' entirely.
5
Apply the principle of ghalaba
The ruling is based on overwhelming power (ghalaba) overriding the general protection from the hadith 'their blood and property are protected from me'. This principle allows the Muslims to take property left in enemy territory.
Ghalaba is a common basis for many legal rulings in siyar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإن جاءنا حربي بأمان وله زوجة ثم وولد ومال عند مسلم أو ذمي أو حربي فأسلم هنا ثم ظاهر عليهم فالكل فيء ) بيان لحكم ما تركه المستأمن في دار الحرب ثم صار من أهل دارنا إما بإسلامه أو بصيرورته ذمياً فتقييده بإسلامه في المختصر ليفهم منه حكم بالأولى. أما المرأة وأولاده الكبار فلأنهم حربيون كبار وليسوا بأتباع، وكذلك ما في بطنها لو كانت حاملاً لما قلنا إنه جزؤها، وأما أولاده الصغار فلأن الصغير إنما يتبع أباه في الإسلام عند اتحاد الدار ومع تباين الدارين لا يتحقق ولذا أطلق في الولد ليشمل الكبير والصغير والجنين. ولو سبى الصبي في هذه المسألة وصار في دار الإسلام فهو مسلم تبعاً لأبيه لأنهما اجتمعا في دار واحدة بخلاف ما قبل إخراجه وهو فيء على كل حال. وأما أمواله فإنها لا تصير محرزة بإحراز نفسه لاختلاف الدارين فبقي الكل غنيمة وعمم المودع لعدم الفرق. فإن قلت : قوله عليه السلام عصموا مني دماءهم وأموالهم يخالفه قلت: هذا باعتبار الغلبة المال الذي في يده وما هو في معناه بالعرف لأن من دأب الشرع بناء الحكم على الغلبة؛ ثم اعلم أن ماله وإن كان غنيمة لا خمس فيه وإنما يصرف كما يصرف الخراج والجزية لأنه مأخوذ بقوة المسلمين من غير قتال بخلاف الغنيمة لأنه مملوك بمباشرة الغانمين وبقوة المسلمين. وفي التتارخانية : وديعته فيء الجماعة المسلمين عند أبي يوسف. وقال محمد: تكون فيئاً للسرية التي أسرت الرجل ويعتق مدبره الذي دبره في دارنا وأم ولده بأسره. وفي المغرب: ظاهر عليه غلب وظهر على اللص غلب .اهـ. فينبغي ضبط المختصر بالبناء للمجهول كما لا يخفى، ولم أر حكم ما إذا كان على المستأمن دين المسلم أو ذمي أدانه له في دارنا ثم رجع، ولا يخفى باق لبقاء المطالبة وينبغي أن يوفى من ماله المتروك ولو صارت وديعته فيئاً اهـ. قوله: (وينبغي ترجيحه الخ) قال في النهر : أنت خبير بأن تقديم قول أبي يوسف يؤذن بترجيحه وهذا لأن الوديعة إنما كانت فيئاً لما مر من أنها في يده حكماً ولا كذلك الرهن اهـ. قال بعض الفضلاء أقول: لما كان الزائد على مقدار الدين في حكم الوديعة كان في يده حكماً فالحق ما في البحر، وأما حديث الترجيح بتقديم القول فليس بمطرد كما لا يخفى على من تتبع اهـ ونحوه في حواشي أبي السعود عن الحموي.
He said: (If a harbi comes to us with aman, and he has a wife, children, and property with a Muslim, a dhimmi, or a harbi, then he becomes Muslim here, then they overpower them, then all is fay'). Explanation of the ruling regarding what the musta'man left in Dar al-Harb, then he became from the people of our dar, either by his Islam or by his becoming a dhimmi. So restricting it to his Islam in al-Mukhtasar is to understand from it the ruling a fortiori. As for the woman and his adult children, because they are adult harbis and not followers, and likewise what is in her womb if she was pregnant because we said it is a part of her. As for his young children, because the young one follows his father in Islam when the dar is united, and with different dar it is not realized; hence he generalized 'child' to include the old, the young, and the fetus. If the boy is captured in this case and comes to Dar al-Islam, he is a Muslim following his father because they met in one dar, unlike before his removal, and he is fay' in any case. As for his property, it does not become protected by his own protection due to difference of dar, so all remains ghanimah, and he made the depositary general due to no difference. If you say: his statement (peace be upon him) 'their blood and their property are protected from me' contradicts it, I say: this is on account of the over-powering of the property that was in his hand and what is in its meaning by custom, because the custom of the Law is to base the ruling on over-powering. Then know that his property, even if it is ghanimah, there is no khums in it; rather it is disbursed as kharaj and jizyah are disbursed, because it is taken by the strength of the Muslims without fighting, unlike ghanimah because it is owned by the direct action of the spoilers and by the strength of the Muslims. In al-Tatarkhaniyyah: His deposit is fay' for the entire group of Muslims according to Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: It is fay' for the detachment that captured the man, and his mudabbar whom he gave tadbir in our dar is set free, and his umm walad is enslaved. In al-Mughrib: 'Overpowered' means conquered, and 'overpowered the thief' means conquered. So it is necessary to read al-Mukhtasar with the passive verb as is not hidden. I have not seen the ruling if the musta'man owes a debt to a Muslim or a dhimmi whom he lent to him in our dar, then he returns. It is not hidden that it remains due to the continuation of the claim, and it should be paid from his left property even if his deposit becomes fay'. He said: (It is appropriate to prefer it, etc.) He said in al-Nahr: You are experienced that giving precedence to the saying of Abu Yusuf indicates its preference, and this is because the deposit was only fay' due to what passed that it was in his hand in legal judgment, but not so the mortgage. Some virtuous people said: I say: Since the excess over the amount of the debt is in the ruling of the deposit, it is in his hand legally, so the truth is what is in al-Bahr. As for the discussion of preference by giving precedence to the saying, it is not consistent, as is not hidden from one who researches. And similar is in the Hawashi of Abu al-Sa'ud from al-Hamawi.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the property of a musta'man (an enemy combatant who enters Dar al-Islam with a safe conduct) who subsequently embraces Islam while in Dar al-Islam. The ruling states that if the Muslims later conquer the enemy territory where his family and property remain, all of that becomes fay' (booty taken without battle, subject to different rules than ghanimah). The commentator explains the reasoning and scope. He clarifies that the wife and adult children are not followers and remain harbis, so they become part of the fay'. The young child, however, follows the father in Islam only if they were in the same dar; since the father became Muslim in Dar al-Islam and the child was in Dar al-Harb, they are not in the same dar, so the child does not automatically become Muslim. But if the child is captured and brought to Dar al-Islam, he becomes Muslim by following his father because they are now in the same dar. The property left behind becomes ghanimah because the musta'man's own inviolability (isma) does not extend to his property across different dar. The commentator then addresses a potential objection based on the hadith 'their blood and property are protected from me' by stating that the ruling is based on the 'over-powering' (ghalaba) principle, which is a customary basis for many legal rulings. A key point is that this fay' is exempt from khums (one-fifth tax), unlike ghanimah taken by direct fighting; it is spent like kharaj and jizyah. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is recorded: Abu Yusuf holds that a deposit (wadi'ah) left by the musta'man with a Muslim or dhimmi in Dar al-Harb becomes fay' for the entire Muslim community, while Muhammad holds it is fay' for the specific military detachment that captured the person. The commentator then notes a preference for Muhammad's view regarding a mortgage (rahn): the mortgage is sold, the debt is paid, and the excess goes to the Muslims as fay'. The author of al-Nahr argues that giving precedence to Abu Yusuf's statement indicates its preference, but a later scholar refutes this, saying the correct view is that the excess is like a deposit and thus in the hand of the mortgagee legally, so it should not be fay'.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'man): A harbi (enemy combatant) who enters Dar al-Islam with a safe conduct (aman).
فيء (fay'): Booty taken from the enemy without fighting or with minimal force; it is not subject to khums and is distributed like kharaj.
وديعة (wadi'ah): A deposit left by the musta'man with a Muslim or dhimmi in Dar al-Harb.
رهن (rahn): Mortgage or collateral left by the musta'man for a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, what happens to a rahn (mortgage) left by a musta'man with a Muslim in Dar al-Harb when the Muslims conquer?
A) The mortgage remains with the mortgagee for his debt and is not fay'.
B) The mortgage is sold, the debt is paid, and the excess becomes fay' for the Muslims.
C) The mortgage becomes fay' entirely, and the debt is lost.
D) The mortgage is returned to the musta'man.

Key Takeaway
The property of a musta'man becomes fay' upon conquest, but deposits and mortgages in the hands of Muslims/dhimmis are subject to an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding their distribution.

Ruling on Musta'man's Property When He Becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb
Clarifies that a musta'man's young child follows him in Islam and his deposits are his own, while other property becomes fay'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the property and child of a musta'man who becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb, and distinguish between what remains his and what becomes fay'.

Core Idea
When a musta'man becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb, only property in a respected hand (Muslim/dhimmi deposit) remains his; all else becomes fay'.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a harbi enters with aman, then leaves, becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb, and returns. After the Muslims conquer that territory, what happens to his property and family? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the type of possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and legal question
A musta'man becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb, then Muslims conquer that dar. We need to determine the status of his young child, deposits, and other property.
2
Understand the child's status
The young child follows his father in Islam because both were in the same dar (Dar al-Harb) at the time of conversion. Thus the child is free and Muslim.
Adult children do not follow; they become fay'.
3
Understand the deposit's status
Property deposited with a Muslim or dhimmi is considered under a 'respected hand' (yad muhtaramah). Such possession is legitimate and thus the deposit remains the musta'man's property.
If deposited with a harbi, the deposit would be fay'.
4
Understand other property's status
All other property, including that held by harbis or even usurped goods in Muslim/dhimmi hands (not as deposit), becomes fay' because the hand is not representative of the musta'man.
Wife and adult children also become fay'.
5
Apply the key criterion
The central distinction is whether the property is in a 'respected hand' (Muslim/dhimmi) that can represent the musta'man. If yes, the property remains his; if no, it is fay'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( وإن أسلم ثمة فجاءنا فظهر عليهم فولده الصغير حر مسلم وما أودعه عند مسلم أو ذمي فهو له وغيره فيء) بيان لحكم متروك الحربي إذا أسلم في دار الحرب وجاء وترك أمواله وأولاده ثم ظهرنا على أهل الحرب. أما الولد الصغير فهو تبع لأبيه إلينا مسلماً حين أسلم إذا الدار واحدة فكان حراً مسلماً، وما كان من وديعة له عند مسلم أو ذمي فهو له لأنه في يد محترمة ويده كيده وما سوى ذلك فهو فيء، فأما المرأة وأولاده الكبار فلما قلنا، وأما المال الذي في يد الحربي فلأنه لم يصر معصوماً لأن يد الحربي ليست يداً محترمة، وشمل غيره العين المغصوبة في يد المسلم أو الذمي فيكون فيئاً لعدم النيابة؛ كذا في فتح القدير.
He said: (If he becomes Muslim there, then comes to us, then they overpower them, then his young child is free and Muslim, and what he deposited with a Muslim or a dhimmi is his, and other than that is fay'). Explanation of the ruling regarding what the harbi leaves behind if he becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb and comes, leaving his property and children, then we overpower the people of war. As for the young child, he follows his father to us as a Muslim when he became Muslim because the dar is one, so he was free and Muslim. What was a deposit for him with a Muslim or a dhimmi is his because it is in a respected hand, and his hand is like his hand. Other than that is fay'. As for the woman and his adult children, it is as we said. As for the wealth that is in the hand of the harbi, because it did not become inviolable since the hand of the harbi is not a respected hand. And it included other than that, such as a usurped object in the hand of a Muslim or a dhimmi, so it is fay' due to lack of representation. This is from Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on a different scenario: a harbi (enemy) enters with aman (musta'man), then leaves Dar al-Islam, goes to Dar al-Harb, becomes Muslim there, and then comes back to Dar al-Islam, leaving his property and family behind. Then the Muslims conquer that territory. The ruling clarifies that his young child (saghir) is free and Muslim because at the time the father became Muslim, both were in Dar al-Harb (the same dar), so the child follows him in Islam. His deposits (wadi'ah) left with a Muslim or dhimmi in Dar al-Harb remain his property, because those hands are respected (muhtaramah) and the deposit is considered legally in the musta'man's own hand. Everything else—including his wife, adult children, and any property in the hands of harbis—becomes fay' because harbi hands are not respected. Even a usurped object (maghsubah) in the hands of a Muslim or dhimmi is considered fay' because the Muslim/dhimmi is not acting as a representative of the musta'man. The source is Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
ثمة (thamma): There (referring to Dar al-Harb). Contrasts with 'huna' (here, Dar al-Islam).
يد محترمة (yad muhtaramah): A respected hand—referring to a Muslim or dhimmi holding property lawfully; such possession is considered legitimate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a musta'man becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb, what is the status of his young child upon the Muslims' conquest?
A) The child becomes a slave of the Muslims.
B) The child is free and Muslim.
C) The child remains a harbi.
D) The child's status depends on his choice.

Key Takeaway
A musta'man's property after conversion in Dar al-Harb is only protected if in a respected hand (Muslim/dhimmi deposit); the young child follows his father in Islam.

Reduction of Kharaj When Land Cannot Bear
Ruling with dalil from Umar's practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on reducing kharaj when the land cannot bear it and explain the prohibition of increase using the practice of Umar (RA).

Core Idea
Kharaj is a ceiling, not a floor: it reduces when the land cannot bear, but never increases even if it can.

This lesson examines a key principle of Hanafi land tax (kharaj) law: the assessment is fixed based on the land’s ability at the time of imposition. If the land’s productivity declines, the kharaj must be reduced accordingly. Conversely, even if productivity rises, the kharaj cannot be increased. The proof comes from the practice of Caliph Umar (RA), who refused to raise the kharaj despite being told the land could bear more. This establishes a protective ceiling for the taxpayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic rule
The text states: if the land cannot bear the assessed kharaj, it is reduced; increase is prohibited.
This is the legal principle (hukm) under discussion.
2
Examine the legal proof (dalil)
The incident of Umar (RA) with his agents: when they said the land could bear more, Umar did not increase.
The dalil is a qawl sahabi (saying of a Companion) considered zanni in transmission but qati in meaning.
3
Understand the rationale
Umar's refusal indicates that the original assessment is a maximum; increasing would harm the taxpayer unjustly.
The underlying principle is that kharaj is a contract (aqd) that cannot be unilaterally altered.
4
Note the scope of application
The rule applies to lands assessed by Umar or by any imam following his pattern; for new lands, the imam cannot exceed Umar's assessment.
Abu Hanifah emphasized that even for newly conquered lands, the assessment must not exceed Umar's precedent.
5
Recall the definition of inability
Inability is when the produce does not reach double the kharaj; then reduction is to half the produce.
This measure ensures the payer retains at least half the produce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإن لم تطق ما وظف نقص بخلاف الزيادة) أي وإن لم تطق الأرض ما جعل عليها من الخراج الموظف السابق نقص عنها ما لا تطيقه وجعل عليها ما تطيقه بخلاف الزيادة على ما وظفه عمر رضي الله عنه فإنها لا تجوز وإن طاقتها الأرض لقول عمر رضي الله عنه لعامليه لعلكما حملتما الأرض ما لا تطيق فقالا : بل حملناها ما تطيق ولو زدنا لأطاقت. وهو دال على ما ذكرناه من الأمرين أطلقه فشمل الأراضي التي صدر التوظيف فيها من عمر رضي الله عنه أو من إمام بمثل وظيفة عمر وهو مجمع عليه، وأما إذا أراد الإمام توظيف الخراج على أرض ابتداء وزاد على وظيفة عمر رضي الله عنه فإنه لا يجوز عند أبي حنيفة وهو الصحيح عمر رضي الله عنه لم يزد لما أخبراه بزيادة الطاقة؛ كذا في الكافي. ومعناه أن الأرض التي فتحت بعد عمر رضي الله عنه لو كانت تزرع الحنطة فأراد أن يضع عليها در همين الأول، ثم رجع أبو يوسف وقال : هو خمسة أرطال وثلث رطل وهو صاع أهل المدينة. قوله : (ولم يذكر المصنف خراج المقاسمة لظهوره) قال الرملي : هو كالموظف مصرفاً وكالعشر مأخذاً لا فرق فيه بين الرطاب والزرع والكرم والنخل المتصل وغيره فيقسم الجميع على حسب ما تطيق الأرض من النصف أو الثلث أو الربع أو الخمس. وقد تقرر أن خراج المقاسمة كالعشر لتعلقه بالخارج ولذا يتكرر بتكرر الخارج في السنة وإنما يفارقه في المصرف فكل شيء يؤخذ منه العشر أو نصفه يؤخذ منه خراج المقاسمة وتجري الأحكام التي قررت في العشر فيه وفاقاً وخلافاً. ثم بحث أنها لو لم تطق الخمس لقلة الريع وكثرة المؤن ينقص، وأنه لو وقع الرضى على دراهم معينة أو على عدد الأشجار ينبغي الجواز. ثم نقل عن الكافي ليس للإمام أن يحول الخراج الموظف إلى خراج المقاسمة اهـ. قال: وكذلك عكسه فيما يظهر من تعليله لأنه قال: لأن فيه نقض العهد وهو حرام فاغتنم هذا التحرير لأن وقفيزاً وهي تطيقه ليس له ذلك. ومعنى عدم الإطاقة أن الخارج منها لم يبلغ ضعف الخراج الموظف فينقص منه إلى نصف الخارج؛ كذا أفاده في الخلاصة وظاهر ما في الكتاب أن النقصان عند الإطاقة لا يجوز وليس كذلك فقد نقل في البناية عن الكاكي أنه إذا جاز النقصان عند قيام الطاقة فعند عدم الطاقة بالطريق الأولى. وهو مقيد
His statement: 'And if it cannot bear what was assessed, it is reduced, contrary to increase.' Meaning: If the land cannot bear the previously assessed kharaj placed on it, that which it cannot bear is reduced from it and that which it can bear is imposed on it, unlike increase upon what Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) assessed, for that is not permissible even if the land can bear it, because of Umar's statement to his agents: 'Perhaps you have burdened the land with what it cannot bear?' They said: 'Rather, we burdened it with what it can bear, and if we increased it, it would bear it.' This indicates what we mentioned of two matters. It is absolute and includes lands for which assessment originated from Umar or from an Imam with the like of Umar's assessment, and it is agreed upon. As for when the Imam wishes to assess kharaj on land initially and exceeds Umar's assessment, it is not permissible according to Abu Hanifah, and it is the correct position. Umar did not increase despite their informing him of increased capacity. Thus in al-Kafi. Its meaning: Land conquered after Umar, if it were wheat-growing and he wanted to place two dirhams and a qafiz on it, then Abu Yusuf retracted and said: 'It is five ratls and a third of a ratl, which is the sa' of the people of Medina.' His statement: 'And the author did not mention kharaj al-muqasamah because it is obvious.' Al-Ramli said: 'It is like fixed kharaj in expenditure and like 'ushr in collection; there is no difference between fresh produce, crops, grapevines, date palms, and other attached things. It is all divided according to what the land can bear: half, third, fourth, or fifth. It has been established that kharaj al-muqasamah is like 'ushr in its connection to the produce, so it repeats with repetition of produce in the year, differing only in expenditure. Thus, anything from which 'ushr or half is taken is subject to kharaj al-muqasamah, and the rulings established for 'ushr apply to it, by agreement and disagreement. Then he discussed that if it cannot bear a fifth due to low yield and high expenses, it is reduced, and if consent is given on specific dirhams or number of trees, permissibility is appropriate. Then he reported from al-Kafi that the Imam cannot convert fixed kharaj to proportional kharaj. He said: Likewise its opposite, as appears from his reasoning because he said: 'Because it breaches the contract, and that is forbidden.' So grasp this verification. Because a qafiz and it bearing it does not have that. The meaning of inability is that the produce does not reach double the assessed kharaj, so it is reduced to half the produce. Thus it is stated in al-Khulasah. It is apparent from the book that reduction when able is not permissible, but it is not so; indeed, it is reported in al-Binayah from al-Kaki that if reduction is permissible when capacity exists, then when capacity is absent, it is all the more so. It is restricted.'
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the principle that kharaj (land tax) is based on the land's ability to pay. If the land cannot bear the fixed assessment (al-wazifah), it is reduced to what it can bear, but it can never be increased even if the land could bear more. The proof is the incident of Caliph Umar (RA) with his agents: when they informed him that the land could bear more, he still did not increase, establishing that the initial assessment is a ceiling. This ruling is agreed upon for lands assessed by Umar or by an imam following his pattern. For new lands, the imam cannot exceed Umar's assessment according to Abu Hanifah, and this is the correct view. The commentator then discusses the author's omission of kharaj al-muqasamah (proportional kharaj), explaining that it is like fixed kharaj in expenditure (صرف) but like 'ushr (tithe) in collection (مأخذ). It is based on produce and varies with yield, typically between one-fifth and one-half. The imam cannot convert fixed to proportional kharaj because it would break the contract. Finally, the passage clarifies the definition of 'inability': when the produce is less than double the fixed kharaj, it is reduced to half the produce. Some sources argue reduction is only for inability, but al-Kaki shows it is also allowed when able, as a concession.

Key Terms
خراج (kharaj): Land tax imposed on cultivated land, either fixed (موظف) or proportional (مقاسمة).
الطاقة (al-taqah): The capacity of the land to produce enough to cover the kharaj without hardship; measured by whether the produce doubles the tax amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if an imam wants to increase the kharaj on land that has become more productive?
A) Permissible because the land can bear it
B) Permissible only with the consent of the landowner
C) Not permissible, following the practice of Umar (RA)
D) Permissible if the increase is less than half the produce

Key Takeaway
Kharaj is a contractual ceiling: it reduces when the land cannot bear, but never increases even if it can.

Kharaj al-Muqasamah: Nature and Rulings
Expansion on kharaj al-muqasamah and its rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe kharaj al-muqasamah, explain its similarity to 'ushr in collection and to fixed kharaj in expenditure, and state the prohibition of converting fixed kharaj to muqasamah.

Core Idea
Kharaj al-muqasamah is a proportional tax on produce, collected like 'ushr but spent like fixed kharaj.

This lesson examines kharaj al-muqasamah (proportional land tax), which the original author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq omitted as obvious. The commentator, al-Ramli, clarifies that it is taken as a share of the produce (half, third, fourth, or fifth) based on the land's capacity. In collection, it resembles 'ushr (tithe) since it is taken from the harvest each season. In expenditure, it is treated like fixed kharaj. The passage also rules that the imam cannot convert fixed kharaj into muqasamah because that would breach the original contract, and discusses reduction when the yield is low.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of kharaj al-muqasamah
It is a share of the produce (half, third, fourth, or fifth) determined by the land's capacity.
Unlike fixed kharaj, it varies with yield.
2
Compare with 'ushr and fixed kharaj
In collection, it is like 'ushr (taken from the harvest, repeating each year). In expenditure, it is like fixed kharaj (spent on public works, etc.).
This dual nature is unique to Hanafi fiqh.
3
Understand the reduction rule for muqasamah
If the land cannot bear even the lowest share (e.g., one-fifth) due to low yield or high expenses, the share is reduced further.
This aligns with the general principle of ability.
4
Note the prohibition of conversion
The imam cannot change fixed kharaj (wazifah) to muqasamah, because that would alter the contract ('aqd) and is therefore haram.
Conversion would break the original agreement with the landowner.
5
Recognize permissibility of mutual agreement
If both parties consent to a specific amount (e.g., a fixed number of dirhams or a count of trees) instead of a share, it is permissible.
This is an exception based on mutual consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

خراج المقاسمة (kharaj al-muqasamah): Proportional kharaj taken as a share of the produce (half, third, fourth, or fifth) based on land capacity.
خراج (kharaj): Land tax imposed on cultivated land, either fixed (موظف) or proportional (مقاسمة).

Suspension and Reduction of Kharaj Due to Crop Failure and Land Inability
Explains when kharaj is reduced or dropped due to land's inability or crop damage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for reducing or dropping kharaj when crop is destroyed, explain the distinction between heavenly and non-heavenly calamities, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the calculation of reduced kharaj.

Core Idea
Kharaj is a tax on potential growth; if a heavenly calamity destroys the crop, kharaj falls or reduces, but expenses must be deducted first.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq delves into a crucial area of Hanafi financial fiqh: the land tax (kharaj). It addresses when a farmer may be relieved of this tax due to crop failure or the land’s inability to produce. The text presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and later scholars (mashayikh) on how to calculate the reduced kharaj. Understanding these rules is vital for applying the Hanafi position on land taxation fairly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
First, identify the act: paying kharaj (land tax) on kharaj land. The ruling is that kharaj is obligatory when the land is productive, but it can be reduced or dropped under certain conditions.
Kharaj is a financial obligation, not a religious act, but its payment is wajib when due.
2
Understand the principle of nama' taqdiri
The key principle is that kharaj is based on estimated growth (nama' taqdiri) throughout the year. If a calamity destroys the crop, this potential growth is lost, and the obligation may be reduced or eliminated.
This is analogous to zakat, which requires growth over a full year.
3
Distinguish heavenly vs. non-heavenly calamities
The text divides calamities into heavenly (sama'iyya) that cannot be guarded against (e.g., flood, fire, severe cold) and non-heavenly that can be prevented (e.g., pests, animal attacks). Only heavenly calamities cause kharaj to fall.
Some early Hanafis disagreed, but the relied-upon view is the distinction.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on calculation
When crop is partially destroyed, Imam Muhammad holds a fixed proportion: if remaining produce equals at least double the kharaj amount, full kharaj is due; if less, half kharaj. The mashayikh instead deduct expenses first, then take kharaj from the surplus.
The mashayikh's view is considered more correct (al-sawab) and is the mufta bihi.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Try applying the mufta bihi view: If a farmer has 1000 dirhams of produce, spent 600 dirhams, and the assessed kharaj is 200 dirhams, deduct expenses first, leaving 400 dirhams. Since 400 is more than double 200, full kharaj (200) is due.
If expenses exceed produce, no kharaj is due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if a calamity destroys the crop, the estimated growth is lost in part of the year, and being productive throughout the whole year is a condition, as in zakat, or the ruling is based on reality at the time of harvest. If anything remains, the amount we mentioned is taken from it. He made the calamity absolute: a heavenly calamity that cannot be guarded against, like drowning, burning, and severe cold. As for a non-heavenly calamity that can be guarded against, like consumption by monkeys, predators, livestock, etc., kharaj does not fall. And some said it falls, and the first is more correct. From this it is known that if worms and mice eat the crop, kharaj does not fall. He qualified it by crop, which is a name for standing produce, because if it perishes after harvest, it does not fall, as Shaykh al-Islam indicated. He qualified it by kharaj because rent falls in the first two cases. As for the third, al-Walwalji mentioned in his Fatawa: If one rents land for planting for a year, then a calamity destroys his crop before the year passes, the rent that became due before the destruction does not fall, and what becomes due after the destruction falls, because rent is owed in exchange for benefit little by little. So what benefit was received, the rent is owed, and what was not received, the contract is rescinded regarding it. In some transmissions, nothing falls, and the reliance is on what we mentioned. The difference between this and kharaj is that it falls. End. Shams al-A'imma said: Among the commendable practices of the Persian kings was that when a calamity befell some of the subjects' crops, they would give from their treasury the amount spent on planting, and they said: The merchant is a partner in loss as he is a partner in profit. So if the imam does not give him anything, at least let him not be charged kharaj. End. It is singular. His statement: (Thus it is stated in al-Khulasa where he said: If the land cannot bear that the kharaj be five, because the produce does not reach ten, it is permissible to reduce until it becomes like half the produce. End. And in this there is no mention of the lands on which Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, assessed kharaj, then they deteriorated and became weak now, or others; thus in Fath al-Qadir. Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, dealing with the suspension and reduction of kharaj (land tax). It opens with a ruling on land that cannot bear the assessed kharaj due to low produce; if the produce is less than double the kharaj, it is reduced to half the produce. The author clarifies that this reduction applies when the land is incapable, and by stronger reason if it had capacity. The main text then addresses cases where water overwhelms the land or the crop is destroyed by a calamity (afah). The key principle is that kharaj is due for potential growth (nama' taqdiri), and if that potential is lost, kharaj may fall. An intra-Hanafi dispute follows: Imam Muhammad rules that if some produce remains, kharaj is due proportionally, while the mashayikh (later scholars) prefer a method deducting expenses first. The passage distinguishes between heavenly calamities (sama'iyya) that are unavoidable—such as flood, fire, extreme cold—and non-heavenly ones like animal attacks; only the former exempt from kharaj. A note from Shaykh al-Islam states that destruction before harvest exempts, and from this the case of worms and mice is deemed not exempt since they can be guarded against. A comparison with rent (ujra) is made: rent does not fall for the period before the calamity because it is compensation for benefit already received, whereas kharaj falls entirely because it is a tax on potential growth. The passage concludes with a historical note from Persian kings who compensated farmers from the treasury, showing the ethical basis of not charging kharaj when no benefit is realized. Marginal notes further refine the rulings on reduction and the status of pests.

Key Terms
خراج (kharaj): A land tax imposed on agricultural land, assessed based on the land's productive capacity.
آفة (afah): A calamity that destroys the crop, divided into heavenly (sama'iyya) and non-heavenly.
نماء تقديري (nama' taqdiri): Estimated or potential growth of the land over the year, which is the basis for kharaj.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of calamity causes kharaj to fall?
A) Any calamity that destroys the crop
B) Heavenly calamities that cannot be guarded against, such as flood and fire
C) Non-heavenly calamities like pests
D) Calamities that occur after harvest

Key Takeaway
Kharaj falls only due to unavoidable heavenly calamities, and when calculating reduced kharaj, expenses must be deducted from the remaining produce before assessing the tax.

12 min
The Core Prohibition and Its Proof
Explains the prohibition and its proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on building new non-Muslim places of worship in Dar al-Islam and explain the evidence from the hadith.

Core Idea
Building new churches in Muslim lands is haram, proven by the Prophet's hadith 'No church in Islam.'

This lesson examines the foundational ruling that non-Muslim places of worship must not be newly constructed in territories under Islamic rule. We begin with the matn's phrase, the proof from the Sunnah, and the consensus on cities founded by Muslims or conquered by force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling from the matn
The matn states that a church (bi'a) or synagogue (kanisa) shall not be built in 'our abode' (Dar al-Islam). This explicitly prohibits new construction.
The prohibition is on new buildings, not on existing ones.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author cites the hadith 'لا إخصاء في الإسلام ولا كنيسة' (No castration in Islam, nor church). The negation implies prohibition of building churches.
The hadith is ahad (single-narrator) but accepted by scholars.
3
Understand the terminology
The passage clarifies that 'kanisa' is used for Jews and Christians, 'bi'a' originally general but later for Christians, and 'dayr' for Christian monasteries. In Egypt, only 'kanisa' is used.
Terminology varies by region, but the ruling applies to all non-Muslim places of worship.
4
Note the consensus for certain city types
For cities founded by Muslims (e.g., Kufa, Basra) and those conquered by force, there is consensus that building new churches is absolutely prohibited.
This consensus is a strong pillar of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا) تحدث بيعة ولا كنيسة في (دارنا أي لا يجوز إحداثهما في دار الإسلام لقوله عليه السلام لا إخصاء في الإسلام ولا كنيسة والمراد إحداثهما . وفي البناية : يقال كنيسة اليهود والنصارى لمتعبدهم وكذلك البيعة كان مطلقاً في الأصل ثم غلب استعمال الكنيسة لمتعبد اليهود والبيعة لمتعبد النصارى. وفي فتح القدير : وفي ديار مصر لا يستعمل لفظ البيعة بل الكنيسة لمتعبد الفريقين ولفظ الدير للنصارى خاصة. والبيع بكسر الباء . دار الإسلام فشمل الأمصار والقرى وهو المختار كما في فتح القدير . وقيده في الهداية بالأمصار دون القرى لأن الأمصار التي تقام فيها الشعائر فلا يعارض بإظهار ما يخالفها. وقيل في ديارنا يمنعون من ذلك في القرى أيضاً لأن فيها بعض الشعائر، والمروي عن صاحب المذهب في قرى الكوفة لأن أكثر أهلها أهل الذمة وفي أرض العرب يمنعون في أمصارها وقراها لقوله عليه السلام لا يجتمع دينان في جزيرة العرب اهـ . وشمل كلامه المواضع كلها وفي البناية : قيل أمصار المسلمين ثلاثة: أحدها ما مصره المسلمون منها كالكوفة والبصرة وبغداد وواسط فلا يجوز فيها إحداث بيعة ولا كنيسة ولا مجتمع لصلاتهم ولا صومعة بإجماع العلماء، ولا يمكنون فيه من شرب الخمر واتخاذ الخنزير وضرب الناقوس. وثانيها ما فتحه المسلمون عنوة فلا يجوز إحداث شيء فيها بالإجماع. وثالثها ما فتح صلحاً فإن صالحهم على أن الأرض لهم ولنا الخراج جاز إحداثهم، وإن صالحهم على أن الدار لنا ويؤدون الجزية، فالحكم في الكنائس على ما يوقع عليه الصلح، فإن صالحهم على شرط تمكين الإحداث لا نمنعهم والأولى أن لا يصالحهم عليه، وإن وقع الصلح مطلقاً لا يجوز الإحداث ولا يتعرض للقديمة .اهـ. والحاصل أنهم يمنعون من الإطلاق مطلقاً إلا إذا وقع الصلح على الإحداث أو على أن الأرض لهم على هذا القول ولا استثناء في ظاهر الرواية.
His statement: 'and a church or synagogue shall not be built in our abode' i.e., it is not permissible to build them in the land of Islam because of his statement, peace be upon him: 'No castration in Islam, nor church.' The intent is building them. In al-Binaya: It is said that kenisa is for the place of worship of Jews and Christians, and likewise bi'a was general originally, then usage prevailed that kenisa is for the place of worship of Jews and bi'a for Christians. In Fath al-Qadir: In the lands of Egypt, the term bi'a is not used; rather kenisa is used for both groups, and the term dayr is specifically for Christians. And 'biya' with a kasrah on the ba. Dar al-Islam includes cities and villages, and that is the preferred view as in Fath al-Qadir. And it was restricted in al-Hidayah to cities alone, not villages, because cities are where the rituals of Islam are established, so they should not be opposed by displaying things contrary to them. It is said in our lands they are prevented from that in villages as well because some rituals exist. The narration from the master of the school regarding the villages of Kufa is because most of their inhabitants are dhimmis. In the land of the Arabs, they are prevented in both cities and villages due to his statement: 'Two religions shall not coexist in the Arabian Peninsula.' So his statement includes all places. In al-Binayah: It is said that the cities of the Muslims are three types: first, what the Muslims founded like Kufa, Basra, Baghdad, and Wasit – it is not permissible to build a church or synagogue or gathering for their prayer or a cell by consensus of scholars, nor can they be permitted to drink wine, keep pigs, or strike the bell. Second, what the Muslims conquered by force – it is not permissible to build anything there by consensus. Third, what was conquered by treaty – if they made peace on the condition that the land is theirs and we have the kharaj, they may build; if they made peace on the condition that the abode is ours and they pay jizya, then the ruling regarding churches is based on what the treaty stipulates. If they made peace with the condition of allowing building, we do not prevent them, but it is better not to make peace with that condition. If the peace was absolute, it is not permissible to build, and the old [churches] are not interfered with. The summary is that they are prevented from building absolutely, except if the treaty included building or that the land is theirs according to this view, and there is no exception in the apparent narration.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil). It explains the commentary on the matn phrase 'and no church or synagogue shall be built in our abode.' The ruling is that new places of worship for dhimmis cannot be built in Dar al-Islam. The proof is the hadith 'No castration in Islam, nor church' – meaning new building. The commentator from al-Binayah clarifies terminology: 'kenisa' for Jews and Christians, 'bi'a' originally general but later for Christians. In Egypt, only 'kenisa' is used. The term 'dayr' is for Christian monasteries. The ruling applies to both cities and villages according to the preferred view (in Fath al-Qadir), but in al-Hidayah it was restricted to cities because villages lack the full establishment of Islamic rituals. However, some say villages also have some rituals. The land of the Arabs (Arabian Peninsula) is special due to the hadith that two religions shall not coexist there. Then the passage details three types of Muslim cities regarding building permissions: (1) cities founded by Muslims: consensus prohibits building new churches; (2) cities conquered by force: also consensus prohibits; (3) cities conquered by treaty: depends on terms. If the treaty specifies that building is allowed, they may build (but it is discouraged to grant such terms). If the treaty is silent, building is prohibited. The old existing churches are not interfered with.

Key Terms
بيعة (bi'a): Church or place of worship for Christians; in some regions used for both Christians and Jews.
كنيسة (kanisa): Place of worship: for Jews in most usage, but in Egypt used for both Christians and Jews.
دار الإسلام (dar al-islam): Territory under Islamic rule where Islamic law prevails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence (dalil) cited for the prohibition of building new churches in Dar al-Islam?
A) Quranic verse
B) Hadith 'لا إخصاء في الإسلام ولا كنيسة'
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) Qiyas on pig farming

Key Takeaway
Building new non-Muslim places of worship in Dar al-Islam is haram, proven by the hadith, with consensus on cities founded by Muslims or conquered by force.

10 min
Scope and Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Cities vs. Villages
Discusses scope: cities vs. villages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the prohibition applies to villages and identify the preferred view (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
The preferred view prohibits building churches in all Dar al-Islam, but a minority restricts it to cities.

While the general prohibition is clear, Hanafi scholars disagreed on whether it applies to villages. This lesson examines the reasoning behind each view, the authority of Fath al-Qadir over al-Hidayah, and the exception for the Arabian Peninsula.

Guided Path
1
State the disagreement
Al-Hidayah restricts the prohibition to cities (amsar) only, reasoning that Islamic rituals are fully established there, so any opposing display is not permitted. Villages lack such full establishment.
The view of al-Hidayah is based on preserving the dominance of Islamic symbols.
2
Present the opposing view
Fath al-Qadir and the preferred view extend the prohibition to villages because some Islamic rituals are also present there, and dhimmis are not to be allowed to manifest their religion even in villages.
This is supported by the narration from Imam Abu Hanifa regarding the villages of Kufa.
3
Identify the tarjih authority
Fath al-Qadir explicitly states 'wa huwa al-mukhtar' (and that is the chosen view), giving it precedence over the restriction in al-Hidayah.
Later Hanafi jurists generally prefer the inclusive view.
4
Note the special case of Arabian Peninsula
Regardless of the dispute, the Arabian Peninsula is treated separately based on the hadith 'Two religions shall not coexist in the Arabian Peninsula,' so prohibition applies absolutely in both cities and villages there.
This is an exception agreed upon by all.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi (operative ruling for fatwa) is the general prohibition in all of Dar al-Islam, including villages, unless a treaty stipulates otherwise.
This is the view adopted by later authorities like Ibn Abidin.

Three Types of Muslim Cities and the Treaty Exception
Classifies cities and explains treaty effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify Muslim cities into three categories regarding the prohibition and state the ruling for each, including the treaty exception.

Core Idea
Cities founded by Muslims or conquered by force have a complete ban; treaty cities depend on terms.

The passage classifies Muslim territories into three types regarding building churches: those founded by Muslims, those conquered by force, and those conquered by treaty. This lesson examines each category's ruling, the role of treaty conditions, and the absolute prohibition in the Arabian Peninsula.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The passage lists: (1) cities founded by Muslims (e.g., Kufa, Basra), (2) cities conquered by force, and (3) cities conquered by treaty.
The first two have consensus of prohibition; the third depends on treaty terms.
2
State the ruling for categories 1 and 2
For cities founded by Muslims and those conquered by force, building new churches is absolutely prohibited by consensus. Also, other manifestations like wine, pigs, and bells are not allowed.
This consensus is across all schools.
3
Analyze category 3 (treaty cities) in detail
If the treaty stipulates that the land remains with the dhimmis and they pay kharaj (land tax), they may build churches. If the treaty gives the land to Muslims and dhimmis pay jizya, then the ruling depends on the treaty terms: if it explicitly allows building, they may (though it is discouraged); if it is silent, building is prohibited.
The phrase 'الأولى أن لا يصالحهم عليه' indicates it is better not to grant such permission.
4
Note the exception for old churches
New construction is prohibited, but old churches that existed before the Muslim conquest are left untouched.
This is a general principle: 'ولا يتعرض للقديمة'.
5
Summarize the absolute prohibition
The passage concludes that they are prevented from building absolutely, except if the treaty specifically allows it or if the land is theirs according to the treaty. There is no exception in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah).
This reinforces the default prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Third, what was conquered by treaty – if they made peace on the condition that the land is theirs and we have the kharaj, they may build; if they made peace on the condition that the abode is ours and they pay jizya, then the ruling regarding churches is based on what the treaty stipulates. If they made peace with the condition of allowing building, we do not prevent them, but it is better not to make peace with that condition. If the peace was absolute, it is not permissible to build, and the old [churches] are not interfered with. The summary is that they are prevented from building absolutely, except if the treaty included building or that the land is theirs according to this view, and there is no exception in the apparent narration.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil). It explains the commentary on the matn phrase 'and no church or synagogue shall be built in our abode.' The ruling is that new places of worship for dhimmis cannot be built in Dar al-Islam. The proof is the hadith 'No castration in Islam, nor church' – meaning new building. The commentator from al-Binayah clarifies terminology: 'kenisa' for Jews and Christians, 'bi'a' originally general but later for Christians. In Egypt, only 'kenisa' is used. The term 'dayr' is for Christian monasteries. The ruling applies to both cities and villages according to the preferred view (in Fath al-Qadir), but in al-Hidayah it was restricted to cities because villages lack the full establishment of Islamic rituals. However, some say villages also have some rituals. The land of the Arabs (Arabian Peninsula) is special due to the hadith that two religions shall not coexist there. Then the passage details three types of Muslim cities regarding building permissions: (1) cities founded by Muslims: consensus prohibits building new churches; (2) cities conquered by force: also consensus prohibits; (3) cities conquered by treaty: depends on terms. If the treaty specifies that building is allowed, they may build (but it is discouraged to grant such terms). If the treaty is silent, building is prohibited. The old existing churches are not interfered with.

Key Terms
بيعة (bi'a): Church or place of worship for Christians; in some regions used for both Christians and Jews.
كنيسة (kanisa): Place of worship: for Jews in most usage, but in Egypt used for both Christians and Jews.
دار الإسلام (dar al-islam): Territory under Islamic rule where Islamic law prevails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on building a new church in a city conquered by force?
A) Permissible with the ruler's permission.
B) Prohibited by consensus.
C) Permissible only if the treaty says so (but it was conquered by force, so no treaty).
D) Prohibited only in the Arabian Peninsula.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition is absolute in cities founded by Muslims or conquered by force; in treaty cities, the terms of the treaty determine the ruling.

Distinguishing Marks for Dhimmis: Obligation and Rationale
Rules for distinguishing dhimmis from Muslims in appearance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules of ghiyar (distinguishing marks) for dhimmis, including the specific requirements for dress and riding, and explain the underlying rationale of humility (saghar).

Core Idea
Dhimmis must be distinguished in dress and riding to uphold humility and prevent confusion with Muslims.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi position on distinguishing dhimmis from Muslims in external appearance. The ruling is based on the need to maintain Islamic honor and protect weak Muslims. We will examine the specific requirements: the kustij (distinctive thread), riding restrictions, and the general principle that the mark must be humiliating, not honoring. The text also discusses an exception for the Jews of Medina and the shift in later scholarly opinion on riding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Obligation and Its Purpose
The passage states 'if distinction is obligatory' (إذا وجب التمييز). Identify why distinction is necessary: to avoid treating dhimmis as Muslims and to display humility (saghar) upon them.
The purpose is not punishment but social distinction to protect Islamic identity.
2
List the Specific Requirements
The text lists: dhimmis must wear the kustij (a thick thread), cannot ride horses, cannot carry weapons, and ride only a packsaddle (ikaf) on donkeys. Note the details: the kustij is made of material like wool and tied above the clothes.
These are not exhaustive; custom may define additional marks.
3
Trace the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Riding
Early scholars (المتقدمون) allowed riding donkeys with a packsaddle. Later scholars (المتأخرون) prohibited all riding except for necessity. Note the cited authority: Fath al-Qadir.
The mufta bihi position is the later, stricter view.
4
Analyze the Exception: Jews of Medina
The Prophet (ﷺ) did not require distinguishing marks for the Jews of Medina because they were well-known by face and their dress was not superior to Muslims. This shows the ruling depends on the risk of confusion.
It is a case of 'illa (cause) being absent, not an abrogation.
5
Apply the Principle of Custom (Urf)
The text concludes that the specific mark is not fixed; it depends on local custom. In the author's region, the mark was on the turban. This shows flexibility while maintaining the underlying obligation.
The mark must still be humiliating, not honoring.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ويميز الذمي عنا في الزي والمركب والسرج فلا يركب خيلا ولا يعمل بالسلاح ويظهر الكستيج ويركب سرجاً كالأكف) إظهاراً للصغار عليهم وصيانة لضعفة المسلمين ولأن المسلم يكرم والذمي يهان فلا يبتدأ بالسلام ويضيق عليه في الطريق، فلو لم تكن علامة مميزة فلعله يعامل معاملة المسلمين وذلك لا يجوز بخلاف يهود المدينة لم يأمرهم عليه الصلاة والسلام بذلك لأنهم كانوا معروفين بأعيانهم الجميع أهل المدينة ولم يكن لهم زي عال عن المسلمين، وإذا وجب التمييز وجب بما فيه صغار لا إعزاز لأن إذلالهم لازم بغير أذى من ضرب أو صفع بلا سبب يكون منه بل المراد اتصافه بهيئة وضيعة. والزي بالكسر اللباس والهيئة وأصله زوى؛ كذا في الصحاح وفي الديوان الزي الزينة والكستيج. عن أبي يوسف: خيط غليظ بقدر الأصبع يشده الذمي فوق ثيابه دون ما يتزينون به من الزنانير من الأبريسم؛ كذا في المغرب. وقيده في المجمع بالصوف وقيد بالخيل لأن لهم أن يركبوا الحمر عند المتقدمين على سروج كهيئة الأكف وهو جمع أكاف وهو معروف. والسرج الذي على هيئته هو ما يجعل على مقدمه شبه الرمانة والوكاف لغة ومنه أو كف الحمار؛ كذا في المغرب. والأكاف البرذعة؛ ذكره العيني. واختار المتأخرون أن لا يركبوا أصلاً إلا إذا خرجوا إلى قرية ونحوها أو كان مريضاً. وحاصله أنه لا يركب إلا لضرورة فيركب ثم ينزل في مجامع المسلمين إذا ؛ كذا مر بهم ؛ في فتح القدير وفيه : وإذا عرف أن المقصود العلامة فلا يتعين ما ذكر بل يعتبر في كل بلدة ما يتعارفه أهله وفي بلادنا جعلت العلامة في المتخذة العمامة
His statement: 'And the dhimmi is distinguished from us in dress, riding, and saddle: he does not ride horses, does not carry weapons, shows the kustij, and rides a saddle like a pack-saddle' – to display humility upon them and to protect weak Muslims, and because the Muslim is honored and the dhimmi is humiliated. So he is not initiated with greeting and is made to walk in narrow paths. If there were no distinguishing mark, he might be treated as a Muslim, which is not allowed. Unlike the Jews of Medina, the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not order that because they were known individually, all being from Madina, and they did not have a dress superior to Muslims. When distinction is obligatory, it must be with something humiliating, not honoring, because degrading them is necessary without harm like beating or slapping without cause from them; rather, he should be characterized with a lowly appearance. Az-ziyy (with kasr) means dress and appearance; its root is zawa; as in al-Sihah. In al-Diwan, az-ziyy is adornment. Al-kustij: from Abu Yusuf: a thick thread the size of a finger that the dhimmi ties above his clothes, unlike the silk girdles they adorn themselves with; as in al-Maghrib. In al-Majma' it was restricted to wool. [The restriction to horses] is because according to the early scholars, they may ride donkeys with saddles like pack-saddles (akf), which is the plural of ikaaf, which is known. The saddle of that shape is that which has a pomegranate-like shape on its front; al-wikaaf is the language for the donkey's pack-saddle; as in al-Maghrib. Al-ikaaf is the bardha'ah (packsaddle); mentioned by al-Ayni. The later scholars chose that they do not ride at all unless they go to a village or similar, or if they are sick. The summary: they do not ride except out of necessity; then they ride and then dismount in gatherings of Muslims. As mentioned in Fath al-Qadir. And in it: If it is known that the goal is the distinguishing mark, then what is mentioned is not specific; rather, in each town, the custom of its people is considered. In our country, the mark has been made in the turban that is taken.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage explains the rules of ghiyar (distinguishing marks) for dhimmis (non-Muslim subjects) as derived from the Hanafi school. The commentator expands on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq: 'ويميز الذمي عنا في الزي والمركب والسرج' (the dhimmi is distinguished from us in dress, riding, and saddle). 

Key points: 
1. The purpose is to show humility (صغار) and to protect weak Muslims from being misled. 
2. Specific prohibitions: dhimmis may not ride horses, carry weapons, or wear fine saddles. They must wear a distinctive thick thread (al-kustij) over their clothes. According to Abu Yusuf, the kustij is a finger-width thread, not a silk belt. Some restrict it to wool. 
3. Regarding riding: early scholars allowed them to ride donkeys with a packsaddle (اكاف), not a proper saddle. Later scholars (المتأخرون) said they should not ride at all except out of necessity (e.g., travel to a village or illness), and then they must dismount when passing through Muslim gatherings. 
4. The general principle: the mark is for identification, so it can vary by locality according to custom. The text notes that in the author's region, the distinguishing mark is in the manner of wearing the turban. 
5. Exceptions: The Jews of Medina were not required to have a distinctive dress because they were well-known by face. 
6. The mark must be humiliating (صغار), not honoring (إعزاز). 
7. The proof is rational (maslaha) and based on the general principle of maintaining the status difference between Muslims and dhimmis. 

This ruling is part of the broader category of siyar (relations with non-Muslims) and aims to prevent assimilation and maintain Islamic honor.

Key Terms
الكستيج (al-kustīj): A thick thread, about a finger's width, tied above the dhimmi's clothes, made of wool according to some scholars, to distinguish them from Muslims. Not a decorative silk belt.
الأكاف (al-ikāf): A packsaddle for a donkey or mule; the type of saddle permitted for dhimmis according to early scholars. It is humble compared to a proper saddle.
الزي (az-ziyy): Dress, appearance, or overall attire. In this context, it refers to the distinctive clothing or markers that set dhimmis apart from Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi scholars (المتأخرون), what is the ruling regarding dhimmis riding horses or donkeys?
A) They may ride horses freely but not donkeys.
B) They may ride donkeys with a packsaddle, but not horses.
C) They should not ride at all unless out of necessity, and then must dismount when passing Muslims.
D) They may ride any mount as long as they wear the kustij.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires dhimmis to have distinguishing marks in dress and riding to uphold humility and prevent confusion with Muslims, with flexibility based on custom and the presence of the underlying cause.

Ruling on Old Churches in Conquered Cities
Ruling on preservation or demolition of old churches based on mode of conquest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conquest by force ('anwah) and by treaty (sulhan), and apply the specific Hanafi ruling on preservation and use of old churches based on the mode of conquest.

Core Idea
Old churches are never demolished, but their use as places of worship depends on conquest mode: force = dwellings; treaty = worship permitted inside.

This lesson examines a key fiqh ruling regarding the treatment of non-Muslim places of worship in cities conquered by Muslims. The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between conquest by force ('anwah) and conquest by treaty (sulhan), leading to different legal outcomes for old churches. Understanding this distinction is critical for proper application of Islamic law to plural societies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
What is the overarching principle regarding old churches? The passage states clearly: 'All of them should not be demolished.'
2
Distinguish the two modes of conquest
Understand 'anwah (by force) and sulhan (by treaty). How does each affect the legal status of the church?
The mode determines whether the church remains a place of worship or becomes a dwelling.
3
Analyze conditions and restrictions for each case
For force-conquered: church becomes a dwelling (مساكن), dhimmis are prevented from gathering for worship. For treaty-conquered: church remains a place of worship, but public displays are forbidden (only allowed secretly).
Compare with al-Karkhi's statement on festivals.
4
Verify authoritative source
The relied-upon ruling is authenticated in al-Tatarkhaniyya: old churches are not demolished.
Note the authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلها ينبغي أن لا تهدم لأنها إن كانت في الأمصار قديمة فلا شك أن الصحابة أو التابعين أجمعين حين فتحوا المدينة علموا بها وبقوها وبعد ذلك ينظر؛ فإن كانت البلدة فتحت عنوة حكمنا بأنهم بقوها مساكن لا معابد فلا تهدم ولكن يمنعون من الاجتماع فيها للتقرب، وإن عرف أنها فتحت صلحاً حكمنا بأنهم أقروها معابد فلا يمنعون من ذلك فيها بل من الإظهار وانظر إلى قول الكرخي إذا حضر لهم عيد يخرجون فيه صلبانهم وغير ذلك فليصنعوا في كنائسهم القديمة من ذلك ما أحبوا، فأما أن يخرجوا ذلك من الكنائس حتى يظهر في المصر فليس لهم ذلك ولكن ليخرجوا خفية من كنائسهم اهـ . وصحح في التتارخانية رواية كتاب الإجارة من عدم هدم القديمة.
All of them should not be demolished because if they are old in the cities, there is no doubt that the Companions or the Successors, when they conquered the city, knew of them and left them. After that, consideration is made: if the town was conquered by force, we rule that they left them as dwellings, not places of worship, so they are not demolished, but they are prevented from gathering in them for worship. And if it is known that it was conquered by treaty, we rule that they confirmed them as places of worship, so they are not prevented from that therein, but only from public display. And consider the saying of al-Karkhi: 'If they have a festival in which they display their crosses and so forth, let them do that in their old churches as they wish. As for bringing that out of the churches until it appears in the city, they do not have that, but let them bring it out secretly from their churches.' End. And it is authenticated in al-Tatarkhaniyya, in the chapter on Ijarah, that old [churches] are not demolished.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the treatment of old churches in cities conquered by Muslims. It distinguishes two cases: (1) if the city was conquered by force (عنوة), the old churches become property of the Muslims and are considered dwellings (مساكن) rather than places of worship. They are not to be demolished, but the non-Muslims are prohibited from gathering there for religious practices. (2) If the city was conquered by peace treaty (صلحاً), the churches remain as places of worship, and non-Muslims may perform their rituals inside them, but they are prohibited from public displays such as processing with crosses. The commentator supports this with a statement from Imam al-Karkhi, who permits indoor activities but forbids public displays outside the church. The passage concludes by noting that the ruling is authenticated in the Tatarkhaniyya, indicating the relied-upon position is not to demolish old churches.

Key Terms
عنوة ('anwah): By force of conquest; taken by military victory without a peace treaty.
صلحاً (sulhan): By peace treaty; the city surrendered through negotiation, preserving certain rights for inhabitants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on old churches in a city conquered by force (عنوة)?
A) They are demolished immediately.
B) They remain as places of worship for dhimmis.
C) They remain as dwellings, and dhimmis cannot gather for worship in them.
D) They become mosques.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on old churches balances preservation with restriction: they are never demolished, but their use as places of worship depends on whether the city was conquered by force or by treaty.
