في ردة يحكم بها به وما يشك أنه ردة لا يحكم بها إذ الإسلام الثابت لا يزول بشك مع أن الإسلام يعلو، وينبغي للعالم إذا رفع إليه هذا أن لا يبادر بتكفير أهل الإسلام مع أنه يقضي بصحة إسلام المكره . أقول : قدمت هذه لتصير ميزاناً فيما نقلته في هذا الفصل من المسائل فإنه قد ذكر في بعضها أنه كفر مع أنه لا يكفر على قياس هذه المقدمة فليتأمل اهـ. وفي الفتاوى الصغرى : الكفر شيء عظيم فلا أجعل المؤمن كافراً متى وجدت رواية أنه لا يكفر اهـ. وقال قبله وفي الجامع الأصغر، إذا أطلق الرجل كلمة الكفر عمداً لكنه لم يعتقد الكفر قال بعض أصحابنا : لا يكفر لأن الكفر يتعلق بالضمير ولم يعقد الضمير على الكفر. وقال بعضهم : يكفر وهو الصحيح عندي لأنه استخف بدينه اهـ. وفي الخلاصة وغيرها: إذا كان المسألة وجوه توجب التكفير ووجه واحد يمنع التكفير فعلى المفتي أن يميل إلى الوجه الذي يمنع التكفير تحسيناً للظن بالمسلم زاد في البزازية إلا إذا صرح بإرادة موجب الكفر فلا ينفعه التأويل حينئذ . وفي التتارخانية : لا يكفر بالمحتمل لأن الكفر نهاية في العقوبة فيستدعي نهاية في الجناية ومع الاحتمال لا نهاية .اهـ. والحاصل أن من تكلم بكلمة الكفر هازلاً أو لاعباً كفر عند الكل ولا اعتبار باعتقاده كما صرح به قاضيخان في فتاواه، ومن تكلم بها مخطئاً أو مكرهاً لا يكفر عند الكل، ومن تكلم بها عالماً عامداً كفر عند الكل، ومن تكلم بها اختياراً جاهلاً بأنها كفر ففيه اختلاف، والذي تحرر أنه لا يفتى بتكفير مسلم أمكن حمل كلامه على محمل حسن أو كان في كفره اختلاف ولو رواية ضعيفة، فعلى هذا فأكثر ألفاظ التكفير المذكورة لا يفتى بالتكفير بها ولقد ألزمت نفسي أن لا أفتي بشيء منها. وأما مسألة تكفير أهل البدع المذكورة في الفتاوى فقد تركتها عمداً لأن محلها أصول الدين وقد أوضحها المحقق في المسايرة. قوله : (يعرض الإسلام على (المرتد أي يعرضه الإمام والقاضي وهو مروي عن عمر الله عنه لأن رجاء العود إلى الإسلام ثابت لاحتمال أن الردة كانت باعتراض شبهة لم يبين صفته، وظاهر المذهب استحبابه فقط ولا يجب لأن الدعوة قد بلغته وعرض الإسلام هو الدعوة إليه ودعوة من بلغته الدعوى غير واجبة ولم يذكر تكرار العرض عليه. وفي الخانية : يعرض عليه الإسلام في كل يوم من أيام التأجيل . قوله : ( وتكشف شبهته) بيان لفائدة العرض أي فإن كان له شبهة أبداها كشفت عنه لأنه عساه اعترضت له شبهة فتزاح عنه . قوله : ( ويحبس ثلاثة أيام فإن أسلم وإلا قتل لأنها مدة ضربت لإبداء الأعذار وهو مروي عن عمر رضي الله عنه أطلقه فأفاد أنه يمهل وإن لم يطلبه وهو رواية، وظاهر الرواية أنه لا يمهل بدون استمهال بل يقتل من ساعته كما في الجامع الصغير إلا إذا كان الإمام يرجو
رضي
قوله : (لم يبين صفته أي صفة العرض. وذكر في النهر أن قوله يعرض» ظاهر في وجوبه
Concerning apostasy – what is ruled as apostasy is judged accordingly, but what is doubted to be apostasy is not judged as such, because established Islam is not removed by doubt, while Islam is exalted. It is fitting for a scholar, when this is brought to him, not to hasten to declare Muslims as disbelievers, even though he rules on the validity of the Islam of one who is coerced. I [Ibn Nujaym] say: I have presented this as a criterion for what I have transmitted in this chapter of issues; for in some of them it is mentioned that it is kufr, though according to the standard of this introduction it is not kufr, so let one ponder. End. In al-Fatawa al-Sughra: 'Kufr is a grave matter, so I do not make a believer a disbeliever as long as I find a narration that he does not disbelieve.' End. And before it, in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If a man deliberately utters a word of kufr but does not believe in it, some of our companions said: He does not disbelieve, because kufr pertains to the heart and he did not intend kufr in his heart. Some said: He disbelieves, and that is the correct view in my opinion, because he has shown contempt for his religion. End. In al-Khulasa and others: If in an issue there are aspects that necessitate takfir and one aspect that prevents takfir, the mufti must incline towards the aspect that prevents takfir, to think well of the Muslim. Al-Bazzaziyya added: Unless he explicitly states his intention to do what necessitates kufr, then interpretation does not benefit him then. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: One does not disbelieve by a [statement that is] probabilistic, because kufr is extreme in punishment, so it requires extreme in offense, and with probability there is no extremity. End. The summary is: Whoever utters a word of kufr jokingly or playfully disbelieves according to all, and his belief is not considered, as Qadikhan stated in his Fatawa. Whoever utters it mistakenly or under compulsion does not disbelieve according to all. Whoever utters it knowingly and deliberately disbelieves according to all. Whoever utters it voluntarily but ignorant that it is kufr – there is disagreement. What has been settled is that one does not give a fatwa of takfir for a Muslim if it is possible to interpret his words in a good way, or if there is a difference of opinion regarding his kufr, even if the narration is weak. On this basis, most of the aforementioned utterances of takfir are not subject to a fatwa of takfir, and I have bound myself not to give a fatwa of takfir regarding any of them. As for the issue of takfir of the people of innovation mentioned in the fatwas, I have intentionally left it because its place is in the principles of religion, and the investigator has clarified it in al-Musayara. His statement: 'Islam is presented to the apostate' – meaning the imam or judge presents it, and it is narrated from 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) because hope of return to Islam is established, due to the possibility that the apostasy was due to an obscure doubt that he did not clarify. The apparent school is that it is only recommended (mustahabb), not obligatory, because the call has already reached him, and presenting Islam is inviting him, and inviting one who has already received the call is not obligatory. And he did not mention repeating the presentation. In al-Khaniyya: It is presented to him every day of the deferment period. His statement: 'And his doubt is removed' – an explanation of the benefit of the presentation: for if he has a doubt, he expresses it, and it is removed from him, because perhaps a doubt occurred to him and it is removed. His statement: 'And he is imprisoned for three days; if he repents, [fine], otherwise he is killed' – because this is a period set for presenting excuses, and it is narrated from 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him). He gave it absolutely, which indicates that he is given respite even if he does not request it – that is one narration. The apparent narration is that he is not given respite without asking for it, but is killed immediately, as in al-Jami' al-Saghir, unless the imam hopes... His statement: 'He did not clarify its description' – meaning the description of the presentation. It is mentioned in al-Nahr that his saying 'presents' apparently indicates obligation.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents key principles for dealing with apostasy in Hanafi fiqh. The author, Ibn Nujaym, first establishes a criterion: established Islam is not removed by doubt. Therefore, when there is any uncertainty about whether an utterance constitutes apostasy, the ruling should lean towards preserving the Muslim's status. He quotes several sources: al-Fatawa al-Sughra advises not to declare a Muslim a disbeliever as long as any narration exists that he does not disbelieve; al-Jami' al-Saghir presents a difference of opinion on whether uttering kufr words without belief in them constitutes apostasy – the preferred view is that it does, due to showing contempt for religion. Then a crucial principle: if there are multiple aspects that point to kufr and one aspect that prevents it, the mufti must incline towards the aspect that prevents kufr, to think well of the Muslim. The only exception is when the person explicitly states his intention to do what necessitates kufr. Then the passage categorizes those who utter kufr: (1) Jokingly or playfully: apostate according to all, regardless of inner belief; (2) Mistakenly or under compulsion: not apostate; (3) Knowingly and deliberately: apostate; (4) Voluntarily but ignorant that it is kufr: there is disagreement. The settled position is that no fatwa of takfir should be issued if the words can be given a good interpretation, or if there is any difference of opinion, even based on a weak narration. Ibn Nujaym then states his personal practice of not issuing fatwas of takfir on such matters. He also mentions that he avoided the issue of takfir of the people of innovation (ahl al-bida') because it belongs to the principles of religion (usul al-din). The second part of the passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding the procedure for an apostate: Islam should be presented to him (mustahabb, not wajib), his doubts should be clarified, and he is imprisoned for three days. If he repents, he is spared; otherwise, he is killed. There is a difference of opinion on whether the three-day deferment is given automatically or only upon request. The author notes that the apparent school is that it is recommended, not obligatory, to present Islam.

Key Terms
الردة (al-riddah): Apostasy from Islam, either by words, actions, or beliefs that clearly reject the fundamentals of the faith.
التأجيل (al-ta'jil): The three-day grace period given to the apostate to repent before execution.
الاستخفاف بالدين (al-istikhfaf bi al-din): Showing contempt or mockery of religion, which is grounds for apostasy even without internal belief in the words uttered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a mufti do if an issue has multiple aspects that point to takfir but one aspect that prevents it?
A) He must rule with takfir because the majority of aspects support it.
B) He should refer the case to a higher authority.
C) He must incline towards the aspect that prevents takfir, to think well of the Muslim.
D) He should ask the person to clarify his intention.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, takfir is a grave matter and must be avoided whenever a good interpretation or any difference of opinion exists; the apostate is given every opportunity to repent, including a three-day grace period.

Nullification of Good Deeds by Apostasy and Their Return
Ruling on apostasy's effect on good deeds and their potential return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether good deeds and their reward return after apostasy and repentance, and explain the difference between the deed and the reward.

Core Idea
Apostasy nullifies the reward of good deeds, but the deeds themselves can generate new reward upon repentance.

Apostasy (riddah) in Islamic law nullifies all previous good deeds. But what happens when a person repents and returns to Islam? Are the deeds restored? This lesson examines a nuanced debate within the Hanafi school and beyond, distinguishing between the act of obedience and the reward it earned. The later Hanafi position offers a balanced view: though the original reward is lost, the deeds themselves become effective again for new reward after repentance.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi rule on apostasy and good deeds
Identify the baseline ruling: apostasy (riddah) nullifies all previous good deeds (habt al-'amal). This is a settled principle.
Habt means the deeds lose their reward and are rendered void.
2
Identify the dispute over the return of deeds after repentance
The text reports that Abu Ali and Abu Hashim (Mu'tazila) hold that the deeds return fully, while some ascribe to Abu Hanifa the view that they do not return at all. This is the ikhtilaf.
The dispute is both intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab.
3
Explain the middle position selected by later Hanafi scholars
The author clarifies that the reward (thawab) that was nullified does not return, but the acts of obedience (ta'at) become effective again for new reward after repentance. This is the mufta bihi view.
This middle path avoids both extremes: total loss or total restoration.
4
Analyze the tree analogy from al-Ka'bi
The analogy of a tree whose branches and fruit are burned but whose roots remain: when the fire (apostasy) is extinguished (repentance), the tree can bear fruit again. This illustrates that the original fruit (reward) is gone, but the tree (deeds) can produce new fruit.
The analogy comes from Mu'tazili al-Ka'bi but is adapted to Hanafi thought.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical result: a repentant apostate is not required to repeat acts of worship performed before apostasy, and he may receive new reward for them. The original reward is not reinstated.
This affects how we view the state of a person's good deeds after returning to Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتارخانية معزياً إلى اليتيمة قيل له : لو تاب أتعود حسناته؟ قال : هذه المسألة مختلفة فعند أبي علي وأبي هاشم وأصحابنا أنها تعود، وعند أبي أنها لا تعود ونحن نقول إنه لا يعود ما بطل من ثوابه لكنه تعود طاعاته المتقدمة مؤثرة في الثواب بعد .اهـ. وفيها معزياً إلى السراجية من ارتد ثم أسلم ثم ارتد ومات فإنه ... قوله : (لكنه تعود طاعته المتقدمة مؤثرة في الثواب بعد أي بعد توبته ولعل المراد بعودها مؤثرة في الثواب أنه سبحانه يثيبه عليها ثواباً جديداً غير الثواب الذي حبط أو أن المراد بالثواب عدم مطالبته بإعادتها وإن بطلت بالردة فإن الاعتداد بها وعدم مطالبته بإعادتها فضل من الله تعالى تأمل. ثم رأيت في شرح المقاصد للسعد التفتازاني في بحث التوبة . ثم اختلفت المعتزلة في أنه إذا سقط استحقاق العقاب للمعصية بالتوبة هل يعود استحقاق ثواب الطاعة الذي أبطلته تلك المعصية؟ فقال أبو علي وأبو هاشم: لا لأن الطاعة تنعدم في الحال وإنما يبقى استحقاق الثواب وقد سقط والساقط لا يعود. وقال الكعبي : نعم لأن الكبيرة لا تزيل الطاعة وإنما تمنع حكمها وهو المدح والتعظيم فلا تزيل ثمرتها فإذا صارت بالثوبة كأن لم تكن ظهرت ثمرة الطاعة كنور الشمس إذا زال الغيم. وقال بعضهم : وهو اختيار المتأخرين لا يعود ثواب السابق لكن تعود طاعته السالفة مؤثرة في استحقاق ثمراته وهو المدح والثواب في المستقبل بمنزلة شجرة احترقت بالنار أغصانها وثمارها ثم انطفأت النار فإنه تعود أصل الشجرة وعروقها إلى خضرتها وثمرتها .اهـ. وهذا يفيد ما قلنا، ويفيد أن الخلاف بين الكعبي وغيره على عكس ما ذكره المؤلف وأن الخلاف المذكور عند المعتزلة في بطلان ثواب الطاعة بالمعاصي الكبائر لأنها عندهم تخرج صاحبها من الإيمان بمنزلة الردة لكن لا تدخله في الكفر نعم إذا مات مصراً عليها كان مخلداً في النار كالكفار.
And in al-Tatarkhaniyyah, quoting from al-Yatimah: It was said to him: If he repents, do his good deeds return? He said: This matter is disputed. According to Abu Ali, Abu Hashim, and our scholars, they return. According to Aba [Hanifa], they do not return. But we say: The reward that was nullified does not return, but his previous acts of obedience return as effective for reward after [repentance]. Also, quoting from al-Sirajiyyah: Whoever apostatizes, then becomes Muslim, then apostatizes and dies... He said: (but his previous act of obedience returns as effective for reward after) i.e., after his repentance. Perhaps the meaning of its return as effective for reward is that Allah the Exalted will reward him with a new reward, different from the reward that was nullified; or that what is meant by 'reward' is not requiring him to repeat them, even though they were nullified by apostasy, because accounting for them and not requiring repetition is a favor from Allah. Then I saw in Sharh al-Maqasid of Sa'd al-Taftazani, in the chapter on repentance: The Mu'tazila differed: When the entitlement to punishment for sin is dropped by repentance, does entitlement to reward for obedience that was nullified by that sin return? Abu Ali and Abu Hashim said: No, because the obedience is annulled immediately, and only entitlement to reward remains, and it has dropped, and what drops does not return. Al-Ka'bi said: Yes, because the major sin does not remove the obedience; it only prevents its effect, which is praise and exaltation. So it does not remove its fruit. When [the sin] becomes, through repentance, as if it had not been, the fruit of obedience appears, like the light of the sun when the cloud disappears. And some said—and this is the choice of the later scholars—the reward of the past does not return, but his previous act of obedience returns as effective in deserving its fruits, which are praise and reward in the future, like a tree whose branches and fruits were burned by fire, then the fire is extinguished: the tree's root and trunk return to their greenness and fruit. This supports what we said, and it shows that the disagreement between al-Ka'bi and others is opposite to what the author mentioned, and that the mentioned disagreement among the Mu'tazila concerns nullification of reward for obedience by major sins, because in their view major sins take the person out of faith like apostasy, but do not enter him into disbelief; if he dies persisting, he will be eternally in Hell like disbelievers.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage delves into a sophisticated theological-legal debate about the effect of apostasy on prior good deeds. The Hanafi school generally holds that apostasy nullifies all good deeds (habt al-a'mal). However, if the apostate returns to Islam, the question is whether those deeds are reinstated. The text reports a difference: Abu Ali and Abu Hashim (Mu'tazila) hold that the deeds return, while some Hanafis (attributed to Abu Hanifa) say they do not. The author then clarifies a middle view: the reward (thawab) that was lost does not return, but the deeds themselves become effective again for future reward, akin to a tree that regrows after a fire. The author also refers to a discussion in Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid on Mu'tazili views. The practical upshot is that a repentant apostate is not required to repeat acts of worship like prayer and fasting that he performed before apostasy, but he may be rewarded anew. This nuanced position guards against a simplistic understanding that all is lost or all is restored.

Key Terms
حبط العمل (habt al-‘amal): Nullification of good deeds due to apostasy; the deeds lose their reward and are rendered void.
ثواب (thawab): Divine reward for obedience; in this context, the specific reward that was earned before apostasy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi scholars (al-muta'akhkhirin), what happens to the good deeds of a person who apostatizes and then repents?
A) All deeds and their reward are completely restored.
B) The deeds remain nullified and cannot ever be effective.
C) The deeds become effective for new reward, but the original reward is not restored.
D) The deeds are restored only if the person did not commit major sins.

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi position distinguishes between the act of obedience (which can become effective again) and its reward (which is lost and cannot return), using the tree analogy to illustrate this balance.

Apostasy Does Not Expiate Past Sins
Ruling on whether apostasy expiates past sins, with proof from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether apostasy expiates past sins, cite the hadith proof, and explain the difference between the 'ammah and muhaqqiqun views.

Core Idea
Apostasy never erases sins; only sincere repentance upon returning to Islam does.

A common misconception is that apostasy 'wipes the slate clean', making a returning apostate sinless. But the Hanafi school, drawing on the hadith 'al-Islam yajibbu ma qablahu', holds that it is Islam itself – understood as repentance – that expiates past sins, not the act of apostasy. This lesson unpacks the proof and the intra-school difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act in question is apostasy (riddah). The ruling being clarified is whether apostasy expiates sins committed before or during it. The author's conclusion: apostasy does NOT expiate any sins.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith 'al-Islam yajibbu ma qablahu' (Islam erases what came before it). The author relies on this as a sunnah ahad.
The hadith is cited in Ahmad 4/199, 204, 205.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The verb 'yajibbu' means 'to obliterate' or 'to erase'. The hadith indicates that entering Islam (after disbelief or sin) wipes away previous sins. It does NOT say that apostasy itself erases anything.
Hence, apostasy cannot be the cause of expiation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There are two layers: (a) the 'ammah (common Hanafis) vs. muhaqqiqun (verifying scholars) on whether Islam automatically expiates sins or requires explicit repentance. (b) An intra-Hanafi exception from Abu Hanifa about obligations like consecutive fasting.
The author sides with the 'ammah view, interpreting Islam itself as repentance.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The author (Ibn Nujaym) considers the 'ammah view the most sound because the hadith's apparent meaning supports it. The exception from Abu Hanifa is restricted to specific obligations and does not generalise.
Thus the mufta bihi is: sins are expiated by returning to Islam with repentance, not by apostasy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And from Abu Hanifa: If a vow of consecutive fasting was obligatory on him, then he apostatizes, then repents, the [necessity to] make up [the fast] drops, as in al-Tatimma. Al-Tamartashi mentioned that according to the common people, sins committed during apostasy and before it are expiated, but according to many verifying scholars they are not expiated. And I say: What appears to me and must be adopted is that sins committed before apostasy are not expiated by apostasy at all; rather they are expiated after his Islam, just as what was committed during apostasy is expiated, because Islam erases what came before it, as in the hadith. Its explanation is that through his Islam and his disavowal of what he was upon, he becomes repentant of what occurred before that Islam. Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the effects of apostasy, focusing on past sins (ma'asi). The author clarifies a key point: apostasy does not erase sins; rather, sins are only expiated through repentance and entry into Islam. The hadith 'al-Islam yajibbu ma qablahu' (Islam erases what came before) is cited as evidence. However, there is a nuance: some early Hanafis (like Abu Hanifa in a specific case) held that certain obligations (like a missed fast) may drop upon apostasy and reversion, but this is not general. The author aligns with the 'common people' ('ammah) view that sins are erased by Islam itself (since Islam is a form of repentance). However, the verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) hold that sins are not automatically expiated; there must be sincere repentance. The author concludes that Islam, by its nature, is repentance, so the hadith supports the 'ammah view. Thus the apostate's sins are not forgiven by the apostasy itself, but by his subsequent reversion to Islam.

Key Terms
المعصية (al-ma‘siyah): Sin, an act of disobedience to Allah; in this context, sins committed before or during apostasy.
الإسلام يجب ما قبله (al-Islam yajibbu ma qablahu): The hadith indicating that embracing Islam erases all previous sins and obligations. Used by Hanafis to argue that sins are expiated by Islam, not by apostasy.
الردة (al-riddah): Apostasy; leaving the fold of Islam.

Apostasy does not expiate past sins; only sincere repentance upon returning to Islam does, based on the hadith 'al-Islam yajibbu ma qablahu'.

Do Sins Persist After Apostasy? The Hanafi View
Critical refinement on sin persistence after apostasy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether sins are forgiven by apostasy or by returning to Islam, and explain the evidence from the hadith.

Core Idea
Sins are not erased by apostasy; they are wiped out by returning to Islam as per the hadith.

A critical question in Hanafi fiqh and theology is whether apostasy itself erases previous sins or whether forgiveness only comes through returning to Islam. This passage presents an intra-madhhab debate, with the author preferring the view that sins persist during apostasy and are dropped only upon re-embracing Islam. Understanding this ruling clarifies the legal status of an apostate who returns and the role of repentance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Determine the question at hand: do sins (ma'asi) committed before apostasy remain or are they forgiven?
The dispute is about the effect of apostasy on past sins.
2
Recognize the two Hanafi positions
Some Hanafi scholars (al-'amma) hold that apostasy drops all sins; others (al-muhaqqiqun) say sins are dropped only by Islam.
The author sides with the latter, citing the hadith.
3
Examine the author's proof
The hadith 'al-Islam yajubbu ma qablahu' (Islam wipes out what preceded it) is used to argue that forgiveness is tied to Islam, not apostasy.
Apostasy is itself a sin, so it cannot remove other sins.
4
Apply the practical consequence
An apostate who returns to Islam is not liable for past sins (including hudud) because his Islam wipes them out, not his apostasy.
If sins were dropped by apostasy, returning to Islam would be unnecessary for forgiveness.

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) discussing a key dispute: whether sins (ma'asi) are forgiven by apostasy itself or by subsequent Islam. The author argues that sins are not removed by apostasy; rather, they are dropped when the person returns to Islam, because 'Islam wipes out what preceded it.' The text references different opinions: some hold that apostasy itself wipes out sins, while others (the chosen view) maintain that only Islam does. The commentary also discusses whether good deeds nullified by apostasy can return, analogizing to a tree that regrows after fire. The practical effect: if an apostate returns to Islam, he is not punished for sins committed before or during apostasy, but this is due to his Islam, not his apostasy.

معصية (ma'siyah): Sin; an act of disobedience to Allah.
ثبوت (thubut): Persistence; here, the persistence of sin despite apostasy.
الإسلام يجب ما قبله (al-Islam yajubbu ma qablahu): Hadith meaning 'Islam wipes out (previous sins)'; used as proof that sins are forgiven upon re-entering Islam.

The Apostate's Valid Return to Islam: Requirement of Disavowal
Detailed conditions for an apostate's valid repentance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the required formula for an apostate's valid Islam and explain why the shahada alone is insufficient.

Core Idea
An apostate's Islam requires explicit disavowal of his former religion; the shahada alone does not remove his kufr.

This lesson examines what an apostate must say to validly return to Islam according to the Hanafi school. The source passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq makes a crucial distinction: while becoming Muslim from original disbelief may only require the shahada, an apostate (one who left Islam) must go further and explicitly disavow the false religion he adopted. Understanding this requirement reveals the Hanafi method of treating the apostate's state as qualitatively different from that of an original non-Muslim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific requirement for an apostate's Islam
The passage opens by stating that the apostate's Islam is by disavowing all religions or that which he turned to. Pinpoint that the key element is tabarru' (disavowal).
The phrase 'أو عما انتقل إليه' gives an alternative: disavow the specific religion he converted to.
2
Analyze the formula given by Abu Yusuf
In Sharh al-Tahawi, Abu Yusuf provides the precise wording: shahada + affirmation of what came from Allah + explicit disavowal including 'I never entered this religion at all and I am free of it.' Note that the word 'qattu' means 'ever' and covers the past, emphasizing total disassociation.
This formula is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
3
Understand why the shahada alone is insufficient for an apostate
The passage explains that if an apostate recites the shahada in a customary manner, it does not benefit him unless he recants what he said. The reason is that his kufr is not removed by them alone—he has already believed in Islam in the past, so his shahada is habitual. Disavowal is necessary to break from his false belief.
This is supported by al-Bazzazi and Jami' al-Fuslayn.
4
Contrast with the conversion of an original non-Muslim
The passage details that for an original kafir (non-Muslim), the requirements vary: a dualist becomes Muslim by one shahada, a monotheist denier of prophethood must also affirm Muhammad's risala. But for the apostate, disavowal is always required.
This distinction is key to avoid conflating the two cases.
5
Note additional points: Islam by actions and special cases
Islam can also be established by actions like praying in congregation or giving adhan. Cases from nawazil show that even if a person says 'La ilaha illallah' and disavows Christianity, he is not Muslim unless he also states he enters Islam. Similarly, affirming Muhammad as a prophet but limiting his mission to Arabs is insufficient without disavowing one's original faith.
These illustrate the strictness of the requirement for apostates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وإسلامه أن يتبرأ عن الأديان كلها أو عما انتقل إليه أي إسلام المرتد بذلك ومراده أن يتبرأ عن الأديان كلها سوى دين الإسلام وتركه لظهوره ولم يذكر الشهادتين . وصرح في العناية بأن التبرأ بعد الإتيان وشأنها خلق الشر؛ كذا قاله بعض
Book of Siyar / Chapter of rulings of apostates. And his Islam is that he disavows all religions or that which he turned to. That is, the Islam of the apostate is by that, and his intent is to disavow all religions except the religion of Islam and leave it because of its manifestness, and he did not mention the two testimonies. And it is stated in al-‘Inaya that the disavowal is after uttering the two testimonies. And in Sharh al-Tahawi, Abu Yusuf was asked: How does he become Muslim? He said: He says 'I bear witness that there is no god but Allah and that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah', and he affirms what came from Allah, and disavows that which he adopted, and says 'I never entered this religion at all and I am free of it.' His word 'qattu' means forever, because 'qattu' is an adverb for the past, not the future. So in Fath al-Qadir. Affirmation of resurrection is recommended. His word 'that which he adopted' means he claimed for himself, like Jews and Christians. So in al-Zahiriyya. The condition of disavowal means that if he recites the two testimonies in a customary manner, it does not benefit him unless he recants what he said, because his kufr is not removed by them. So in al-Bazzazi and Jami' al-Fuslayn. And it is restricted to the Islam of the apostate because in the Islam of other disbelievers there is detail. If the disbeliever denies the Creator Almighty, like idol worshippers, or affirms the Creator but associates others with Him, like the Dualists, then he becomes Muslim by one of the two testimonies. Likewise if he says 'I am upon the religion of Islam' or 'upon al-Hanifiyya'. But if he is a monotheist who denies the message, he does not become Muslim by the word of tawhid until he says 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah'. In Majmu' al-Nawazil: If he says 'May Allah bless Muhammad' he is not a Muslim; if he says 'I have embraced Islam' it is Islam. In al-Rawda: If the disbeliever says 'I believe in what the messengers believed in' he becomes a Muslim. In Majmu' al-Nawazil: If the disbeliever says 'Allah is one' he does not become a Muslim. And if a Muslim says 'Your religion is true' he becomes a Muslim; it is said he becomes a Muslim unless he says 'true but I do not believe in it'. And if he says 'I am free of Judaism' and does not say 'I have entered the religion of Islam' he is not a Muslim. In al-Tajrid: If a Jew or Christian says 'There is no god but Allah and I disavow Christianity', it is not Islam; if he adds 'and I enter the religion of Islam or the religion of Muhammad صلى الله عليه وسلم' he is a Muslim. All from al-Khulasa. In al-Muhit: Whoever among Jews and Christians affirms the prophethood of Muhammad صلى الله عليه وسلم but claims he was sent to Arabs not to Israelites, as in Iraq, he is not a Muslim by his affirmation that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah until he disavows that religion or affirms that he has entered the religion of Islam. Then know that Islam can also be by action, like praying in congregation, or affirming it, or giving adhan in some mosques. His statement: 'Because in the Islam of other non-Muslims there is detail' – the author already mentioned the categories of non-Muslims and what makes a non-Muslim a Muslim, whether by word or deed, in the beginning of the book of Jihad. His statement: 'such as the Dualists' – they are the Magians who believe in two gods: Nur (light) called Yazdan whose affair is creating good, and the darkness called Ahriman whose affair is creating evil.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq focuses on the specific wording an apostate must use to validly return to Islam. It opens by stating that the apostate's repentance (Islam) is by disavowing all religions or the one he adopted. The commentary explains that disavowal is essential and must accompany the shahada; simply uttering the shahada out of habit does not suffice. Abu Yusuf's answer in Sharh al-Tahawi gives the precise formula: shahada plus affirmation of what came from Allah plus explicit disavowal of the false religion, including a statement that he never truly entered that religion. The word 'qattu' means 'ever' in the past, implying total disassociation. This requirement is specific to the apostate because for an original kafir (non-Muslim), the conditions differ: those who deny God or associate partners need only one shahada (e.g., saying 'La ilaha illallah') to become Muslim, but a monotheist who denies prophethood must also affirm Muhammad's risala. The text then gives various scenarios from nawazil (fatwa collections): a Christian who says 'La ilaha illallah' and disavows Christianity is not Muslim unless he also states he enters Islam; acknowledging Muhammad as a prophet but limiting his mission to Arabs is insufficient without disavowing one's original faith. Finally, Islam can also be established by actions like praying in congregation or giving adhan. The marginal note explains that 'al-thanawiyya' refers to the Magians (Zoroastrians) who believe in two gods: Yazdan (light) and Ahriman (darkness).

Key Terms
تبرؤ (tabarru'): Disavowal or declaring oneself free from a religion; a necessary element of an apostate's repentance.
شهادتان (shahadatān): The two testimonies of faith: 'La ilaha illallah' and 'Muhammadun Rasulullah'.
ثنوية (thanawiyyah): Dualists, specifically the Magians (Zoroastrians) who believe in two divine principles of good and evil.
قط (qattu): An adverb meaning 'ever' or 'never' in the past sense; used in the disavowal formula: 'I never entered this religion at all'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum statement required for an apostate's return to Islam to be valid?
A) Saying 'La ilaha illallah' alone
B) Saying both shahadas without any disavowal
C) Saying the shahadas and explicitly disavowing the former religion
D) Saying the shahadas and performing two rak'ahs of prayer

Key Takeaway
An apostate's valid return to Islam requires, in addition to the shahada, an explicit disavowal (tabarru') of the religion he adopted, ensuring a sincere break from his apostasy.

The Apostate's Suspended Ownership (Zawal al-Milk Mawqufan)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of apostasy on property ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi doctrine of suspended ownership for the apostate and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
An apostate's ownership is suspended, not terminated – return to Islam restores it, death transfers it to heirs.

This lesson unpacks a core Hanafi distinction: the apostate's property ownership is held in abeyance (mawquf). Unlike the Sahibayn who maintain full ownership, Abu Hanifa views the apostate as a subdued harbi, but with hope of return, leading to suspension. Understanding this concept clarifies many downstream rulings on transactions and inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine the effect of apostasy on the apostate's property ownership. Is it immediately terminated, suspended, or unchanged?
The outcome depends on whether the apostate is seen as a harbi or a mukallaf.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa: ownership ceases but in a suspended manner. Reason: the apostate is a subdued harbi, but hope of return suspends cessation.
The suspension is conceptual: if he returns, ownership reinstated as if never ceased.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: ownership does not cease. Reason: the apostate is a responsible person (mukallaf) in need, like one sentenced to retaliation.
They emphasize continuity of legal capacity.
4
Compare the practical consequences (thamarah)
If the apostate returns to Islam, his property returns to him under Abu Hanifa, while under the Sahibayn it never left. His transactions before return are suspended vs. valid.
Also affects bequests: Abu Yusuf valid, Muhammad limited to one-third.

Book of Siyar / Chapter on Rulings of Apostates. Suspended, so if he becomes Muslim his ownership returns, and if he dies or is killed in his apostasy, the earnings of his Islam are inherited by his heir. This is the meaning adopted in every discretionary punishment by beating as in al-Hawi al-Qudsi. It is mentioned in Fath al-Qadir here: It is narrated from Abu Hanifa that she is beaten every day, and some estimated it as three, and from al-Hasan that she is beaten every day thirty-nine lashes until she dies or becomes Muslim. He did not specify free or slave, and this is killing because continuous beating leads to it. End. And he spoke generally about imprisoning her, so it includes if she joined dar al-harb then was captured and enslaved, then she is compelled to Islam by beating and imprisonment and not killed, as clarified in al-Bada'i. Her enslavement does not remove the compulsion to Islam, just as if a slave woman apostatized initially, she is compelled to Islam. And it includes if she is a minor with reason, because in al-Muhit from the chapter on what is obligatory for a divorced woman before consummation: it is permissible to hold the minor accountable for it. Do you not see that she is imprisoned for apostasy as an adult is imprisoned, and imprisonment is the penalty for apostasy. End. All. His saying: (And the ownership of the apostate over his property ceases with a suspended cessation, so if he becomes Muslim his ownership returns.) They said: This is according to Abu Hanifa. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) his ownership does not cease, because he is a responsible person in need, so until he is killed his ownership remains, like one sentenced to stoning or retaliation. And for him (Abu Hanifa) he is a harbi subdued under our control until he is killed, and he is not killed except by weapons, and this necessitates the cessation of his ownership and his property-right, except that he is called to Islam by compulsion and it is hoped he will return to it, so we suspend his matter. If he becomes Muslim, we make the obstacle as if it never existed regarding this ruling, so he becomes as if he never ceased being Muslim, and the cause does not operate. If he dies or is killed in his apostasy or joins dar al-harb and it is judged that he has joined, his matter is settled, the cause operates, and his ownership ceases. Then the two shaykhs differed regarding the ruling on his voluntary donations: Abu Yusuf said it is from the property like the disposition of one obligated to retaliation. Muhammad said he is like a sick person, so it is from the one-third because he is on the verge of destruction. And in al-Bada'i: There is no dispute that if he becomes Muslim his properties remain under his ownership, and that if he dies or is killed or joins dar al-harb they cease from his ownership. The dispute is only about their cessation by these three matters, whether it is limited to the current state (their view) or traced back to the time of apostasy (his view). The consequence appears in his transactions: according to them they are effective before Islam, and according to him they are suspended due to the suspension of his ownership. End. He restricted it to ownership, because there is no suspension in nullifying his acts of worship, the occurrence of separation between him and his wife, and renewal of faith, for apostasy regarding them has already taken effect. Likewise in al-Inayah. And it is mentioned in al-Khaniyyah: If a Muslim rents a house or real estate or movable property then apostatizes (Allah forbid) and joins dar al-harb and the judge judges his joining, his rental becomes void as if he died, likewise if he hires then apostatizes. And if he bequeaths one-third of his wealth to a man then apostatizes and joins dar al-harb or does not join, his bequest becomes void. Likewise if he appoints a man as executor and makes him trustee of his wealth then apostatizes and joins dar al-harb or does not join, his appointment becomes void. And if he appoints a man as agent then the principal apostatizes and joins dar al-harb, his agent is dismissed according to their statement. If he returns to us as a Muslim, does he become agent again? It is mentioned in Wakalah that he does not return, and in Siyar that he returns. And if the agent apostatizes and joins and it is judged, then returns as a Muslim, Abu Yusuf said he does not return as agent, and Muhammad said he returns. End. The summary: there is no suspension in nullifying his acts of worship, the separation of his wife, his renting and hiring, his bequest and appointment, his agency and being agent. And we have mentioned that among his acts of worship, those that require obligation are suspended by his apostasy and do not return with his Islam. And he restricted it to the apostate, because the female apostate's ownership over her property does not cease without dispute, so her transactions in her property are valid by consensus, because she is not killed, so her apostasy was not a cause for her ownership to cease, as in al-Bada'i. And the apostate who is not killed, and his Islam is questionable as we mentioned, should be joined to her due to the common factor of not being killed, though I have not seen it explicitly. And in al-Ziyadat: The female apostate, if she transacts: if the transaction is valid from a Muslim, it is valid from her; if the transaction is not valid from a Muslim but is valid from someone who follows a religion she has adopted, like Jews and Christians, then her transaction is valid according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) while according to him (Abu Hanifa) the shaykhs differed: some said it is valid, some said only what is valid from a Muslim is valid from her, as in al-Tatar Khaniyyah. Its consequence is in her sale of wine and pork. And he indicated by his saying 'ownership of the apostate over his property' that the discussion concerns the free person, so what the mukatab owns by acquisition does not cease by his apostasy. Hence al-Khaniyyah said: The transactions of the mukatab in his apostasy are valid according to their statement. End. His saying: (And if he dies or is killed in his apostasy, the earnings of his Islam are inherited, and his inheritance goes to the Muslim after paying the debts of his Islam, and the earnings of his apostasy are fay' after paying the debts of his apostasy) is an explanation of the inheritance of the apostate after his actual death. Its gist: what he earned during his Islam is inheritance for his Muslim heirs by agreement, and it is not fay' according to us, contrary to the three imams, because he died as an unbeliever, and a Muslim does not inherit an unbeliever, and it is the property of a harbi with no aman, so it would be fay'. For us, his ownership after apostasy remains, so it transfers at his death to his heirs, traced back to before his apostasy, because apostasy is a cause for death, so it is inheritance of a Muslim from a Muslim, and tracing back is necessary according to the three imams as well, because the Muslims taking it if he has no heir is by way of inheritance, which necessitates the ruling of tracing back legally to before his apostasy; otherwise it would be inheritance of a Muslim from an unbeliever. And the interpretation of the hadith is about the original unbeliever who never had Islam, so his kinship equaled the Muslims in that regard, and his kinship was preferred by the aspect of kinship. Its completion is in Fath al-Qadir. And he argued in al-Bada'i with the fact that Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) when he killed al-Mustawrid al-Ijli for apostasy, he divided his wealth among the Muslims, and that was in the presence of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) without any objection, so it was a consensus. And he indicated by his saying 'his heir' that the consideration is the existence of an heir at the time of death or killing or judgment of joining, and this is the narration of Muhammad from the Imam, and it is the most correct as in al-Nihayah and Fath al-Qadir, because the event after the cause before... reflect. His saying: (Otherwise it would be inheritance of a Muslim from an unbeliever) – I saw it thus in al-Fath, and the phrase is inverted, reflect. His saying: (so his kinship equaled the Muslims in that) – thus in the copies, and it seems that before this some speech is missing. And the phrase from Fath al-Qadir: And the interpretation of the hadith is the original unbeliever who never had Islam, or we say: the entitlement of the Muslims to it is Islam, and the heirs equaled the Muslims in that and were preferred by the aspect of kinship. His saying: (at death or killing or due to)
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the rulings on apostates. It focuses on the legal status of an apostate's property and its disposition. The author first states the relied-upon position: the apostate's ownership over his property is suspended (zawal mawquf). If he returns to Islam, his ownership is restored; if he dies or is killed in apostasy, the earnings from his period of Islam are inherited by his Muslim heirs. The commentary explains that this suspension is the view of Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), ownership does not cease at all during apostasy because the apostate remains legally capable and in need. Abu Hanifa analogizes the apostate to a harbi (enemy combatant) who is subdued; his ownership should cease, but due to the hope of his return to Islam, it is suspended. The passage then details the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the apostate's voluntary transactions (tabarru'at): Abu Yusuf considers them like transactions of one liable to retaliation (valid without restriction), while Muhammad likens him to a sick person, so donations are limited to one-third. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is not explicitly stated here, but the commentary notes the disagreement. Further, the commentary clarifies that the suspension applies only to ownership, not to other effects of apostasy such as nullification of worship, separation from wife, etc. It also distinguishes the female apostate (murtadda): her ownership never ceases, so her transactions are valid without suspension. The inheritance ruling is then explained: earnings during Islam are inherited by Muslim heirs (by consensus among the Hanafis, contrary to the other three imams who consider it fay'). The proof is the consensus (ijma') from the incident of Ali ibn Abi Talib dividing the wealth of al-Mustawrid al-Ijli among the Muslims. The commentary concludes by noting that the existence of an heir at the time of death/execution/judgment of joining dar al-harb is the criterion for inheritance, which is the narration of Muhammad from Abu Hanifa and the most correct position.

Key Terms
المرتد (al-murtadd): A Muslim who apostatizes from Islam by word or deed.
زوال الملك موقوفاً (zawal al-milk mawqufan): Cessation of ownership that is suspended; ownership does not immediately cease but is held in abeyance pending the apostate's return or death.
فيء (fay'): Property taken from enemies without war (booty) that goes to the public treasury for the benefit of Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the legal status of an apostate's property after he returns to Islam?
A) It becomes fay'.
B) It is returned to him.
C) It is distributed to his Muslim heirs.
D) It remains in the bayt al-mal.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi doctrine of suspended ownership (zawal mawquf) uniquely balances the penal status of apostasy with the hope of return.

Inheritance of the Apostate: Earnings of Islam vs. Apostasy
Cross-madhhab dispute on the inheritance of apostate's property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi inheritance ruling for an apostate and explain why earnings of Islam are inherited by Muslim heirs, citing the dalil from Ali's incident.

Core Idea
Earnings during Islam are inherited by Muslim heirs; earnings during apostasy become fay' – a unique Hanafi position backed by companion consensus.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rules for the apostate's estate. The key distinction: what he earned while Muslim is inherited by his Muslim relatives, while what he earned after apostasy becomes fay' for the public treasury. This contradicts the other three schools, so we trace the Hanafi proof, including a companion consensus (ijma) from the incident of Ali (RA).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of property
Distinguish between property acquired during Islam and property acquired after apostasy.
This distinction is crucial for the ruling.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each category
Islam earnings: inherited by Muslim heirs. Apostasy earnings: fay' for the public treasury.
This is agreed upon within the Hanafi school.
3
Contrast with the other three imams
Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali consider all apostate property as fay', because a Muslim cannot inherit from a kafir.
They rely on the hadith: 'The Muslim does not inherit the disbeliever.'
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafis cite the incident of Ali (RA) who divided the wealth of al-Mustawrid al-Ijli after executing him for apostasy. The Companions did not object, indicating consensus that the property goes to Muslim heirs as inheritance, not fay'.
The Hanafis interpret the hadith as referring to the original kafir, not the apostate.
5
Explain the istinad (retroactive effect)
The Hanafi doctrine of suspension allows the property to be traced back to before apostasy, enabling inheritance by Muslim heirs from a time when the apostate was Muslim.
This resolves the apparent conflict with the hadith.

Book of Siyar / Chapter on Rulings of Apostates. Suspended, so if he becomes Muslim his ownership returns, and if he dies or is killed in his apostasy, the earnings of his Islam are inherited by his heir. This is the meaning adopted in every discretionary punishment by beating as in al-Hawi al-Qudsi. It is mentioned in Fath al-Qadir here: It is narrated from Abu Hanifa that she is beaten every day, and some estimated it as three, and from al-Hasan that she is beaten every day thirty-nine lashes until she dies or becomes Muslim. He did not specify free or slave, and this is killing because continuous beating leads to it. End. And he spoke generally about imprisoning her, so it includes if she joined dar al-harb then was captured and enslaved, then she is compelled to Islam by beating and imprisonment and not killed, as clarified in al-Bada'i. Her enslavement does not remove the compulsion to Islam, just as if a slave woman apostatized initially, she is compelled to Islam. And it includes if she is a minor with reason, because in al-Muhit from the chapter on what is obligatory for a divorced woman before consummation: it is permissible to hold the minor accountable for it. Do you not see that she is imprisoned for apostasy as an adult is imprisoned, and imprisonment is the penalty for apostasy. End. All. His saying: (And the ownership of the apostate over his property ceases with a suspended cessation, so if he becomes Muslim his ownership returns.) They said: This is according to Abu Hanifa. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) his ownership does not cease, because he is a responsible person in need, so until he is killed his ownership remains, like one sentenced to stoning or retaliation. And for him (Abu Hanifa) he is a harbi subdued under our control until he is killed, and he is not killed except by weapons, and this necessitates the cessation of his ownership and his property-right, except that he is called to Islam by compulsion and it is hoped he will return to it, so we suspend his matter. If he becomes Muslim, we make the obstacle as if it never existed regarding this ruling, so he becomes as if he never ceased being Muslim, and the cause does not operate. If he dies or is killed in his apostasy or joins dar al-harb and it is judged that he has joined, his matter is settled, the cause operates, and his ownership ceases. Then the two shaykhs differed regarding the ruling on his voluntary donations: Abu Yusuf said it is from the property like the disposition of one obligated to retaliation. Muhammad said he is like a sick person, so it is from the one-third because he is on the verge of destruction. And in al-Bada'i: There is no dispute that if he becomes Muslim his properties remain under his ownership, and that if he dies or is killed or joins dar al-harb they cease from his ownership. The dispute is only about their cessation by these three matters, whether it is limited to the current state (their view) or traced back to the time of apostasy (his view). The consequence appears in his transactions: according to them they are effective before Islam, and according to him they are suspended due to the suspension of his ownership. End. He restricted it to ownership, because there is no suspension in nullifying his acts of worship, the occurrence of separation between him and his wife, and renewal of faith, for apostasy regarding them has already taken effect. Likewise in al-Inayah. And it is mentioned in al-Khaniyyah: If a Muslim rents a house or real estate or movable property then apostatizes (Allah forbid) and joins dar al-harb and the judge judges his joining, his rental becomes void as if he died, likewise if he hires then apostatizes. And if he bequeaths one-third of his wealth to a man then apostatizes and joins dar al-harb or does not join, his bequest becomes void. Likewise if he appoints a man as executor and makes him trustee of his wealth then apostatizes and joins dar al-harb or does not join, his appointment becomes void. And if he appoints a man as agent then the principal apostatizes and joins dar al-harb, his agent is dismissed according to their statement. If he returns to us as a Muslim, does he become agent again? It is mentioned in Wakalah that he does not return, and in Siyar that he returns. And if the agent apostatizes and joins and it is judged, then returns as a Muslim, Abu Yusuf said he does not return as agent, and Muhammad said he returns. End. The summary: there is no suspension in nullifying his acts of worship, the separation of his wife, his renting and hiring, his bequest and appointment, his agency and being agent. And we have mentioned that among his acts of worship, those that require obligation are suspended by his apostasy and do not return with his Islam. And he restricted it to the apostate, because the female apostate's ownership over her property does not cease without dispute, so her transactions in her property are valid by consensus, because she is not killed, so her apostasy was not a cause for her ownership to cease, as in al-Bada'i. And the apostate who is not killed, and his Islam is questionable as we mentioned, should be joined to her due to the common factor of not being killed, though I have not seen it explicitly. And in al-Ziyadat: The female apostate, if she transacts: if the transaction is valid from a Muslim, it is valid from her; if the transaction is not valid from a Muslim but is valid from someone who follows a religion she has adopted, like Jews and Christians, then her transaction is valid according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) while according to him (Abu Hanifa) the shaykhs differed: some said it is valid, some said only what is valid from a Muslim is valid from her, as in al-Tatar Khaniyyah. Its consequence is in her sale of wine and pork. And he indicated by his saying 'ownership of the apostate over his property' that the discussion concerns the free person, so what the mukatab owns by acquisition does not cease by his apostasy. Hence al-Khaniyyah said: The transactions of the mukatab in his apostasy are valid according to their statement. End. His saying: (And if he dies or is killed in his apostasy, the earnings of his Islam are inherited, and his inheritance goes to the Muslim after paying the debts of his Islam, and the earnings of his apostasy are fay' after paying the debts of his apostasy) is an explanation of the inheritance of the apostate after his actual death. Its gist: what he earned during his Islam is inheritance for his Muslim heirs by agreement, and it is not fay' according to us, contrary to the three imams, because he died as an unbeliever, and a Muslim does not inherit an unbeliever, and it is the property of a harbi with no aman, so it would be fay'. For us, his ownership after apostasy remains, so it transfers at his death to his heirs, traced back to before his apostasy, because apostasy is a cause for death, so it is inheritance of a Muslim from a Muslim, and tracing back is necessary according to the three imams as well, because the Muslims taking it if he has no heir is by way of inheritance, which necessitates the ruling of tracing back legally to before his apostasy; otherwise it would be inheritance of a Muslim from an unbeliever. And the interpretation of the hadith is about the original unbeliever who never had Islam, so his kinship equaled the Muslims in that regard, and his kinship was preferred by the aspect of kinship. Its completion is in Fath al-Qadir. And he argued in al-Bada'i with the fact that Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) when he killed al-Mustawrid al-Ijli for apostasy, he divided his wealth among the Muslims, and that was in the presence of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) without any objection, so it was a consensus. And he indicated by his saying 'his heir' that the consideration is the existence of an heir at the time of death or killing or judgment of joining, and this is the narration of Muhammad from the Imam, and it is the most correct as in al-Nihayah and Fath al-Qadir, because the event after the cause before... reflect. His saying: (Otherwise it would be inheritance of a Muslim from an unbeliever) – I saw it thus in al-Fath, and the phrase is inverted, reflect. His saying: (so his kinship equaled the Muslims in that) – thus in the copies, and it seems that before this some speech is missing. And the phrase from Fath al-Qadir: And the interpretation of the hadith is the original unbeliever who never had Islam, or we say: the entitlement of the Muslims to it is Islam, and the heirs equaled the Muslims in that and were preferred by the aspect of kinship. His saying: (at death or killing or due to)
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the rulings on apostates. It focuses on the legal status of an apostate's property and its disposition. The author first states the relied-upon position: the apostate's ownership over his property is suspended (zawal mawquf). If he returns to Islam, his ownership is restored; if he dies or is killed in apostasy, the earnings from his period of Islam are inherited by his Muslim heirs. The commentary explains that this suspension is the view of Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), ownership does not cease at all during apostasy because the apostate remains legally capable and in need. Abu Hanifa analogizes the apostate to a harbi (enemy combatant) who is subdued; his ownership should cease, but due to the hope of his return to Islam, it is suspended. The passage then details the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the apostate's voluntary transactions (tabarru'at): Abu Yusuf considers them like transactions of one liable to retaliation (valid without restriction), while Muhammad likens him to a sick person, so donations are limited to one-third. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is not explicitly stated here, but the commentary notes the disagreement. Further, the commentary clarifies that the suspension applies only to ownership, not to other effects of apostasy such as nullification of worship, separation from wife, etc. It also distinguishes the female apostate (murtadda): her ownership never ceases, so her transactions are valid without suspension. The inheritance ruling is then explained: earnings during Islam are inherited by Muslim heirs (by consensus among the Hanafis, contrary to the other three imams who consider it fay'). The proof is the consensus (ijma') from the incident of Ali ibn Abi Talib dividing the wealth of al-Mustawrid al-Ijli among the Muslims. The commentary concludes by noting that the existence of an heir at the time of death/execution/judgment of joining dar al-harb is the criterion for inheritance, which is the narration of Muhammad from Abu Hanifa and the most correct position.

Key Terms
المرتد (al-murtadd): A Muslim who apostatizes from Islam by word or deed.
زوال الملك موقوفاً (zawal al-milk mawqufan): Cessation of ownership that is suspended; ownership does not immediately cease but is held in abeyance pending the apostate's return or death.
فيء (fay'): Property taken from enemies without war (booty) that goes to the public treasury for the benefit of Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafis, what is the fate of property earned by an apostate during his period of apostasy if he dies in apostasy?
A) Inherited by his Muslim heirs.
B) Given to his non-Muslim relatives.
C) Becomes fay' for the public treasury.
D) Returned to his heirs after a waiting period.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafis uniquely distinguish between pre- and post-apostasy earnings, relying on the suspension doctrine and companion consensus.

intermediate

The Status of an Apostate's Earnings: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Explaining the intra-Hanafi dispute over apostate's earnings and debt payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on an apostate's earnings during apostasy, explain the underlying usul difference, and apply the mufta bihi ruling for debt payment.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: apostate's earnings during apostasy are fay'; Sahibayn: they are owned and inherited.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq delves into a key intra-Hanafi dispute: what happens to property an apostate earns while in a state of apostasy? The answer hinges on whether apostasy nullifies prior ownership—a foundational question with sharp practical consequences. You will trace the arguments of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and see how this dispute affects the payment of the apostate's debts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling
The core question: Are earnings acquired during apostasy inherited by the apostate's Muslim heirs, or do they become fay' (booty placed in the Muslim treasury)?
This is not about property owned before apostasy; that is inherited by agreement.
2
State each position
Abu Hanifa: earnings during apostasy are fay'. Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): earnings are owned and inherited like property before apostasy.
The Sahibayn's view is the one adopted in Zahir al-Riwayah for many rulings, but here both views are from Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Trace the underlying usul principle
Abu Hanifa's argument: ownership requires existence before apostasy; earnings during apostasy have no prior existence, so they cannot be owned or inherited. The Sahibayn argue that apostasy does not nullify property rights, so ownership continues and new earnings are owned.
This disagreement is rooted in a broader ikhtilaf: does apostasy dissolve all prior proprietary rights?
4
Analyze the debt payment rulings
On Sahibayn's view, both types of earnings are the apostate's property, so debts can be paid from either. On Abu Hanifa's view, there are two narrations: (1) debts of Islam paid from Islamic earnings first, (2) debts of apostasy paid from apostasy earnings first. The correct view (sahih) according to the author is that Islamic earnings are the apostate's true wealth, so debts of Islam are paid from them first, and apostasy earnings (being community property) are used only if necessary.
The narration of al-Hasan (from Islamic earnings) is considered the strongest.
5
Consider the scope and exceptions
These rulings apply only to free male apostates. A female apostate's earnings go to her heirs (no fay'), and a mukatib's earnings belong to his master. The wife of a male apostate inherits absolutely; the husband of a female apostate inherits only if she apostatized while sick.
The distinction for females is because they are not combatants (harb), so the cause for fay' is absent.

أيضاً كما في النهاية. وقوله في الهداية إنها رواية عن أبي حنيفة أي رواية زفر عنه لكنها ضعيفة كما علمت. وظاهر الولوالجية أنه لو لم يكن له إلا أحد النوعين يقضى الدينان منه اتفاقاً وسنوضحه من بعد إن شاء الله تعالى.
وقدمنا أن الكلام إنما هو في الحر وأن المكاتب خارج عن هذه الأحكام فلذا في الجوهرة : إن ما اكتسبه المكاتب في حال ردته لا يكون فيئاً وإنما يكون لمولاه لتعلق حقه به وسنوضحه من بعد إن شاء الله تعالى. وقيد بالمرتد لأن المرتدة كسباها لورثتها لأنه لا حراب منها فلم يوجد سبب الفيء بخلاف المرتد عند أبي حنيفة، ويرثها زوجها المسلم إن ارتدت وهي مريضة لقصدها إبطال حقه، وإن كانت صحيحة لا يرثها لأنها لا تقتل فلم يتعلق حقه بمالها بالردة بخلاف المرتد . والحاصل أن زوجة المرتد ترث منه مطلقاً وزوج المرتدة لا يرثها إلا إذا ارتدت مريضة والكسب بفتح الكاف وكسرها الجمع كسبه جمعه؛ كذا في القاموس. وقد قدمنا حكم المرتدة في النكاح والعدة في باب نكاح الكافر .
Book of Siyar / Chapter of Rulings of Apostates. Also: the one whose waiting period has expired does not inherit; thus in Fath al-Qadir. And it should be considered a branching also on the narration other than Abu Yusuf's; as for his narration, there is no difference between the one who has been consummated and others. And the heir is restricted by Islam because the disbeliever does not inherit from the apostate. He is his heir. In al-Bada'i: If both spouses apostasize together, then she gives birth to a child, then the father is killed on his apostasy: if she gives birth to him less than six months from the time of apostasy, he inherits from him because it is known that impregnation occurred in the state of Islam definitely; if she gives birth to him six months or more from the time of apostasy, he does not inherit from him because it is possible that he was conceived in the state of apostasy, so he does not inherit with doubt. And if the husband apostasizes without the woman, or he has a Muslim umm walad, along with his Muslim heirs, even if she gives birth to him more than six months, because the mother is Muslim, so the child is under the ruling of Islam following his mother, so he inherits from his father. End. As for that which was his earning during his apostasy, there is disagreement: They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: like the first, inheritance, because his ownership remains after apostasy, so it transfers upon his death to his heirs, attributed to before his apostasy. And Imam (Abu Hanifa) said: It is fay' placed in the Muslims' treasury like a lost item, because attribution is only possible for the earning of Islam due to its existence before apostasy, and attribution is not possible for the earning of apostasy due to its non-existence before it, and the condition for attribution of inheritance is its existence before it. The summary is that he has no ownership in what he earned during his apostasy when he dies or is killed, and what is not owned by him is not inherited from him. The two (Sahibayn) since they said that his properties do not cease with apostasy, they said that his earning during it is owned by him, so it is inherited from him. So the disagreement here is based on the previous disagreement about the cessation of his properties by apostasy. In al-Qamus: al-fay' is what was sun and the shadow copies it, and spoils, and kharaj, and a piece of bird, and return. End. So it has five linguistic meanings. As for terminologically, it is what is placed in the Muslims' treasury. As for the ruling of his debts, it indicates that his debts of Islam are paid from his earning of Islam, and his debt of apostasy is paid from his earning of apostasy. The summary: on their (Sahibayn's) view, his debts are paid from both earnings because both are his property, so inheritance applies in both. As for the Imam's view, there are two narrations: in the narration of Abu Yusuf from him, it is from the earning of apostasy unless it is insufficient, then the remainder is paid from the earning of Islam. In the narration of al-Hasan from him, it is from the earning of Islam unless it is insufficient, then the remainder is paid from the earning of apostasy, and this is the correct one, because a person's debt is from his wealth, it is not paid from the wealth of others; similarly, the debt of the deceased is paid from his wealth, not from the wealth of his heir, and his wealth is the earning of Islam. As for the earning of apostasy, it is the wealth of the community of Muslims, so debt is not paid from it except for necessity. So if the earning of Islam is insufficient for it, necessity materializes, so the remainder is paid from it; thus in al-Bada'i, and thus al-Walwaliji authenticated. You have known that what is in the text is not according to any of the three views; rather, al-Bada'i mentioned it as a view of al-Hasan and Zufar: he said al-Hasan said: the debt of Islam is from the earning of Islam, and the debt of apostasy is from the earning of apostasy, and it is the view of Zufar. End. The truth is that it is a narration of Zufar from the Imam also, as in al-Nihaya. And his statement in al-Hidaya that it is a narration from Abu Hanifa, i.e., a narration of Zufar from him, but it is weak, as you have known. And the apparent view of al-Walwaliji is that if he has only one of the two types, both debts are paid from it by agreement, and we will clarify it later, God willing. And we have said that the discussion is only about the free person, and that the mukatib is excluded from these rulings; thus in al-Jawhara: what the mukatib earns during his apostasy is not fay' but rather belongs to his master due to his right being attached to it, and we will clarify later, God willing. And it is restricted to the male apostate because the female apostate's earnings are for her heirs because there is no harb (war) from her, so the cause of fay' does not exist, unlike the male apostate according to Abu Hanifa. And her Muslim husband inherits from her if she apostasized while sick, intending to nullify his right; but if she was healthy, he does not inherit from her because she is not killed, so his right does not attach to her wealth by apostasy, unlike the male apostate. The conclusion: the wife of the male apostate inherits from him absolutely, and the husband of the female apostate inherits from her only if she apostasized while sick. And al-kasb (earning) with fatha on the kaf and kasra on it, the plural is kasbahu jam'ahu; thus in al-Qamus. And we have already presented the ruling of the female apostate in marriage and waiting period in the chapter of marriage of the disbeliever.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the earnings and debts of an apostate. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq is commenting on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding on rulings related to apostates. The passage begins by discussing inheritance of the apostate's wife after her waiting period, then moves to the core dispute: whether earnings acquired during apostasy are inherited by the apostate's heirs or become fay' (booty) for the Muslim treasury. Abu Hanifa holds that such earnings are not owned by the apostate because ownership requires existence before apostasy, so upon death they become fay'. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that property rights do not cease with apostasy, so earnings are owned and inherited. This disagreement stems from a foundational dispute about whether apostasy nullifies past ownership. The passage then explains the payment of debts: on the Sahibayn's view, both types of earnings are owned, so debts can be paid from either. On Abu Hanifa's view, there are two narrations: one preferring payment from apostasy earnings, the other from Islamic earnings. The correct view according to the author is the latter (from Islamic earnings first), because a person's debt should be paid from his own wealth, and Islamic earnings are his wealth, while apostasy earnings belong to the Muslim community. The passage also notes that the view attributed to al-Hasan and Zufar is a weak narration. Finally, it clarifies that these rulings apply only to free male apostates; a female apostate's earnings go to her heirs, and a mukatib's earnings go to his master. The husband's inheritance from an apostate wife is conditional on her being sick at the time of apostasy, to prevent intentional disinheritance.

Key Terms
فيء (fay'): Property taken from enemies without war, placed in the Muslim treasury; here specifically the earnings of an apostate that become public property.
ردة (riddah): Apostasy from Islam by a Muslim; triggers specific rulings on property, marriage, and penal law.
كسب (kasb): Earnings or acquisitions; here distinguished between earnings before apostasy (Islamic) and during apostasy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi view on debt payment (as per the author's preferred narration), from which earnings are an apostate's Islamic debts paid first?
A) From his apostasy earnings, because those are more recent.
B) From his Islamic earnings, because those are his own wealth.
C) From the combined total of both earnings.
D) From the Muslim treasury, because apostasy earnings belong to the community.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has a genuine internal dispute on whether an apostate's earnings during apostasy are owned or become fay', with practical consequences for inheritance and debt payment.
