Legal Death of an Apostate by Lihaq: Requirements and Consequences
Expansion on lihaq as legal death with procedural requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on lihaq (an apostate joining the land of war) and explain the requirement of a judge's decree, the consequences for mudabbars, umm walad, and debts, and the intra-madhhab dispute regarding the order of the decree.

Core Idea
Lihaq makes an apostate legally dead, but only after a judge's decree—a procedural safeguard against potential return.

This passage expands on the Hanafi rule that an apostate who joins the land of war (lihaq) is treated as dead for certain purposes: his mudabbars and umm walad are freed, and his debts become due immediately. However, this status is not automatic; a judge must confirm the lihaq because the apostate might return to Islam. The author then navigates a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute: must the judge issue a standalone decree of joining before applying these consequences, or can the decree be combined with the ruling on a specific consequence? Understanding this procedural nuance is essential for correctly applying the law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Determine the act being ruled upon: the apostate's joining of the land of war (lihaq). The ruling is that lihaq triggers legal death for specific consequences: freeing of mudabbars and umm walad, and immediate payment of debts.
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to actual death. The common illa (effective cause) is the severance of Islamic authority (inqita' wilayat al-ilzam). Since lihaq severs this authority, the same rulings apply as for death.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The analogy operates through the idea that once an apostate joins the enemy, he is beyond Islamic jurisdiction, just as a dead person is. Thus, rulings that depend on the master being alive (like slave ownership) terminate.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
There is a dispute about the order of the judge's decree. The majority (including Muhammad) require a prior, standalone decree of lihaq before applying consequences. Some narrations (e.g., in al-Mujtaba) allow the decree on a specific consequence (like freeing a mudabbar) to suffice, implying simultaneous recognition of lihaq.
The author clarifies that the prior decree can be issued within a claim by an affected party.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The correct (mufta bihi) position is that the decree of lihaq must precede specific rulings, but it does not have to be a separate formal decree; it can be made in the context of a claim (e.g., by a mudabbar seeking freedom). This balances the need for certainty with practicality.
See Fath al-Qadir and al-Nahr for support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن حكم بلحاقه عتق مدبروه وأم ولده وحل دينه لأنه باللحاق صار من أهل الحرب وهم أموات في حق أحكام الإسلام لانقطاع ولاية الإلزام كما فصار كالموت إلا أنه لا يستقر لحاقه إلا بقضاء القاضي لاحتمال العود إلينا فلا بد من وهو باتفاق الإمام وصاحبيه كما في الجوهرة. وإذا تقرر موته تثبت الأحكام المتعلقة به من عتق المدبر وأم الولد وسقوط الأجل كما في الموت الحقيقي، والمرتدة إذا لحقت بدار الحرب فهي على هذا من عتق مدبريها وحلول دين عليها . ولم يذكر قسمة ماله بين ورثته لظهوره ولما سيشير إليه عند قوله فما وجده في يد وارثه ولم يذكر حكم مكاتبه وحكمه كما في البدائع أنه يؤدى إلى الورثة فيعتق وإذا عتق فولاؤه للمرتد لأنه المعتق .اهـ. وفي المجتبى بعلامة حس ظ : القضاء باللحاق ليس بشرط وإنما يشترط قضاؤه بشيء من أحكام الموتى. وعامتهم على أنه يشترط القضاء باللحاق سابقاً على قضائه بهذه الأحكام وإليه أشار محمد في كثير من المواضع اهـ. وفي فتح القدير : وإذا صار اللحاق كالموت لا أنه حقيقة الموت لا يستقر حتى يقضى به سابقاً على القضاء بشيء من هذه الأحكام المذكورة في الصحيح لا أن القضاء بشيء منها يكفي بل يسبق القضاء باللحاق ثم تثبت الأحكام المذكورة .اهـ. وظاهرهما أن القضاء باللحاق قصداً أن لا يصح إلا في ضمن دعوى حق للعبد وقد قالوا : إن يوم الموت لا يدخل تحت القضاء ويوم القتل يدخل كما في جامع الفصولين والبزازية واللحاق موت حكماً فينبغي أن لا يدخل تحت القضاء قصداً فينبغي أنه لو حكم بعتق مدبره لثبوت لحاقه مرتداً ببينة عادلة فإنه صحيح، ولا يشترط له تقدم الحكم بلحاقه ولم أر إلى الآن . أوضح هذا المحل وقوله «عتق مدبروه» معناه من ثلث ماله وإنما لم يصرح به لما تقدم في القضاء بلحاقه وقوله فعلى الأصح وهي رواية عن محمد قوله : (وظاهرهما أن القضاء باللحاق قصداً صحيح) قال في النهر : ليس معنى الحكم بلحاقه سابقاً على هذه الأمور أن يقول ابتداء حكمت بلحاقه بل إذا ادعى مدير مثلاً على وارثه أنه لحق بدار الحرب مرتداً وأنه عتق بسببه وثبت
His statement: (And if it is judged regarding his joining [the land of war], his mudabbars and umm walad are freed, and his debt becomes due, because by joining he becomes one of the people of war, and they are dead with respect to the rulings of Islam due to the severance of the authority of obligation, so he becomes like dead, except that his joining is not established except by the judge's decree due to the possibility of his return to us, so it is necessary, and it is by agreement of the Imam and his two companions as in al-Jawhara. And when his death is established, the rulings related to it are established: freeing of the mudabbar and umm walad, and lapse of term, as in actual death. And the female apostate if she joins the land of war, then she is like this regarding the freeing of her mudabbars and the falling due of debt upon her. And he did not mention the division of his wealth among his heirs because it is apparent and due to what he will indicate when he says: what is found in the hand of his heir. And he did not mention the ruling of his mukatib; his ruling as in al-Bada'i is that he is paid to the heirs and becomes free, and when he becomes free, his wala' is for the apostate because he is the manumitter. End. In al-Mujtaba with the mark of Hasan: the decree regarding joining is not a condition; rather, his decree regarding something from the rulings of the dead is required. And the majority hold that the decree regarding joining is required prior to his decree regarding these rulings, and Muhammad indicated that in many places. End. In Fath al-Qadir: And when joining becomes like death, not that it is actual death, it is not established until it is decreed prior to the decree regarding any of these mentioned rulings in the correct view; it is not sufficient to decree something from them; rather, the decree regarding joining precedes, then the mentioned rulings are established. End. And their apparent meaning is that the decree regarding joining intentionally is not valid except within a claim of right for the slave, and they said: the day of death is not subject to decree, and the day of killing is subject, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn and al-Bazzaziyyah, and joining is death legally, so it should not be subject to decree intentionally. So it should be that if he decreed the freeing of his mudabbar due to the establishment of his joining as an apostate with just witnesses, then it is valid, and prior decree of joining is not required for it. And I have not yet seen this point clarified. His statement 'his mudabbars are freed' means from one-third of his wealth, and he did not explicitly mention it due to what preceded regarding the decree of joining. And his statement: according to the correct view, and it is a narration from Muhammad. His statement: (And their apparent meaning is that the decree regarding joining intentionally is valid). He said in al-Nahr: the meaning of the decree regarding joining prior to these matters is not that he says initially 'I decreed his joining'; rather, when a mudabbar, for example, claims against his heir that he joined the land of war as an apostate and that he became free due to it, and it is established...
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling that when an apostate joins the land of war (lihaq), he is treated as dead for certain purposes: his mudabbars (slaves promised freedom upon his death) and umm walad (slave mother of his child) are freed, and his debts become due immediately. The reasoning is that by joining the enemy, he falls under the category of 'people of war' (harbi), who are considered dead regarding Islamic rulings because Islamic authority no longer applies. However, the author notes that this status is not automatic; it requires a judge's decree because the apostate might return. The agreement of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn is that the decree is necessary, but there is a dispute about the order: the majority, including Muhammad, hold that the decree of joining must precede any ruling on the specific consequences (like freeing slaves). Some, like in al-Mujtaba, say that the decree on the consequences suffices. The author clarifies that the decree of joining can be made within a claim (e.g., by a mudabbar seeking freedom) and does not need to be a standalone ruling. The freed mudabbars and umm walad are from one-third of the apostate's wealth, as with actual death. The passage also notes that the mukatib's contract is affected—he must pay the remaining installments to the heirs and becomes free, with his wala' going to the apostate. The female apostate who joins the land of war is treated similarly. This nuanced discussion ensures that the legal fiction of 'death by joining' is properly procedural and does not lead to injustice.

Key Terms
لحاق (lihaq): An apostate joining the land of war (dar al-harb), which triggers legal death for certain rulings.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death; here, the mudabbar is freed upon the apostate's lihaq from one-third of the estate.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master; upon the master's death (including legal death by lihaq), she is freed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the agreed-upon position of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, what is required for the rulings of death to apply to an apostate who joins the land of war?
A) A judge's decree regarding the joining.
B) The apostate's written statement.
C) The testimony of two Muslim witnesses.
D) The passage of one year.

Key Takeaway
Lihaq is treated as legal death for specific purposes but requires a prior judge's decree, which can be issued within a claim from an affected party.

Classification of an Apostate's Transactions
Summarizes categories of apostate's actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five actions of an apostate that are valid by agreement and the five that are invalid by agreement, and explain the basis for the suspended category.

Core Idea
An apostate's actions are not all void: five remain valid, five are invalid by agreement, and the rest are suspended pending his return to Islam.

In Hanafi fiqh, the status of a murtadd (apostate) is unique. His legal acts are not automatically void. Instead, the jurists categorized his transactions based on whether they depend on the millah (Islamic legal personality) or not. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents this classification, distinguishing between actions that remain valid despite apostasy, those that are invalid by agreement, and those that are suspended (mawquf) until the apostate returns to Islam or dies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of actions
The passage distinguishes actions that 'depend on the millah' (الملة) and those that 'do not depend on the millah in terms of guardianship or ownership'. Actions depending on millah are valid by agreement; those not depending on millah are invalid by agreement.
Millah here refers to the legal capacity to act as a Muslim.
2
List the five valid actions
The valid actions by agreement are: marriage (نكاح), slaughter of an animal (ذبيحة), hunting with a trained dog, saker, or arrow (صيد بالكلب والبازي والرمي), inheritance (إرث), and testimony (شهادة).
These actions are valid even from an apostate according to all Hanafi authorities.
3
List the five invalid actions
The invalid actions by agreement are: taking a female slave as an umm walad (استيلاد), divorce (طلاق), accepting a gift (قبول الهبة), handing over preemption (تسليم الشفعة), and placing interdiction on a slave who has been given permission to trade (حجر على عبده المأذون).
Note: 'handing over preemption' is invalid, but the right to claim preemption is disputed (see Lesson 2).
4
Understand the suspended category
All other transactions, such as manumission, wills, gifts (given by the apostate), partnerships, and disposal over his young children, are suspended (موقوف). They become effective if the apostate returns to Islam, and become void if he dies or is executed without repentance.
The suspended category is a distinctive Hanafi concept; other schools may treat them as invalid outright.
5
Review the couplet of al-Maqdisi
The passage ends with a couplet by al-Maqdisi summarizing the valid and invalid actions, and noting that partnership and disposal over a child are suspended.
The couplet: 'وباتفاق صح دعوى ولده كذا طلاقه وحجر وهكذا قبوله لهبته وهكذا تسليمه لشفعته وباطل بالاتقاق نکحه وهكذا میراثه وذبحه وأوقفوا مفاوضات شــرکـتـه تصريفه لطفله وطفلته'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن ما يعتمد الملة لا منه اتفاقاً يصح وهي خمسة؛ النكاح والذبيحة والصيد بالكلب والبازي والرمي والإرث والشهادة وما لا يعتمد الملة ولاية ولا حقيقة ملك فإنه منه اتفاقاً وهي خمس أيضاً؛ الاستيلاد والطلاق وقبول الهبة وتسليم الشفعة والحجر على عبده المأذون... فقد ظهر أن تصرفاته على أربعة أقسام ) نظمها العلامة المقدسي في شرحه فقال :
وباتفاق صح دعوى ولده كذا طلاقه وحجر وهكذا قبوله لهبته وهكذا تسليمه لشفعته وباطل بالاتقاق نکحه وهكذا میراثه وذبحه وأوقفوا مفاوضات شــرکـتـه تصريفه لطفله وطفلته
The conclusion: what depends on the millah (religion) — from him (the apostate) — is valid by agreement, and they are five: marriage, slaughter, hunting with dog/saker/arrow, inheritance, and testimony. And what does not depend on the millah in terms of guardianship or real ownership — from him it is invalid by agreement, and they are also five: istilad (taking as umm walad), divorce, accepting a gift, handing over preemption, and placing interdiction on his slave with permission... So it has become clear that his transactions are on four categories. The scholar al-Maqdisi organized them in his commentary and said: By agreement his claim of paternity is valid, likewise his divorce and interdiction, likewise his acceptance of a gift, and likewise his handing over of preemption; invalid by agreement are his marriage, inheritance, slaughter; and they suspended his partnership (mufawada), his company, and his disposal over his young child.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq summarizes the Hanafi classification of an apostate's legal actions. The author states that actions which depend on the millah (the religion of Islam, specifically the capacity to act as a Muslim) are valid from an apostate by agreement among the Hanafis. These include marriage, slaughter of animals, hunting with trained dogs or birds, inheritance, and giving testimony. On the other hand, actions that do not depend on the millah in terms of having guardianship or full ownership are invalid by agreement. These include making a female slave an umm walad, divorce, accepting a gift, exercising preemption, and placing interdiction on a permitted slave. The remaining actions are suspended, such as manumission, wills, gifts, etc., as detailed earlier. The text then presents a couplet by al-Maqdisi that organizes these into four categories: valid by agreement, invalid by agreement, suspended, and another category? Actually the couplet lists valid and invalid, and then 'they suspended' (awqifu) for partnership and disposal over child. This classification is central to understanding how Hanafi fiqh treats the apostate's acts: they are not automatically void, but their validity depends on whether the act requires Islamic legal personality.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): A person who has apostatized from Islam by explicit statement or action, subject to specific rulings including potential execution if male.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended: a legal action that is not immediately effective but becomes valid if the apostate returns to Islam, and becomes void if he dies or leaves.
الملة (al-millah): The religion of Islam, specifically the legal capacity to act as a Muslim (e.g., for marriage, slaughter).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification presented in the passage, which of the following actions of an apostate is valid by agreement?
A) His marriage
B) His inheritance
C) His acceptance of a gift
D) His divorce

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh classifies an apostate's transactions into three categories: valid by agreement (5 acts), invalid by agreement (5 acts), and suspended (everything else), with the suspension being a distinctive feature that protects the apostate's rights if he returns to Islam.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Apostate's Preemption
Explains the intra-Hanafi dispute on preemption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad regarding an apostate's right to claim preemption (shuf'a), identify the proofs on each side, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Can an apostate claim preemption? Abu Hanifa blocks it, Muhammad allows it—the dispute centers on whether contractual capacity or ownership is the basis of shuf'a.

Among the actions of an apostate, the right to claim preemption (shuf'a) is a point of contention within the Hanafi school. While the passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq states that 'handing over preemption' is invalid by agreement, the act of claiming preemption is a separate matter. Imam Muhammad argues that the apostate retains ownership and therefore may exercise shuf'a, while Imam Abu Hanifa argues that preemption requires the ability to fulfill the contract, which the apostate lacks. This lesson examines the reasoning and the settled position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested action
The dispute is specifically about the apostate claiming preemption (shuf'a), not about handing over preemption (which is invalid by agreement). Shuf'a is a right of ownership to take over property sold to a neighbor.
2
State the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the apostate cannot claim shuf'a until he returns to Islam. Imam Muhammad holds that he can claim it immediately, and his claim is valid.
Both are based on different understandings of the nature of shuf'a.
3
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa's proof: Shuf'a is a contract-like right that requires the claimant to be able to complete the transaction. The apostate's status makes him unreliable and his actions are suspended, so he cannot claim. Muhammad's proof: Shuf'a is a right of ownership; the apostate still owns property, so he retains the right to claim preemption regardless of his religious status.
The disagreement is rooted in whether shuf'a is primarily a property right or a contractual act.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a neighbor sells a shared property and an apostate claims preemption, under Muhammad's view the apostate can acquire the property immediately. Under Abu Hanifa's view, the claim is suspended until the apostate returns to Islam; if he dies without repenting, the preemption right lapses.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The later Hanafi position, as stated in Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir and other works, favors Muhammad's view. Thus, in practice, an apostate may claim preemption.
This is an example of an intra-madhhab tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): A preemptive right of purchase in cases of co-ownership or adjacency of property.

D) No, because handing over preemption is invalid by agreement.

Four Categories of an Apostate's Transactions
Classification of apostate's transactions based on legal capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories of a male apostate's transactions and explain the rulings for each category, including the intra-Hanafi disagreement on mufawadah.

Core Idea
A male apostate's transactions are classified: valid, suspended, invalid, and those that become a limited partnership (inan).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq systematically classifies the transactions of a male apostate based on legal capacity. It draws a key distinction between transactions that require equality of possession or transitive guardianship and those that do not. The ruling splits into four categories, each with a distinct outcome depending on the apostate's return to Islam or death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction type
Recognize whether the transaction involves equality of possession (mufawadah) or transitive guardianship (e.g., over minor children). These are subject to suspension.
2
Apply the four-category framework
Categorize each transaction as: 1) valid by agreement, 2) suspended by agreement, 3) invalid by agreement, or 4) subject to disagreement (mufawadah). The poem by al-Maqdisi lists examples.
3
Analyze the mufawadah dispute
Note that Abu Hanifa holds it becomes void, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it becomes an 'inan partnership if the apostate perishes.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Distinguish male vs. female apostate
The rulings apply only to male apostates; female apostates' transactions are valid by all, as she is not killed.
5
Apply to new cases
Use the framework to classify other transactions mentioned in the text, such as marriage, inheritance, slaughter, testimony, hunting, etc.
The poem gives a comprehensive list.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يعتبر المساواة من التصرف أو ولاية متعدية فإنه لا ينفذ منه اتفاقاً، فالأول المفاوضة فإذا فاوض مسلماً توقفت اتفاقاً إن أسلم نفذت وإن هلك بطلت وتصير عناناً . من الأصل عندهما وتبطل عنده ؛ كذا في الخانية والثاني التصرف على ولده الصغير وفي مال ولده موقوف اتفاقاً فقد ظهر أن تصرفاته على أربعة أقسام ولم أر حكم التقاطه لقيطاً أو لقطة. وفي غاية البيان من باب الاستيلاد الجد إذا وطئ جارية ابن ابنه والأب مرتد فادعاه الجد بعد الولادة لم تصح دعوى الجد عندهما، وعند أبي حنيفة موقوفة، فإن أسلم الأب لم تصح دعوى الجد، وإن مات على الردة أو لحق بدار الحرب وحكم بلحاقه تصح اهـ. وهذه لا ترد على ما في الكتاب لأنها تصرف المسلم وهو الجد لا تصرف المرتد. وقيد بالمرتد لأن تصرفات المرتدة نافذة عند الكل لأنها لا تقتل وقد قدمناه مع بيان تصرفات المكاتب المرتد. وأطلق الهلاك فشمل الحقيقي بالموت أو القتل والحكمي بالقضاء بلحاقه بدار الحرب كما في الخانية. وعبر بالإيمان في قوله فإن آمن وأراد الإسلام فإنه المراد هنا كما عبر به في الهداية والخانية فإنه الانقياد الظاهر الذي تبتنى عليه الأحكام. نظمها العلامة المقدسي في شرحه فقال : عبده وباتفاق صح دعوى ولده كذا طلاقه وحجر وهكذا قبوله لهبته وهكذا تسليمه لشفعته وباطل بالاتقان نکحه وهكذا میراثه وذبحه وأوقفوا مفاوضات شــرکـتـه تصريفه لطفله وطفلته انتهى. ولعله سقط بيت إذ لم يستوف الباطل بأقسامه الخمسة وقد غيرت بيته الثالث فقلت : وباطل نكاحه شهادته وصيده وإرثه ذبيحته
As for what is considered equality of possession or transitive guardianship, it is not effective by agreement. First: partnership (mufawadah) – if he enters into mufawadah with a Muslim, it is suspended by agreement; if he becomes Muslim it is effective; if he perishes (dies or is killed) it is void and becomes a limited partnership (inan). According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is effective; according to Abu Hanifa, it becomes void. Second: transactions regarding his minor child and his child's property are suspended by agreement. So it appears that his transactions are of four types. I have not seen a ruling on his picking up a foundling or a lost item. In Ghayat al-Bayan, from the chapter on istilad: If a grandfather has intercourse with the slave-girl of his son's son while the father is an apostate, and the grandfather claims the child after birth, the grandfather's claim is not valid according to the two, but according to Abu Hanifa it is suspended. If the father becomes Muslim, the grandfather's claim is not valid; if he dies in apostasy or joins the dar al-harb and is judged to have joined, his claim is valid. This does not contradict the book because it is the transaction of a Muslim (the grandfather), not the apostate. Restricted to the male apostate because the transactions of the female apostate are valid by all, as she is not killed. We have already mentioned this with the discussion of the transactions of a mukatib who apostatizes. 'Perishing' is general, including real death or killing, and legal perishing by judgment of joining dar al-harb. He expressed with 'iman' (faith) in his saying 'if he believes and desires Islam' because that is what is meant here, as expressed in al-Hidayah and al-Khaniyah, i.e., outward submission upon which rulings are based. Al-Maqdisi organized it in his commentary: His slave, and by agreement his claim of his child is valid; likewise his divorce and sequestration; thus acceptance of his gift; thus his delivery of his pre-emption; and invalid by agreement: his marriage, his inheritance, his slaughter; and they suspended his mufawadah partnership, his transactions regarding his child. He said: Perhaps a line is missing as he did not cover the invalid types in their five divisions. I changed his third line and said: Invalid are his marriage, his testimony, his hunting, his inheritance, his slaughtered animal.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq classifies the transactions of an apostate (male) into four categories based on whether they involve equality of possession or transitive guardianship. The first category is mufawadah partnership: if he partners with a Muslim, the partnership is suspended. If he returns to Islam, it becomes valid; if he dies or is legally considered dead, it becomes a limited partnership (inan) according to the two, but void according to Abu Hanifa. The second category is his transactions regarding his minor child: these are suspended by agreement. The commentator notes that the apostate's transactions fall into four types, but he also mentions he has not seen a ruling on foundlings. He then discusses a case from Ghayat al-Bayan about a grandfather claiming a child from the apostate's slave-girl, showing how ownership is transferred. The passage ends with a poem by al-Maqdisi summarizing the four categories, with the commentator suggesting a missing line and offering his own correction.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): A male apostate who has left Islam.
مفاوضة (mufawadah): An equal partnership where partners have full agency.
عنان (inan): A limited partnership where partners contribute capital and share profits but not necessarily equal in agency.
تصرف (tasarruf): A legal transaction or disposition of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the classification, what happens to an apostate's mufawadah partnership with a Muslim if the apostate returns to Islam?
A) It becomes void immediately.
B) It is suspended; if he returns to Islam it becomes valid.
C) It remains valid regardless.
D) It becomes a limited partnership (inan) automatically.

Key Takeaway
The apostate's transactions are classified into four categories: valid, suspended, invalid, and subject to dispute, with the mufawadah partnership being the key case of ikhtilaf.

The Returning Apostate: Reclaiming Property from Heirs
Ruling on property rights after apostate's return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi ruling on the returning apostate's claim to property held by his heirs, and IDENTIFY the relied-upon position requiring a court order or consent.

Core Idea
A returning apostate reclaims only existing property from heirs, and may need a court order — ownership once vested does not revert automatically.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the ruling of an apostate who returns to Islam after the judge has declared him legally dead (lahaq). The key question is whether he can simply take back his property from his heirs or whether a court order is needed. We will examine the main ruling, the exceptions, and the intra-madhhab tarjih that settles the operative position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the apostate returning after the judge's decree of lahaq. The ruling: he takes what he finds in the hand of his heir; otherwise not.
This is the core rule stated in the matn.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is rational (qiyas): analogy with a resurrected dead person who would reclaim property. Also, the principle that a valid legal judgment (inheritance) cannot be undone without due process.
The dalil is aqli, not naqli.
3
Trace the ikhtilaf
The apparent wording suggests the apostate can take without court order. However, later scholars (al-Zaylai, al-Tatar Khaniyah) hold that a court order or heir's consent is required because the property entered the heir's ownership by a valid legal ruling.
This is a classic example of tarjih between apparent meaning and relied-upon position.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the apostate returns, the heir's transactions between return and court order are valid (since heir still owns). In the opposing view, those transactions would be void.
This has significant implications for third parties who bought property from the heir.
5
Apply to subcases
The passage applies the ruling to specific cases: mudabbars (remain free), kitaba (annullable only if unpaid), etc. These show the scope of what can and cannot be reclaimed.
Each subcase illustrates the principle that irrevocable transfers stand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن عاد مسلماً بعد الحكم بلحاقه فما وجده في يد وارثه أخذه وإلا لا) أي وإن لم يجده قائماً في يده فليس له أخذ بدله منه لأن الوارث إنما يخلفه فيه لاستغنائه، وإذا عاد مسلماً يحتاج إليه فيقدم عليه. وعلى هذا لو أحيا الله ميتاً حقيقة وأعاده إلى دار الدنيا كان له أخذ ما في يد ورثته، وأطلق في قوله وإلا لا فشمل ما إذا كان هالكاً أو أزاله الوارث ملکه عن وهو قائم، سواء كان بسبب يقبل الفسخ كبيع أو هبة أو لا يقبله كعتق وتدبير واستيلاد فإنه يمضي ولا عود له فيه ولا يضمنه. وشمل ما لم يدخل في يد وارثه أصلاً كمدبريه وأمهات أولاده المحكوم بعتقهم بسبب الحكم بلحاقه فإنهم لا يعودون في الرق لأن القضاء بعتقهم قد صح بدليل مصحح له والعتق بعد نفاذه لا يقبل البطلان وولاؤهم لمولاهم أعني المرتد الذي عاد مسلماً، وكذلك مكاتبه إذا كان أدى المال إلى الورثة لا سبيل عليه أيضاً لأنه عتق بأداء المال والعتق لا يحتمل الفسخ وما أدى إلى الورثة إن كان قائماً أخذه، وإن زال ملكهم عنه لا ضمان عليه كسائر أمواله، وإن كان لم يؤد بدل الكتابة يأخذها منه، وإن عجز عاد رقيقاً له ؛ كذا في البدائع. وفي الخانية : إذا عاد مسلماً بعد الحكم بحل ديونه وعتق مدبريه وأم ولده لا يملك أن يبطل شيئاً إلا شيئان: الأول الميراث يبطله ويسترد ماله إن كان قائماً، والثاني إذا كاتب ورثته عبداً من ماله ثم رجع فإن رجع بعدما أدى بدل الكتابة لا يملك إبطالها، فإن رجع قبل أن يؤدي جميع بدل الكتابة كان له أن يبطل الكتابة اهـ. وظاهر الكتاب أنه يأخذ ما في يد الوارث بغير قضاء ولا رضا والمنقول خلافه. قال في التتارخانية : وما كان قائماً في يد الورثة إنما يعود إلى ملكه بقضاء أو رضا فإنه ذكر في السير الكبير أن وارث المرتد إذا تصرف في المال الذي ورثه بعد ما عاد المرتد مسلماً نفذ تصرفه اهـ. وجزم به الزيلعي معللاً بأنه دخل في ملكه بحكم شرعي فلا يخرج عن ملكه إلا بطريقه اهـ. وقد يقال طريقه عوده مسلماً فإن الحكم الشرعي الموجب للدخول الحكم بخلافته عنه بعد موته حكماً وقد بطلت فبطل ما ابتنى عليه وقد قدمنا عن التتارخانية أن كسب ردته فيء بعد الحكم بلحاقه كموته حقيقة لكن لم أر حكم ما إذا عاد مسلماً ووجد كسب ردته قائماً عند الإمام فهل يسترده كما يسترد من وارثه كسب إسلامه؟ الظاهر أنه لا يسترده لأن أخذه ليس بطريق قوله: (ولم أر حكم التقاطه لقيطاً) أو لقطة. قال في النهر : وبقي إيداعه واستيداعه وأمانه وعقله، ولا شك في عدم صحة أمانه إذ أمان الذمي لا يصح فهذا أولى ، وكذا عقله لأن التناصر لا يكون بالمرتد ... وأما التقاطه ولقطته وإبداعه واستيداعه فلا ينبغي التردد في جوازها منه . قوله : (والثاني إذا كاتب الخ سيأتي ما يخالفه كما ينبه عليه قوله : (وقد يقال طريقه عوده مسلماً) قال في النهر : ممنوع اهـ. قوله : (فحكمه كما إذا لم يرتد ليس على إطلاقه لأنه لا ينفذ ما تصرف فيه في ماله بنفسه بعد اللحاقه ففي شرح السير الكبير : ولو لحق فلم يقض بلحاقه حتى أعتق عبده الذي في دار الإسلام أو باعه من مسلم كان معه . في دار الحرب ثم رجع تائباً قبل القضاء بلحاقه فماله عليه كله وجميع ما صنع فيه باطل لأنه باللحاق زال ملكه، وإنما توقف على القضاء دخوله في ملك مردود
His statement: (And if he returns as a Muslim after the judgment of his joining, then whatever he finds in the hand of his heir he takes, otherwise not). That is, if he does not find it existing in his hand, he has no right to take its substitute from him because the heir only takes his place due to the apostate's own abandonment, and when he returns as a Muslim, he needs it so he takes precedence. Based on this, if God had truly brought a dead person back to life and returned him to this world, he would take what is in the hands of his heirs. He made it absolute in his saying 'otherwise not', so it includes whether the property is lost or the heir has transferred ownership of it while it was existing, whether by a transaction that can be annulled like sale or gift, or one that cannot like manumission, tadbir, or istilad – these are effective and cannot be reversed, nor is the heir liable. It also includes things that never came into the heir's hand at all, like his mudabbars and umm walads who are judged free due to the judgment of his joining – they do not return to slavery because the judgment of their freedom is valid, and manumission once effective cannot be invalidated, and their wala belongs to their former master, meaning the apostate who returned as a Muslim. Similarly, his mukatib if he has paid the kitaba money to the heirs, he cannot be touched because he is free by payment, and manumission does not admit rescission. If the payment is still in the heirs' hands and existing, he takes it; if their ownership has ceased, no liability on them like other property. If he has not paid the kitaba, he takes the amount, and if he becomes unable, the slave returns to his ownership. In al-Bada'i. In al-Khaniyah: If he returns as a Muslim after the judgment, his debts are dissolved, his mudabbars and umm walad become free; he cannot annul anything except two things: first, the inheritance – he annuls it and recovers his property if it is still in existence; second, if his heirs have made a kitaba contract with a slave from his property and then he returns, if he returns after the slave has paid the kitaba money, he cannot annul the kitaba; if he returns before paying all of it, he may annul the kitaba. The apparent meaning of the book is that he takes what is in the heir's hand without court order or consent, but the transmitted position is contrary. In al-Tatar Khaniyah: What is existing in the hands of the heirs only returns to his ownership by court order or consent. It is stated in al-Siyar al-Kabir that if the heir of the apostate transacts with the property after the apostate has returned as a Muslim, his transaction is valid. Al-Zayla'i affirmed this, reasoning that it entered his ownership by a legal ruling and cannot leave his ownership except by its proper channel. It could be said that its proper channel is his return as a Muslim, because the legal ruling requiring entry was the ruling that he is a successor after his legal death, and that ruling has been annulled, so what was built upon it is annulled. We have already transmitted from al-Tatar Khaniyah that his earnings from apostasy are booty after the judgment of joining, like his real death. But I have not seen a ruling on if he returns as a Muslim and finds his apostasy earnings existing with the imam – does he recover them as he recovers from his heir his Islamic earnings? Apparently, he does not recover them because his taking is not by way of... His statement: (And I have not seen a ruling on his picking up a foundling or lost item). He said in al-Nahr: There remain his deposits, his safekeeping, his aman, and his 'aql. There is no doubt that his aman is invalid, as the aman of a dhimmi is invalid, so this is more so. Similarly, his 'aql, because mutual support does not come from an apostate. As for his picking up a foundling or lost item, and his deposits and safekeeping, there should be no hesitation in their permissibility. His statement: (And the second is if he made a kitaba... it will come later that contradicts it, as pointed out by his statement: (And it could be said his way is his return as a Muslim). He said in al-Nahr: That is prevented. His statement: (So his ruling is as if he did not apostasize is not absolute, because what he transacted in his property by himself after his joining is not effective. In Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir: If he joined and was not judged to have joined until he freed his slave in Dar al-Islam or sold him to a Muslim with him in Dar al-Harb, then he repents before the judgment of joining, his property is all upon him and all that he did is void, because by joining his ownership ceased, and his entry into ownership was only suspended upon the judgment. [The text ends incomplete])
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the ruling that if an apostate returns to Islam after a judge has ruled that he has joined the dar al-harb (legally considered dead), he may reclaim any property that remains in the physical possession of his heirs. If the property is no longer in their hands – whether lost, consumed, or transferred via a valid contract like manumission or sale – he cannot reclaim it or demand compensation. The commentary extends this to mudabbars and umm walads (already freed), and mukatibs who have paid their kitaba. The commentator notes a difference of opinion: the apparent wording suggests he can reclaim without a court order, but the relied-upon position (from al-Tatar Khaniyah and al-Zaylai) is that he needs a court order or the heir's consent. The passage also discusses the case of an apostate who returns before the judgment, in which case his transactions are void because his ownership had not yet passed to the heir. The commentator also mentions that he has not seen a ruling on an apostate's picking up a foundling or lost property, but cites al-Nahr that it is permissible.

Key Terms
لحاق (lahaq): The apostate's joining of dar al-harb, which triggers legal death rulings after a judge's decree.
ميراث (mirath): Inheritance that passed to the apostate's heirs after the judgment of lahaq.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract of manumission between master and slave, where the slave pays installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a male apostate returns to Islam after the judge has ruled his joining (lahaq), what happens to his mudabbars (slaves promised freedom after death) that were already freed by the judgment?
A) They become slaves again because the judgment is voided.
B) They remain free, and their wala goes to the returning apostate.
C) They remain free, but wala goes to the state.
D) They remain in limbo until a new judgment.

Key Takeaway
The returning apostate's right to reclaim property is limited to existing assets and may require a court order; property that has been validly transferred or freed cannot be reversed.

Apostate's Property Upon Return: Reclamation and Limitations
Commentary on ruling of apostate reclaiming property from heirs after reversion to Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on an apostate's property after return to Islam, distinguishing between reclaimable and non-reclaimable assets and identifying when qada or rida is required.

Core Idea
An apostate's return does not automatically restore all property; manumissions are irrevocable and earnings of apostasy become fay'.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq (a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq) delves into the complex rules governing an apostate (murtadd) who returns to Islam. While the base text suggests automatic reclamation, the commentary reveals crucial distinctions: certain acts like manumission cannot be undone, and a difference exists between the apparent meaning and the relied-upon view requiring a judge's ruling (qada) or the heir's consent (rida). We will navigate these distinctions to understand what an apostate can and cannot recover.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main rule and its exceptions
Start by noting the commentator's opening analogy: if a dead person were revived, he would take what is in his heirs' hands. Then note the exceptions: the apostate cannot reclaim slaves freed by manumission (ʿitq), tadbīr, or istīlād, nor revert the status of mudabbars and umm walad who were freed by the judge's ruling of ilḥāq.
The key principle: manumission is irrevocable, even if the basis of the heir's ownership is later invalidated.
2
Analyze the mukātab slave
Examine the three scenarios for a mukātab: (1) if he paid the kitāba amount to the heirs and the payment is still extant, the apostate takes it; (2) if the payment is consumed, no liability; (3) if not yet fully paid, the apostate may annul the kitāba and reclaim the slave if he becomes unable to pay.
The kitāba is a binding contract; payment effects freedom, but an unpaid kitāba can be rescinded by the returning apostate.
3
Differentiate between rescissible and non-rescissible transfers
The passage distinguishes between transfers that can be rescinded (like sale or gift) and those that cannot (like manumission). For rescissible transfers, if the property is still extant, the apostate can reclaim it; if the heir sold it, the apostate has no recourse. For non-rescissible transfers, the slave remains free and the apostate cannot reclaim his value.
This is based on the nature of the act: manumission is a pure act of worship that cannot be undone, while sale is a contract that can be invalidated.
4
Contrast the apparent matn with the transmitted view
The apparent meaning of Kanz is that the apostate takes his property from heirs without qada or rida. However, the transmitted view from al-TatarKhāniyya and al-Zaylaʿī requires qada (judgment) or rida (consent) of the heirs. The commentator leans towards the transmitted view, supported by the reasoning that the property entered the heir's ownership by a valid legal ruling (the judgment of ilḥāq), so it can only be undone through a similar process.
This is an intra-Hanafī dispute; the muftā bihī is the view requiring qada or rida.
5
Understand the ruling on kasb al-ridda (earnings of apostasy)
The earnings the apostate acquired during his apostasy (before ilḥāq) become fay' (booty for the treasury) after the judgment of ilḥāq, and even if he returns to Islam, he cannot reclaim them because they are not taken by way of inheritance (khilāfa) but as spoils. This is analogous to a harbī who embraces Islam and does not reclaim his former property.
This contrasts with his Islamic earnings (kasb al-Islām), which he could reclaim from an heir after return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عاد مسلماً يحتاج إليه فيقدم عليه. وعلى هذا لو أحيا الله ميتاً حقيقة وأعاده إلى دار الدنيا كان له أخذ ما في يد ورثته، وأطلق في قوله وإلا لا فشمل ما إذا كان هالكاً أو أزاله الوارث ملکه عن وهو قائم، سواء كان بسبب يقبل الفسخ كبيع أو هبة أو لا يقبله كعتق وتدبير واستيلاد فإنه يمضي ولا عود له فيه ولا يضمنه. وشمل ما لم يدخل في يد وارثه أصلاً كمدبريه وأمهات أولاده المحكوم بعتقهم بسبب الحكم بلحاقه فإنهم لا يعودون في الرق لأن القضاء بعتقهم قد صح بدليل مصحح له والعتق بعد نفاذه لا يقبل البطلان وولاؤهم لمولاهم أعني المرتد الذي عاد مسلماً، وكذلك مكاتبه إذا كان أدى المال إلى الورثة لا سبيل عليه أيضاً لأنه عتق بأداء المال والعتق لا يحتمل الفسخ وما أدى إلى الورثة إن كان قائماً أخذه، وإن زال ملكهم عنه لا ضمان عليه كسائر أمواله، وإن كان لم يؤد بدل الكتابة يأخذها منه، وإن عجز عاد رقيقاً له؛ كذا في البدائع. وفي الخانية: إذا عاد مسلماً بعد الحكم بحل ديونه وعتق مدبريه وأم ولده لا يملك أن يبطل شيئاً إلا شيئان: الأول الميراث يبطله ويسترد ماله إن كان قائماً، والثاني إذا كاتب ورثته عبداً من ماله ثم رجع فإن رجع بعدما أدى بدل الكتابة لا يملك إبطالها، فإن رجع قبل أن يؤدي جميع بدل الكتابة كان له أن يبطل الكتابة اهـ. وظاهر الكتاب أنه يأخذ ما في يد الوارث بغير قضاء ولا رضا والمنقول خلافه. قال في التتارخانية: وما كان قائماً في يد الورثة إنما يعود إلى ملكه بقضاء أو رضا فإنه ذكر في السير الكبير أن وارث المرتد إذا تصرف في المال الذي ورثه بعد ما عاد المرتد مسلماً نفذ تصرفه اهـ. وجزم به الزيلعي معللاً بأنه دخل في ملكه بحكم شرعي فلا يخرج عن ملكه إلا بطريقه اهـ. وقد يقال طريقه عوده مسلماً فإن الحكم الشرعي الموجب للدخول الحكم بخلافته عنه بعد موته حكماً وقد بطلت فبطل ما ابتنى عليه وقد قدمنا عن التتارخانية أن كسب ردته فيء بعد الحكم بلحاقه كموته حقيقة لكن لم أر حكم ما إذا عاد مسلماً ووجد كسب ردته قائماً عند الإمام فهل يسترده كما يسترد من وارثه كسب إسلامه؟ الظاهر أنه لا يسترده لأن أخذه ليس بطريق الخلافة بل لكونه مالاً كما قدمناه فصار لبيت المال فلا يسترده كما أن الحربي الحقيقي لا يسترد ماله بعد إسلامه. وقيد بقوله بعد الحكم بلحاقه لأنه لو عاد مسلماً قبله فحكمه كما إذا لم يرتد فلا يعتق مدبره وأم ولده ولا تحل ديونه وله إبطال ما تصرف فيه الوارث لكونه فضولياً.

قوله: (والثاني إذا كاتب الخ سيأتي ما يخالفه كما ينبه عليه قوله: (وقد يقال طريقه عوده مسلماً) قال في النهر: ممنوع اهـ. قوله: (فحكمه كما إذا لم يرتد ليس على إطلاقه لأنه لا ينفذ ما تصرف فيه في ماله بنفسه بعد الحاقه ففي شرح السير الكبير: ولو لحق فلم يقض بلحاقه حتى أعتق عبده الذي في دار الإسلام أو باعه من مسلم كان معه في دار الحرب ثم رجع تائباً قبل القضاء بلحاقه فماله عليه كله وجميع ما صنع فيه باطل لأنه باللحاق زال ملكه، وإنما توقف على القضاء دخوله في ملك. مردود
If he returns as a Muslim, he needs it so he takes precedence over it. Based on this, if Allah truly brings a dead person to life and returns him to the world, he would be entitled to take what is in the hands of his heirs. And he (the author) spoke generally in his statement 'and if not, no', so it includes whether it is destroyed or the heir removed his ownership while it was existing, whether due to something that accepts rescission like sale or gift or does not accept it like manumission, tadbir, and istilad; indeed it is effective and there is no return for him in it and he is not liable for compensation. And it includes what never entered the hand of the heir at all, like his mudabbars and ummahat awlad who are judged to be freed due to the judgment of his joining (dar al-harb); they do not return to slavery because the judgment of their emancipation was valid by a proof that validates it, and manumission after its execution does not accept annulment, and their wala goes to their master, meaning the apostate who returned as a Muslim. Likewise, his mukatab, if he paid the money to the heirs, there is no way against him either because he became free by payment of money and manumission does not bear rescission; what he paid to the heirs, if it is still extant, he (the apostate) takes it; if their ownership of it is removed, there is no liability upon him like his other property. And if he had not paid the kitaba amount, he takes it from him, and if he becomes incapable, he returns to being his slave; thus in al-Bada'i. And in al-Khaniya: If he returns as a Muslim after the judgment of dissolution of his debts and emancipation of his mudabbars and umm walad, he has no power to annul anything except two things: first, the inheritance, he annuls it and reclaims his property if it is extant; second, if his heirs wrote a kitaba for a slave from his property, then he returns, if he returns after payment of the kitaba amount, he cannot annul it; if he returns before paying the entire kitaba amount, he has the right to annul the kitaba. The apparent meaning of the book is that he takes what is in the hand of the heir without judgment or consent, but the transmitted view is contrary. He said in al-TatarKhaniya: What is extant in the hands of the heirs only returns to his ownership by judgment or consent, for it is mentioned in al-Siyar al-Kabir that the heir of an apostate, if he disposes of the property he inherited after the apostate returns as a Muslim, his disposition is effective. Al-Zayla'i affirmed it, reasoning that it entered his ownership by a legal ruling, so it does not leave his ownership except by its method. It may be said that its method is his returning as a Muslim, for the legal ruling that necessitated the entry is the judgment of his being different from him after his death legally, and it has become void, so what was built upon it becomes void. And we have previously transmitted from al-TatarKhaniya that the earnings of his apostasy are fay' after the judgment of his joining, like his death in reality, but I have not seen the ruling if he returns as a Muslim and finds the earnings of his apostasy extant with the Imam: does he reclaim them as he reclaims from his heir the earnings of his Islam? The apparent view is that he does not reclaim them because his taking is not by way of khilafa but because it is property as we have mentioned, so it becomes for bayt al-mal, so he does not reclaim it, just as a true harbi does not reclaim his property after his Islam. And he restricted it by his statement 'after the judgment of his joining' because if he returns as a Muslim before it, his ruling is as if he did not apostatize, so his mudabbar and umm walad are not freed, his debts are not dissolved, and he has the right to annul what the heir disposed of because it is a disposition of a fuduli.

His statement: 'And the second: if he wrote a kitaba...' will come what contradicts it, as indicated by his statement: 'And it may be said his method is his returning as a Muslim.' He said in al-Nahr: It is denied. His statement: 'His ruling is as if he did not apostatize is not absolute because what he disposed of in his property by himself after his joining is not effective. In Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir: If he joined and the judge did not judge his joining until he freed his slave who was in Dar al-Islam or sold him to a Muslim who was with him in Dar al-Harb, then he repented before the judgment of his joining, then all his property is upon him and everything he did in it is void because by joining his ownership ceased; only the entry into his ownership depended on the judgment. Rejected.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq dealing with the property of an apostate who returns to Islam. The commentator, Imam Zayn al-Din ibn Nujaym, explains the ruling in detail. The opening statement establishes that if an apostate returns to Islam, he has priority in reclaiming his property from heirs, similar to a dead person brought back to life. The commentary then distinguishes cases: whether the property is destroyed, whether the heir transferred ownership via a rescissible contract (like sale) or non-rescissible (like manumission). Manumission and tadbir are effective and cannot be undone; the apostate cannot recover the slave nor claim compensation. The wala of freed slaves goes to the apostate master. For a mukatab slave, if he paid the kitaba to the heirs, the payment is effective and the slave is free; if the payment is still extant the apostate takes it, but if consumed there is no liability. If the kitaba was not fully paid, the apostate may annul the kitaba. The commentary then notes a difference: the apparent ruling of the book (Kanz) is that the apostate takes without qada (judgment) or rida (consent), but the transmitted view requires qada or rida for property in the hands of heirs. The commentator cites al-TatarKhaniya and al-Zayla'i supporting the latter, reasoning that the property entered the heir's ownership by a valid legal ruling. An objection is raised: the method of exit is the apostate's return, which voids the basis of the ownership. The commentator then discusses the earnings of apostasy (kasb al-ridda) after the judgment of joining dar al-harb: they become fay' for bayt al-mal, and the apostate cannot reclaim them even if he returns, unlike the earnings of Islam which he could reclaim from an heir. This is because the earnings of apostasy are not taken by way of khilafa but as property for the treasury, analogous to a harbi who does not reclaim his property after embracing Islam. The restriction 'after the judgment of his joining' is crucial: before judgment, the apostate's return means he is as if he never apostatized; all heir dispositions are voidable as fuduli acts. The commentary then addresses the second issue concerning kitaba, noting a cross-reference and rejecting a possible interpretation. The final portion from Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir illustrates: if the apostate joins dar al-harb but no judgment is passed, and he frees or sells a slave in Dar al-Islam before repenting, his acts are void because ownership ceased upon joining, pending judgment. The marginal note 'mardud' indicates the rejection of a contrary view.

Key Terms
المرتد (al-murtadd): A Muslim who abandons Islam by word or deed.
الكتابة (al-kitaba): A contract of manumission by which a slave pays his owner in installments.
الحكم بلحاقه (al-hukmu bi-lhaqihi): The judge's ruling that the apostate has joined dar al-harb (enemy territory), treating him as dead.
كسب الردة (kasb al-ridda): Earnings acquired by the apostate during his apostasy, after the judgment of ilhaq.
الفيء (al-fay'): Spoils or property that goes to the Muslim treasury without battle, here applied to the apostate's earnings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the text, what does an apostate need to reclaim property that is still extant in the hands of his heirs after he returns to Islam?
A) Nothing, he takes it automatically.
B) A judgment (qada) from a qadi or the consent (rida) of the heirs.
C) He must repay the value to the heirs.
D) He cannot reclaim it because it belongs to bayt al-mal.

Key Takeaway
An apostate returning to Islam can reclaim property from heirs only if it is extant, the transfer was rescissible, and he obtains qada or rida; manumissions are irrevocable, and earnings of apostasy become fay'.

Apostate's Custodial Acts: Valid, but Aman is Not
Commentary on validity of apostate's acts: custodial vs. trust-based

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid legal acts performed by an apostate by applying the distinction between acts of custodianship and acts requiring trust.

Core Idea
An apostate may pick up a foundling or deposit, but cannot give aman.

The matn notes that no explicit ruling was found on an apostate picking up a foundling. The commentator clarifies: acts like granting safe conduct (aman) and blood money agreements (aql) are invalid because they imply mutual trust and support, which an apostate lacks. However, picking up a foundling, lost property, and handling deposits are purely custodial and thus permissible without hesitation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Acts
Read the passage and list all acts mentioned: picking up a foundling, lost property, deposit, receiving deposit, giving aman, and aql.
2
Categorize by Validity
The text explicitly states that aman and aql are invalid ('no doubt in invalidity'), while the rest are permissible ('no hesitation in their permissibility').
Invalid acts involve trust; permissible acts are custodial.
3
Understand the Reasoning
Aman and aql require mutual support and trustworthiness. An apostate lacks 'isma (inviolability), so he cannot provide reliable aman. Custodial acts like picking up a foundling only require physical care, not trust.
The comparison with dhimmi strengthens the reasoning: if a dhimmi's aman is invalid, an apostate's is even more so.
4
Apply the Principle
In practice, if an apostate finds an abandoned child, he may rescue and care for it. But he cannot grant safe conduct to an enemy combatant.
The ruling applies to any trust-based contract, e.g., guaranteeing debts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولم أر حكم التقاطه لقيطاً) أو لقطة. قال في النهر: وبقي إيداعه واستيداعه وأمانه وعقله، ولا شك في عدم صحة أمانه إذ أمان الذمي لا يصح فهذا أولى، وكذا عقله لأن التناصر لا يكون بالمرتد... وأما التقاطه ولقطته وإبداعه واستيداعه فلا ينبغي التردد في جوازها منه.
His statement: 'I have not seen the ruling of his picking up a foundling.' Or lost property. He said in al-Nahr: And his deposit, receiving deposit, giving aman, and aql remain; there is no doubt in the invalidity of his aman because the aman of a dhimmi is invalid, so this is more appropriate, and likewise his aql because mutual support is not done by an apostate. But his picking up of a foundling and lost property and deposit and receiving deposit, there should be no hesitation in their permissibility from him.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage comments on a matn phrase where the original author (presumably al-Nasafi) states that he has not seen a ruling on the apostate picking up a foundling. The commentator explains the view of al-Nahr: the apostate's aman (guarantee of safe conduct) is invalid, as is his 'aql (blood money agreement?), because these involve mutual support which is not valid from an apostate. However, his acts of picking up a foundling (laqit) or lost property (luqta), and giving or receiving deposits, are permissible from him without hesitation. The reasoning is that these are acts of custodianship that do not require the same level of trustworthiness as aman.

Key Terms
اللقيط (al-laqit): An abandoned child found by someone; the finder takes custody.
الأمان (al-aman): A guarantee of safety granted to a non-Muslim enemy.
اللقطة (al-luqta): Lost property picked up by someone for safekeeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following acts of an apostate is NOT valid?
A) Picking up a foundling
B) Accepting a deposit
C) Giving aman (safe conduct)
D) Picking up lost property

Key Takeaway
An apostate retains capacity for physical custodianship (foundling, deposit) but lacks legal capacity for acts requiring trust (aman, aql).

Apostate's Property Upon Joining Dar al-Harb: Fay' or Heir's Right?
Ruling on apostate's property with intra-madhhab nuance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an apostate's property when he joins dar al-harb, explain the condition for it becoming fay', and distinguish the relied-upon position regarding his return.

Core Idea
An apostate's property is fay' upon joining dar al-harb; if he returns, it reverts to his heirs.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh concerning an apostate who flees to enemy territory with his possessions. The primary ruling classifies his property as fay' (booty) placed in the public treasury. However, a nuance arises if the apostate later returns and is defeated: according to the preferred view, the property goes to his original heirs. We will trace the evidence and the intra-madhhab difference on this point.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an apostate joining dar al-harb with his property. The ruling is that his property becomes fay' (booty) by consensus.
The author states 'بالإجماع' (by consensus) for this initial ruling.
2
Locate the proof
The proof cited is ijma (consensus). How does this consensus operate? It establishes that the property loses its inviolability and becomes public treasury.
The inviolability of property follows the inviolability of the person, which is lost by apostasy.
3
Identify the intra-madhhab dispute
Some narrations from al-Siyar hold that the property remains fay' even after the apostate's return. The preferred position (mufta bihi) is that it goes to the heirs.
The author follows the view that the property goes to heirs, supported by Fath al-Qadir.
4
Trace the reasoning (talil)
The judge's adjudication of the apostate's joining transfers ownership to heirs prior to his return. His return and recapture confirm that ownership. The opposing view sees the property as already fay' and unaffected by return.
The return is treated as equivalent to adjudication by legal presumption.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi is that upon return and defeat, the property belongs to the heirs, as per al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and Fath al-Qadir.
This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإن لحق المرتد بماله فظهر عليه فهو فيء) أي ماله غنيمة يوضع في بيت المال بالإجماع لا لورثته لسقوط عصمة ماله تبعاً لعصمة نفسه. وقيد بالمال لأن المرتد بعد الظهور لا يسترق وإنما يقتل إن لم يسلم، ولا يشكل كون ماله فيئاً دون نفسه لأن مشركي العرب كذلك. وفي المغرب: ظهر عليه غلب، وظهر على اللص غلب وهو من قولهم ظهر فلان السطح إذا علاه وحقيقته صار على ظهره اهـ. فعلى هذا ظهر في كلام المصنف بالبناء للمفعول قوله : (فإن رجع وذهب بماله وظهر عليه فلوارثه لأنه انتقل إليهم بقضاء القاضي بلحاقه فكان الوارث مالكاً قديماً. وحكمه أنه إن وجده قبل القسمة أخذه بغير بدل، وإن وجده بعدها أخذه بقيمته إن شاء، وإن كان مثلياً فقد تقدم أنه لا يؤخذ لعدم الفائدة؛ كذا في فتح القدير. والمثلي وارد على أن في عبارته إيهام أن يأخذه بغير شيء مطلقاً، ولم يقيد المصنف أن يكون مع رجوعه بعد الحكم بلحاقه تبعاً للجامع الصغير فأفاد أنه لا فرق بين أن يكون بعده أو قبله، أما إذا كان بعده فظاهر لتقرر الملك للوارث بالقضاء بلحاقه، وأما قبله فلأن عوده وأخذه ولحاقه ثانياً. جانب عدم العود ويؤكده فيتقرر ،موته وما احتيج للقضاء باللحاق يرجح لصيرورته ميراثاً إلا ليترجح عدم عوده فيتقرر إقامته ثمة فيتقرر ،موته، فكان رجوعه ثم عوده ثانياً بمنزلة القضاء. وفي بعض روايات السير جعله فيئاً لأن المصنف بمجرد اللحاق لا المال يصير ورثته فتصرفه بعد اللحاق صادف مالاً غير مملوك له فلا ينفذ وإن عاد إلى ملكه بعد كبالبائع بشرط خيار المشتري إذا تصرف في المبيع ثم عاد إلى ملكه بفسخ المشتري لم ينفذ تصرفه، ولو أقر بحرية عبده أو بأنه لفلان جاز إذا عاد مسلماً لأنه ليس بإنشاء التصرف بل هو إقرار والإقرار لازم في حق
His saying: (And if the apostate joins [dar al-harb] with his property and is defeated, then it is fay') i.e., his property is booty placed in the public treasury by consensus, not for his heirs, because the inviolability of his property falls away following the inviolability of his person. And he restricted it to property because the apostate after being defeated is not enslaved but is killed if he does not accept Islam. And it is not problematic that his property is fay' while he himself is not, because the Arab polytheists were treated likewise. And in al-Mughrib: 'After upon him' means overcome, and 'after upon the thief' means overcome, from their saying 'he after the roof' meaning he ascended it, its reality: he became on its back. So accordingly, 'after' in the speech of the author is in the passive voice. His saying: (And if he returns and goes with his property and is defeated, then it is for his heir because it had transferred to them by the judge's adjudication of his joining, so the heir was an old owner. Its ruling: if he finds it before division, he takes it without compensation; if he finds it after division, he takes it by its value if he wishes. If it is fungible (mithli), it has been stated that it is not taken because there is no benefit; like that in Fath al-Qadir. And the mention of mithli comes because in his expression there is an indication that he takes it without anything absolutely, and the author did not restrict that his return be after the adjudication of his joining, following al-Jami' al-Saghir, so it indicates that there is no difference between whether it is after or before. As for after, it is apparent because of the establishment of ownership for the heir by the adjudication of his joining. As for before, because his return, taking, and second joining — the possibility of not returning is confirmed, and if his death and the need for adjudication of joining are needed, the return to inheritance is weighted except to outweigh the possibility of his non-return, so his residence there is established, so his death is established. So his return and then second joining is equivalent to adjudication. And in some riwayat of al-Siyar, it is made fay' because the author [holds] that by mere joining the property does not become [for] his heirs, so his disposition after joining met property not owned by him, so it is not effective even if it returns to his ownership later, like a seller with option of the buyer who disposes of the sold item then it returns to his ownership by the buyer's revocation, his disposition is not effective. And if he acknowledges the freedom of his slave or that it belongs to so-and-so, it is valid if he returns Muslim, because it is not an originating disposition but an acknowledgment, and acknowledgment is binding in his right.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage addresses the fate of an apostate's property when he flees to dar al-harb. The standard Hanafi position (as per the text) is that once the apostate is adjudged to have joined dar al-harb, his property is considered spoils (fay') for the public treasury, not for his heirs, because his inviolability is lost. However, if the apostate returns (even after adjudication) and then his property is captured again by Muslims, it belongs to his original heirs. The reasoning is that by adjudication, ownership transferred to the heirs, so when he returns and is defeated again, it is as if the heirs' ownership is confirmed. There is a difference of opinion within the school: some narrations consider it fay' in all cases, but the author prefers the view that it goes to the heirs. The passage also details that if the property is found before division, the heir takes it for free; after division, he must pay its value. Fungible goods (mithli) are an exception because there is no benefit in returning them. The commentary clarifies that the return need not be after adjudication; even if before, it is treated similarly by legal presumption.

Key Terms
فيء (fay'): Booty or property taken from enemies without war; here it refers to the apostate's property that becomes public treasury.
ظهر عليه (zahara 'alayh): To overcome or defeat; used in passive voice to mean 'was defeated'.
مثلي (mithli): Fungible goods that are measured or weighed and can be substituted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if an apostate's property was adjudged to his heirs and later the apostate returns and is defeated again with the same property, what is the ruling?
A) The property becomes fay' for the treasury.
B) The property goes to the apostate himself.
C) The property goes to his heirs.
D) The property is divided between the treasury and the heirs.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on an apostate's property distinguishes between immediate fay' and heirship upon return, based on the effect of judicial adjudication.

Kitaba by Heir and Return of Apostate
Ruling on kitaba by heir of apostate with return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a kitaba made by an heir of an apostate and identify to whom the wala' belongs upon the apostate's return as a Muslim, distinguishing based on whether the kitaba payment has been made.

Core Idea
A kitaba by the heir remains valid, but wala' and payments revert to the original owner if he returns Muslim before payment.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced scenario: an apostate's slave is assigned to his son, who then executes a kitaba with the slave. The apostate later returns as a Muslim. Does the kitaba stand? Who gets the wala'? The Hanafi school provides a precise answer based on the principle of deputyship and the priority of the original owner's right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and key parties
The apostate's slave is adjudged to the son (heir). The son makes a kitaba with the slave before the apostate's return as Muslim.
The apostate's ownership is suspended during apostasy, so the heir acts as deputy.
2
Determine the effect of the apostate's return
Upon return as Muslim, the apostate's original ownership is restored. The kitaba remains valid, but all rights (payment and wala') revert to the father.
The son's role as deputy ends, and the contract's benefits transfer to the principal.
3
Analyze the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The kitaba cannot be nullified because it was issued by a valid legal authority (the son as deputy). The analogy is made to a master who makes a kitaba and then the slave becomes insolvent: the first kitaba is rescinded but the second remains.
The deputy is treated as an agent; the rights of the contract revert to the principal.
4
Distinguish based on payment timing
If the mukatab paid the kitaba payment to the son before the father's return, the slave becomes free by the son and wala' remains with the son permanently, just as if the son had simply manumitted the slave.
The payment completes the emancipation, fixing the wala' with the son.
5
Contrast kitaba with tadbir
If the son had made the slave a mudabbar (postponed manumission) instead of kitaba, the wala' does not transfer to the father upon his return. Tadbir is a weaker form of manumission.
This distinction highlights the binding nature of kitaba compared to tadbir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن الحق وقضى بعبده لابنه فكاتبه فجاء مسلماً فالمكاتبة والولاء لمورثه) وهو المرتد الذي عاد مسلماً لأنه لا وجه إلى إبطال الكتابة لنفوذها بدليل منفذ وهو القضاء بلحاقه فجعلنا الوارث الذي هو خلفه كالوكيل من جهته وحقوق العقد فيه ترجع إلى الموكل والولاء لمن يقع العتق عنه نظيره المكاتب إذا كاتب عبده ثم عجز وفسخت الكتابة الأولى تبقى الثانية على حالها ويكون بدل الكتابة وولاؤه لمولاه، وليس انتقال الكتابة إلى المرتد الذي أسلم انتقال المكاتب من ملك الابن إليه وإنما هو لسقوط ولاية الخلف عند ظهور ولاية الأصل، وأشار بفاء التعقيب في قوله فجاء مسلماً إلى أن مجيئه عقيب كتابته يعني من غير أداء بدل الكتابة إلى الابن فلو أداها إليه ثم جاء مسلماً فإنه عتق على الابن حين أدى وكان الولاء له فلا ينتقل بعده إلى أبيه كما لو أعتق الابن عبده ثم جاء مسلماً والمكاتبة بدل الكتابة. وقيد بالكتابة لأن الابن إذا دبره ثم جاء الأب مسلماً فإن الولاء لا يكون للأب كما في التتارخانية. وأشار بكون البدل والولاء فقط للأب إلى أنه لا يمكن فسخ الكتابة لصدورها عن ولاية شرعية وقد صرح به الشارح وقدمنا عن الخانية أنه يملك إبطال كتابة الوارث قبل البدل إلا أن يقال إن مرادهم أنه لا يمكن فسخها بمجرد مجيئه من غير أن يفسخها، أما إذا فسخها انفسخت إلا أن جعلهم الوارث كالوكيل من جهته يأباه وقدمنا حكم ما إذا كاتب ثم ارتد ثم لحق . بسبب أداء. جميع
His saying: (And if he [the apostate] is adjudged [to have joined dar al-harb] and the judge assigns his slave to his son, then the son makes a kitaba [with the slave], then [the apostate] comes as Muslim, then the kitaba and the wala' belong to his [the apostate's] heir.) i.e., the apostate who returned as Muslim, because there is no way to nullify the kitaba due to its validity by a valid proof, namely the adjudication of his joining [dar al-harb]. So we made the heir, who is his deputy, like an agent from his side, and the rights of the contract revert to the principal, and the wala' belongs to the one for whom the emancipation takes place. Analogous to it is the case of a master who makes a kitaba with his slave, then [the slave] becomes insolvent, and the first kitaba is rescinded, the second one remains as it is, and the kitaba payment and wala' go to his master. And the transfer of the kitaba to the apostate who became Muslim is not a transfer of the mukatab from the ownership of the son to him, but rather due to the removal of the deputy's guardianship when the original's guardianship appears. And he indicated by the fa of consequence in his saying 'then he came Muslim' that his coming is immediately after his writing the kitaba, meaning without having paid the kitaba payment to the son. If he paid it to him and then came Muslim, then the slave becomes free by the son at the time of payment, and the wala' belongs to him, so it does not transfer to his father afterwards, just as if the son manumitted his slave and then the father came Muslim. And he restricted it to kitaba because if the son made the slave a mudabbar and then the father came Muslim, the wala' does not belong to the father, as in at-TatarKhaniyya. And he indicated that only the payment and wala' belong to the father, that the kitaba cannot be rescinded because it originated from a valid legal authority, and the commentator has stated it. And we have mentioned from al-Khaniyya that he has the power to nullify the heir's kitaba before payment, unless it is said that their meaning is that it cannot be rescinded by his mere coming without him rescinding it. If he rescinds it, it becomes rescinded, except that their making the heir like an agent from his side prevents that. And we have mentioned the ruling when he makes a kitaba and then apostatizes and then joins [dar al-harb].
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage deals with a complex scenario: an apostate's slave is adjudged to his son (as heir). The son then executes a kitaba (contract of manumission) with the slave. Later, the apostate returns as a Muslim. The ruling is that the kitaba remains valid but the rights (payment and wala') revert to the apostate, not the son. The reasoning is that the son was acting as a substitute for the father (since the father's ownership was suspended), and when the father returns Muslim, his original ownership is restored. However, the kitaba is not nullified because it was issued by a legally capable authority (the son as deputy). If the slave had already paid the kitaba payment to the son and become free before the father's return, then the wala' belongs to the son permanently. The passage distinguishes kitaba from tadbir (postponed manumission): in tadbir, the wala' does not transfer to the father. This demonstrates the Hanafi precision in contractual rights and the effect of restored ownership.

Key Terms
مكاتبة (mukatabah): A contract between a master and slave whereby the slave is granted freedom upon payment of a sum.
ولاء (wala'): The legal relationship of patronage between the emancipator and the freed slave, including inheritance rights.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose manumission is postponed until the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The wala' remains with the son permanently.
B) The wala' transfers to the apostate father.
C) The kitaba is invalidated and the slave remains a slave.
D) The wala' goes to the public treasury.

Key Takeaway
A kitaba by an heir remains valid upon the original owner's return, but rights revert to the original owner, reflecting the Hanafi principle of deputyship and the binding nature of the contract.
