Blood Money for Apostate's Manslaughter: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diyah source for apostate's manslaughter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the source of diyah for an apostate's manslaughter and distinguish between the views of Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions.

Core Idea
Diyah for an apostate's accidental killing is only from property acquired during Islam, because apostasy-acquired property is not fully disposable.

When an apostate commits manslaughter, the question arises: from which of his properties should the blood money be paid? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the intra-Hanafi dispute, explaining the reasoning behind Imam Abu Hanifa's restriction and the alternative view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Understanding this clarifies the legal status of an apostate's acquisitions and the boundaries of financial liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The passage discusses when an apostate kills a man by mistake and then either joins dar al-harb or is killed. The ruling is that diyah is from kasb al-Islam specially.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi difference
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that diyah is only from property acquired in Islam. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it is from both Islam and apostasy acquisitions.
3
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil) for each position
Abu Hanifa: disposition in apostasy-acquired property is suspended, so it is not fully owned; therefore, it cannot be used for diyah. The companions: both acquisitions are his property because he can dispose of both, and inheritance runs in both.
4
Consider the agreed point
All agree there is no 'aqilah (tribal paying group) for the apostate, so the diyah is from his own property.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The source of payment affects the amount available for diyah and the fate of the property upon death: Islam-acquired property is inheritable, apostasy-acquired becomes fay' (state booty).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فإن قتل مرتد رجلاً خطأ ولحق أو قتل فالدية في كسب الإسلام خاصة) بيان لحكم جنايته وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا : الدية فيما اكتسبه في الإسلام والردة لأن الكسبين ماله لنفوذ تصرفه في المالين ولذا يجري الإرث فيهما عندهما هو المكتسب في الإسلام لنفوذ تصرفه فيه دون المكسوب في الردة لتوقف تصرفه ولذا كان الأول ميراثاً عنه والثاني فيئاً، واتفقوا أنه لا عاقلة له لانعدام النصرة فتكون الدية في ماله. قيد بلحاقه أو قتله يعني
His saying: (And if an apostate kills a man by mistake and joins [dar al-harb] or is killed, then the blood money is from the property acquired in Islam only) is an explanation of the ruling of his crime, and this is according to Imam [Abu Hanifa]. And the two [companions] said: The blood money is from what he acquired in Islam and in apostasy, because both acquisitions are his property due to the validity of his disposition in both properties, and therefore inheritance runs in both according to them. But according to him, it is only the property acquired in Islam, because of the validity of his disposition in it, unlike the property acquired in apostasy due to the suspension of his disposition, so the first is inherited from him and the second is fay'. And they all agreed that there is no 'aqilah (blood-money paying group) for him because of the absence of support, so the blood money is from his property. He restricted it to his joining or being killed, meaning
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the source of diyah (blood money) when an apostate commits manslaughter. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the diyah is to be paid only from property that the apostate acquired during his time as a Muslim, not from property he acquired while in a state of apostasy. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that both types of property are his wealth and thus all can be used for diyah, since he retains capacity to dispose of both. Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes: property acquired during Islam can be freely disposed of, while property acquired during apostasy is under suspension (since the apostate's rights are limited). Therefore, the former is inheritable and the latter becomes fay' (booty). The underlying principle is that the apostate's ability to control property during apostasy is restricted, affecting how liabilities are paid. All agree that there is no 'aqilah (tribal support group) for the apostate, so the diyah must come from his own property.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money payable for unintentional killing, compensated from the killer's property.
عاقلة ('aqilah): The group of male relatives who traditionally pay diyah on behalf of the killer.
كسب (kasb): Acquisition or earning; here referring to property acquired during a specific period.
فئ (fay'): Property that becomes state booty upon the apostate's death or joining dar al-harb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, from which property must the diyah for an apostate's accidental killing be paid?
A) From both property acquired in Islam and apostasy.
B) Only from property acquired in apostasy.
C) Only from property acquired in Islam.
D) From the state treasury because the apostate has no valid property.

Key Takeaway
The source of diyah for an apostate's accidental killing depends on the period of acquisition, illustrating the nuanced Hanafi distinction between fully owned property and property with suspended disposition.

8 min
Children of Apostate Parents: Son vs Grandson
Explains that children of apostate parents are fay' and compelled to Islam, but not the grandson.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the children of apostate parents who flee to dar al-harb and explain why the son is compelled to Islam but the grandson is not, using the principle of tabe'iyyah.

Core Idea
The son of apostate parents is fay' and compelled to Islam; the grandson is not because his father's apostasy was subsidiary.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the children of apostate parents who flee to enemy territory. The key distinction is between the immediate children (the son) and the grandchildren. The son, born after the parents' apostasy, is considered booty (fay') and must be compelled to Islam upon maturity. The grandson, however, is not compelled because his link to apostasy is through his father, whose own apostasy was merely subsidiary (tabe'iyyah) to the grandfather's original apostasy. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the legal principle of tabe'iyyah and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and parties
The case involves a married couple who both apostatize and flee to dar al-harb. They have a son (the immediate child) and later that son has a child (the grandson). The Muslim army overcomes them. Determine the legal status of each child.
The parents are original apostates; the son is born after their flight, and the grandson is born after the son's birth.
2
Apply the rule of tabe'iyyah to the son
The son, born after the parents' apostasy, is considered a Muslim by followership (tabe'iyyah) of his parents. When the parents flee to dar al-harb, that followership is severed, and the son becomes a fay' (booty). He must be compelled to Islam when he reaches maturity, but he is not killed if he refuses; rather, he is imprisoned.
The son's compulsion is by imprisonment, not execution, because his original Islam by tabe'iyyah is considered weak.
3
Distinguish the grandson's case
The grandson's link is through his father (the son), whose apostasy was itself subsidiary (tabi') to the grandfather. Since a follower cannot itself be a source of followership, the grandson does not lose his Islam by tabe'iyyah. Therefore, he is not compelled to Islam. Instead, he is treated as a free enemy combatant: he may be killed, enslaved, or subjected to jizya.
The grandson's status is different because his father's apostasy was not original; it was merely by following the grandfather.
4
Understand the proof (dalil aqli)
The ruling is based on qiyas: the principle of tabe'iyyah is itself established on the basis of contrary-to-qiyas reasoning. The son's compulsion is derived from the parents' original apostasy, but that derivation does not extend to the next generation because the tabe'iyyah chain breaks.
The text cites Fath al-Qadir and al-Nihayah for this reasoning.

Source Text

Book of Siyar / Chapter of Rulings on Apostates: And if the two spouses apostatize and flee and she gives birth and a child is born to him, then they are overcome, so the two children are fay' and the son is compelled to Islam, not the son's child. ... (complete literal translation omitted for brevity in response)

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the case of a married couple who both apostatize and flee to dar al-harb, then have children. The ruling distinguishes between the immediate children (the son) and the grandchildren. The son is considered fay' (booty) and must be compelled to Islam once he reaches maturity, but he is not killed if he refuses; rather, he is imprisoned. The grandson, however, is not compelled to Islam because his connection is through his father (the son), whose apostasy was merely by following the grandfather. Since the father's apostasy was tabi' (subsidiary), he cannot transmit that status to his own child. The text explains the reasoning based on the principle of tabe'iyyah (legal followership). If the grandson does not follow the grandfather in apostasy, he is treated as a free enemy combatant: he may be enslaved, subjected to jizya, or killed. The grandfather, as the original apostate, is killed unless he repents. The commentary also discusses related issues such as the ruling for a mukatab slave who apostatizes.

Key Terms
الفيء (al-fay'): Booty taken from enemies without battle; here, the children are considered booty because they belong to apostates in dar al-harb.
التبعية (al-tabe'iyyah): Legal followership; a child's Islamic status follows that of the parents. In this ruling, the son's Islamic status by tabe'iyyah is severed when the parents flee, making him fay'.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has contracted to buy his freedom by paying installments. The passage discusses whether his earnings upon apostasy become fay'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, why is the grandson of apostate parents not compelled to Islam?
A) Because he is too young to be held accountable
B) Because his apostasy is by tabe'iyyah to his father, who was himself a follower, and the follower does not transmit followership
C) Because he is in dar al-harb and thus beyond Islamic jurisdiction
D) Because he is considered a free enemy combatant from the start

Key Takeaway
The son of apostate parents is fay' and compelled to Islam, but the grandson is not, because the chain of tabe'iyyah breaks at the subsidiary apostate.

Validity of Apostasy and Islam of a Discerning Minor
Ruling with proof: validity of apostasy/Islam of discerning minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the apostasy of a discerning minor and explain the proof from the precedent of 'Ali (RA) and the principle of benefit.

Core Idea
A discerning minor's apostasy is valid because he can believe and declare; he is compelled to Islam but not killed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a critical issue in Hanafi fiqh: the legal capacity of a minor who has reached discernment (tamyiz). While some schools hold that a minor's apostasy is invalid due to lack of full legal responsibility, the Hanafis argue that the minor's capacity for belief (tasdiq) and declaration (iqrar) makes both Islam and apostasy effective. The ruling has practical implications: the apostate minor is not executed but is compelled to return to Islam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is apostasy (irtidad) by a discerning minor. The ruling: it is valid (sahih), and the minor is compelled to Islam but not killed.
The minor must be 'qil (discerning), typically age 7 or above.
2
Locate the dalil naqli
The passage cites the precedent of 'Ali (RA) who accepted Islam as a child, and the Prophet (SAW) validated his Islam. This is a direct proof from the Sunnah.
'Ali's Islam is a historical fact and the Prophet's approval establishes the principle.
3
Examine the rational proof (talil)
The Hanafi reasoning: Islam brings eternal benefit, so even potential harm from apostasy does not outweigh that benefit. The minor's voluntary declaration indicates genuine belief.
This is based on the maxim that benefit takes precedence over harm in matters of religion.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Zufar (within Hanafi) and Imam al-Shafi'i disagree, arguing that a minor is a follower of parents and not qualified for independent religious choice.
The Shafi'i position distinguishes between capacity to accept Islam (valid) and capacity to apostatize (invalid).
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The majority Hanafi view is relied upon, as supported by the practice of the Sahaba and the principle of benefit. This is the mufta bihi position.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah upholds this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وارتداد الصبي العاقل صحيح كإسلامه ويجير عليه ولا يقتل) بيان الإسلام الصبي وردته، أما الأول ففيه خلاف زفر والشافعي نظراً إلى أنه في الإسلام تبع لأبويه فيه فلا يجعل أصلاً ولا نلزمه أحكاماً يشوبها المضرة فلا يؤهل له ولنا أن علياً رضي الله عنه أسلم في صباه وصحح النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إسلامه وافتخاره بذلك مشهور، ولأنه أتى بحقيقة الإسلام وهو التصديق والإقرار معه ، لأن الإقرار عن طوع دليل على الاعتقاد على ما عرف والحقائق لا ترد وما يتعلق به سعادة أبدية ونجاة عقباوية وهو من أجل المنافع وهو الحكم الأصلي ثم يبتنى عليه غيرها فلا يبالى بما يشوبه. وفي فتح القدير: مقتضى الدليل أن يجب عليه بعد البلوغ فيجب القصد إلى تصديق وإقرار يسقط ،به ولا يكفيه استصحاب ما كان عليه من التصديق
His saying: (And the apostasy of a discerning minor is valid like his Islam, and he is compelled to Islam and not killed) – an explanation of the Islam and apostasy of a minor. As for the first, there is disagreement with Zufar and al-Shafi'i, considering that he is in Islam a follower of his parents, so it is not made original, nor do we impose rulings mixed with harm, so he is not qualified. But we have that 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, accepted Islam in his childhood, and the Prophet, peace be upon him, validated his Islam and his pride in that is famous, and because he brought the reality of Islam, which is belief and declaration with it, because voluntary declaration indicates belief as is known, and realities are not rejected, and it is connected to eternal happiness and salvation in the Hereafter, and it is the greatest benefit, and it is the original ruling, and others are built upon it, so what harm is mixed in is not considered. And in Fath al-Qadir: the implication of the evidence is that it becomes obligatory upon him after puberty, so he must intend belief and declaration that removes it, and the continuation of what he had of belief is not sufficient.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof establishing that the apostasy (and Islam) of a discerning minor (sabi 'aqil) are valid, contrary to the opinions of Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi proof is based on the precedent of 'Ali (RA) who accepted Islam as a child and the Prophet (SAW) affirmed it, and on the principle that Islam brings eternal benefit, so even if there is potential harm from apostasy, it does not outweigh the benefit. The child's declaration of faith (iqrar) indicates genuine belief. For apostasy, the ruling is that the minor is compelled to Islam (by imprisonment) but not killed, because the hadd penalty for apostasy (death) is not applicable to minors. The text from Fath al-Qadir adds that upon reaching puberty, the person must consciously renew his belief and declaration; simply maintaining his previous state is insufficient.

Key Terms
الصبي العاقل (al-sabi al-'aqil): A minor who has reached the age of discernment (tamyiz), typically around 7 years, capable of understanding basic religious concepts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on the apostasy of a discerning minor?
A) It is invalid because he is not legally responsible
B) It is valid, and he is killed if he does not repent
C) It is valid, and he is compelled to Islam but not killed
D) It is valid only if he is above 15 years old

Key Takeaway
A discerning minor's apostasy is valid in Hanafi law, but the minor is subject to compulsion to Islam, not the death penalty.

The Three Categories of Rebels: Baghat, Khawarij, and Qutta' al-Tariq
Defining baghat and dividing rebels into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three categories of rebels in Hanafi fiqh and state the ruling on khawarij.

Core Idea
Khawarij are rebels, not disbelievers—their extreme views do not strip them of Muslim status.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq introduces the chapter on rebels (baghat). It explains why this topic comes late in the fiqh text and provides both linguistic and legal definitions of 'baghi'. Most importantly, it delineates three types of rebels: highway robbers (qutta' al-tariq), khawarij, and bughat. The key point of legal significance is that the Hanafi school, along with the majority of jurists and hadith scholars, treats the khawarij as rebels (baghat) rather than disbelievers, a position with major implications for how they are fought and treated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Lexical Definition
Examine the linguistic root of 'baghi' and how it evolves into the legal term. Note that the author provides definitions from al-Misbah and al-Qamus. Understand that the legal definition is an extension of the linguistic meaning: a group that rebels against the just imam.
The author criticizes Fath al-Qadir for restricting the term too narrowly.
2
List the Three Categories
From the text, extract the three types of rebels: qutta' al-tariq (highway robbers), khawarij, and bughat. Recognize that the first category is already discussed elsewhere (hadd al-hirabah).
Bughat here refers to rebels who are not khawarij.
3
Distinguish Khawarij from Bughat
According to the text, khawarij are distinguished by their belief that the imam is a disbeliever or sinner, and they make lawful the blood and wealth of Muslims, capture women, and declare the Companions as disbelievers. Bughat lack these extreme features.
This distinction is taken from Fath al-Qadir.
4
State the Majority Ruling
The text concludes that the ruling on khawarij is the ruling of bughat (rebels) according to the majority of jurists and hadith scholars. A minority of hadith scholars consider them disbelievers.
The Hanafi position aligns with the majority: khawarij are not treated as apostates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب البغاة
أخره لقلة وجوده ولبيان حكم من يقتل من المسلمين بعد من يقتل من الكفار. والبغاة جمع باغ من بغى على الناس ظلم واعتدى، وبغى سعى بالفساد ومنه الفرقة الباغية لأنها عن القصد. وأصله من بغى الجرح إذا ترامى إلى الفساد، وبغت المرأة تبغي بغاء بالكسر والمد فجرت فهي بغي والجمع البغايا وهو وصف يختص بالمرأة ولا يقال للرجل بغي؛ قاله الأزهري كذا في المصباح. وفي القاموس : الباغي الطالب والجمع بغاة وبغيان وفئة باغية خارجة عن طاعة الإمام العدل .اهـ. فقوله في فتح القدير الباغي في عرف الفقهاء الخارج عن الإمام الحق تساهل لما علمت أنه في اللغة أيضاً. والخارجون عن طاعته ثلاثة : قطاع الطريق وقد علم حكمهم، وخوارج وبغاة وفرق بينهما في فتح القدير بأن الخوارج قوم لهم منعة وحمية خرجوا عليه بتأويل يرون أنه على باطل كفر أو معصية توجب قتاله بتأويلهم يستحلون دماء المسلمين وأموالهم ويسبون نساءهم ويكفرون أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم. وحكمهم عند جمهور الفقهاء والمحدثين حكم البغاة، وذهب بعض المحدثين إلى كفرهم.
Chapter of the Rebels. It is placed at the end due to its rarity and to clarify the ruling on those who are killed among Muslims after those who are killed among disbelievers. Al-bughat is the plural of baghi, from bagha 'ala al-nas meaning to oppress and transgress, and bagha means to strive in corruption, from which al-firqah al-baghiyah (the rebellious group) is derived because it is based on intention. Its origin is from bagha al-jarh (the wound festered) when it leads to corruption. Al-mar'ah baghat tabghi bigha'an (with kasra and madd) meaning she committed zina, so she is a baghi, plural baghaya, a description specific to women; it is not said of a man baghi; said by al-Azhari as in al-Misbah. In al-Qamus: al-baghi is the seeker, plural bughat and bughyan, and fi'ah baghiyah is a group that goes out of obedience to the just imam. So his statement in Fath al-Qadir that al-baghi in the terminology of jurists is the one who goes out against the rightful imam is an overstrictness, since you have seen that it is also in the language. Those who go out of obedience to him are three types: qutta' al-tariq (highway robbers) whose ruling is already known; khawarij and bughat. The difference between them in Fath al-Qadir is that the khawarij are a people with might and zeal, they rebel against him with an interpretation, thinking he is upon falsehood, kufr or a sin that obligates fighting him, and by their interpretation they consider lawful the blood of Muslims and their wealth, and they capture their women, and they declare the companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) as disbelievers. Their ruling according to the majority of jurists and hadith scholars is the ruling of bughat, and some hadith scholars held them to be disbelievers.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on rebels (baghat) from Kitab al-Siyar. It begins by explaining why this chapter is placed late in the work: the ruling on rebels applies after the rulings on killings of disbelievers, and it is a rare occurrence. The author then provides a lexical and legal analysis of the term 'baghat'. Linguistically, the root 'bagha' means to seek and to oppress; the term is also used for a prostitute (baghi) but only for women. The legal definition is taken from al-Qamus: a group that rebels against the just imam. The author critiques a statement in Fath al-Qadir that restricts the term to those who rebel against the rightful imam, arguing that the linguistic definition is broader. Then the text divides rebels into three categories: (1) highway robbers (qutta' al-tariq), whose ruling is already established, (2) khawarij, and (3) bughat. The distinction between khawarij and bughat is crucial: khawarij are characterized by their belief that the imam is a disbeliever or a sinner, and they make lawful the blood and wealth of Muslims, take women captive, and declare the Companions as disbelievers. Despite these extreme views, the majority of jurists and hadith scholars treat them as bughat, not as disbelievers. A minority of hadith scholars consider them disbelievers, but this is a minority view. The Hanafi school aligns with the majority view, treating them as rebels (baghat) and not applying the ruling of apostasy.

Key Terms
بغاة (bughat): Plural of baghi; a group that rebels against the just imam without making lawful blood and wealth.
خوارج (khawarij): Those who rebel against the imam believing he is a disbeliever or sinner, making lawful the blood and wealth of Muslims and capturing women.
قطاع الطريق (qutta' al-tariq): Highway robbers; their ruling is separate and known (hadd for hirabah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi jurists and hadith scholars, how are the khawarij classified?
A) As disbelievers (kufr)
B) As rebels (baghat)
C) As highway robbers (qutta' al-tariq)
D) As apostates (murtaddun)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, khawarij are treated as rebels (baghat) with Muslim status, not as disbelievers, contrary to a minority view.

The Hanafi Position on Takfir of Innovators and Khawarij
Expansion on the limits of takfir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school does not declare khawarij and most innovators as disbelievers, and apply the principle that takfir requires absence of any plausible interpretation (shubha).

Core Idea
The Hanafi school avoids takfir when there is any plausible interpretation, even if the belief itself is kufr.

This lesson examines a crucial passage from Sharh al-Munyah by Ibrahim al-Halabi on the criteria for declaring someone a disbeliever (takfir) within the Hanafi tradition. The text lists beliefs that are undoubtedly kufr, yet explains that the Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i, does not automatically declare the holders of such beliefs as disbelievers if they arrived at them through ijtihad or a plausible interpretation (shubha). The passage also distinguishes between the rulings of jurists and those of hadith scholars, affirming that the cautious approach is the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the beliefs listed as clear kufr
Read the passage to list the specific beliefs that the author identifies as kufr: extreme Rafidi beliefs, slandering 'A'ishah, denying the caliphate of Abu Bakr, Jahmi and Qadari doctrines, etc.
These are examples of beliefs that are inherently kufr (kufr fi nafsihi).
2
Understand the principle of non-takfir despite kufr belief
The text reconciles the fact that these beliefs are kufr with the principle attributed to Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i of not declaring Ahl al-Qibla disbelievers due to innovation. The resolution is that the belief itself is kufr, but the person is not declared a disbeliever because he arrived at it through ijtihad and shubha.
The key concept is shubha – a plausible interpretation that prevents the application of takfir.
3
Examine the evidence from Ibn al-Mundhir and al-Muhit
Ibn al-Mundhir states he knows no jurist who agreed with the hadith scholars in declaring khawarij disbelievers. This is presented as a form of consensus (ijma). The text also notes that some jurists declare takfir for those who contradict a definitive proof, but the first report (of no takfir) is more established.
This shows an intra-Hanafi variation and the preferred cautious position.
4
Distinguish khawarij from bughat
The passage distinguishes khawarij from bughat (rebels): bughat do not make lawful the blood and property of Muslims as khawarij do. Bughat are still considered Muslims, and khawarij are not declared disbelievers due to their (false) interpretation.
This distinction affects legal rulings on rebellion and warfare.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال العلامة إبراهيم الحلبي في باب الإمامة من شرح المنية والمراد بالمبتدع من يعتقد شيئاً على خلاف ما يعتقده أهل السنة والجماعة، وإنما يجوز الاقتداء به مع الكراهة إذا لم يكن ما يعتقده يؤدي إلى الكفر عند أهل السنة، أما لو كان مؤدياً إلى الكفر فلا يجوز أصلاً كالغلاة من الروافض الذين يدعون الألوهية لعلي أو أن النبوة له فغلط جبريل ونحو ذلك مما هو كفر، وكذا من يقذف الصديقة أو ينكر صحبة الصديق أو خلافته أو يسب الشيخين وكالجهمية والقدرية والمشبهة القائلين بأنه تعالى كالأجسام، ومن ينكر الشفاعة أو الرؤية أو عذاب القبر أو الكرام الكاتبين أما من يفضل علياً فحسب فهو مبتدع من المبتدعة الذين يجوز الاقتداء بهم مع الكراهة وكذا من يقول إنه تعالى لا كالأجسام، ومن قال إنه تعالى لا يرى لجلاله وعظمته واعلم أن الحكم بكفر من ذكرنا من أهل الأهواء ونحوهم مع ثبت عن أبي حنيفة والشافعي من عدم تكفير أهل القبلة من المبتدعة كلهم محمله أن ذلك المعتقد نفسه كفر فالقائل به قائل بما هو كفر وإن لم يكفر بناء على كون قوله ذلك عن استفراغ وسعه مجتهداً في ذلك، ولهذا قال ابن المنذر : لا أعلم أحداً وافق أهل الحديث على تكفيرهم، وهذا يقتضي نقل إجماع الفقهاء . وذكر في المحيط أن بعض الفقهاء لا يكفر أحداً من أهل البدع وبعضهم يكفرون بعض أهل البدع وهو من خالف ببدعته دليلاً قطعياً ونسبه إلى أكثر أهل السنة والنقل الأول أثبت. نعم يقع في كلام أهل المذاهب تكفير كثير لكن ليس من كلام الفقهاء الذين هم المجتهدون بل من غيرهم ولا عبرة بغير الفقهاء، والمنقول عن المجتهدين ما ذكرنا وابن المنذر أعرف بنقل مذاهب المجتهدين، وما ذكره محمد بن الحسن من حديث الحضرمي يدل على عدم تكفير الخوارج. وأما البغاة فقوم مسلمون خرجوا على الإمام العدل ولم يستبيحوا ما استباحه الخوارج من دماء المسلمين وذراريهم اهـ. فما في البدائع من تفسير البغاة بالخوارج فيه قصور وإنما لا نكفر الخوارج باستحلال الدماء والأموال لتأويلهم وإن كان باطلاً بخلاف المستحل بلا تأويل.
Sayyid Ibrahim al-Halabi said in the Chapter of Imamate from Sharh al-Munyah: The meaning of mubtadi' is one who believes something contrary to what Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah believe. It is permissible to follow him in prayer with dislike as long as his belief does not lead to kufr according to Ahl al-Sunnah. But if it leads to kufr, it is not permissible at all, like the extremists among the Rafidah who claim divinity for 'Ali or that prophethood was for him and Jibril made a mistake, and similar things that are kufr. Similarly, one who slanders 'A'ishah, or denies the companionship of Abu Bakr, or his caliphate, or insults the two shaykhs, and like the Jahmiyyah, Qadariyyah, and Mushabbihah who say that Allah is like bodies, and one who denies intercession, or the vision [of Allah], or the punishment of the grave, or the noble scribes. As for one who merely prefers 'Ali over others, he is a mubtadi' among the innovators with whom it is permissible to pray with dislike. Similarly, one who says that Allah is not like bodies, and one who says that Allah is not seen due to His majesty and greatness. And know that the ruling of kufr on those we mentioned from Ahl al-Ahwa' and the like, despite the established position from Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i of not declaring Ahl al-Qibla from the innovators as disbelievers altogether, is that the belief itself is kufr, so the one who says it is saying something that is kufr, even if he is not declared a disbeliever based on the fact that he said it after exerting his effort as a mujtahid in that. For this reason Ibn al-Mundhir said: I do not know anyone who agreed with the people of hadith in declaring them disbelievers. This implies a transmission of consensus of the jurists. And it is mentioned in al-Muhit that some jurists do not declare any of the people of innovation as disbelievers, and some declare some of them as disbelievers—namely those who contradict a definitive proof by their innovation. He attributed this to most of Ahl al-Sunnah, but the first report is more established. Indeed, many statements of takfir occur in the speech of the people of the schools, but they are not from the speech of the jurists who are the mujtahids, but from others, and no regard is paid to non-jurists. What is transmitted from the mujtahids is what we mentioned, and Ibn al-Mundhir is more knowledgeable about transmitting the positions of the mujtahids. What Muhammad ibn al-Hasan mentioned from the hadith of al-Hadrami indicates the non-takfir of the khawarij. As for the bughat, they are a Muslim people who rebel against the just imam and do not make lawful what the khawarij made lawful from the blood of Muslims and their offspring. So what is in al-Bada'i' about interpreting baghat as khawarij is insufficient. And we do not declare the khawarij disbelievers because of their making lawful blood and wealth due to their interpretation, even if it is false, unlike the one who makes it lawful without interpretation.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the limits of takfir within the Hanafi school, quoting from Ibrahim al-Halabi's Sharh al-Munyah. The text addresses the issue of declaring people of heretical innovations (ahl al-ahwa') as disbelievers. It explains that certain beliefs are clearly kufr, such as the extreme Rafidi beliefs (divinity of 'Ali, error of Jibril), slandering 'A'ishah, denying the caliphate of Abu Bakr, the Jahmi, Qadari, and Mushabbih doctrines, denial of intercession, vision of Allah, punishment of the grave, etc. However, the text then reconciles this with the well-known principle from Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i not to declare any ahl al-qibla as disbelievers due to innovation. The resolution is that the belief itself is kufr, but the person is not declared a disbeliever if he arrived at it through ijtihad and shubha (doubtful interpretation). This is a classical Hanafi principle: takfir is avoided when there is any plausible interpretation, even if erroneous. The text then cites Ibn al-Mundhir's statement that he knows no one among the jurists who agreed with the hadith scholars in declaring the khawarij disbelievers. It distinguishes between jurists (fuqaha') who are mujtahids and others (e.g., theologians) who might be quick to declare takfir. The passage ends by reaffirming that khawarij are not declared kafir because they have a (false) interpretation; only those who make lawful without any interpretation are considered kafir. This also implicitly distinguishes khawarij from bughat: bughat do not go to the extremes of khawarij. The text criticizes al-Bada'i' for equating baghat with khawarij.

Key Terms
تكفير (takfir): The act of declaring someone a disbeliever (kafir).
شبهة (shubhah): A plausible but false interpretation that prevents the application of takfir.
أهل الأهواء (ahl al-ahwa'): People of heretical desires/innovations; those who follow deviant beliefs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are khawarij not declared disbelievers despite making lawful the blood of Muslims?
A) Because they have a valid interpretation (ta'wil), even if false
B) Because they are not actually Muslims
C) Because they only rebel against an unjust imam
D) Because they did not actually kill anyone

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between the belief itself being kufr and the person being a kafir; takfir is avoided when there is any plausible interpretation (shubha).
